Please assess this conversation and critique and advise "So I am trying to understand kind of the geopolitical rationale for the United States for regime change in Iran. Be concise, just like two or three paragraphs max.

At a high level, the U.S. rationale (when regime change is even discussed) comes from security + regional power balance. The core concern is that Iran is one of the few states in the Middle East that both opposes the U.S. order and has the capacity to challenge it—through its nuclear program, ballistic missiles, and network of proxy groups (e.g., Hezbollah, Hamas, Houthis). A different regime is seen as a way—at least in theory—to eliminate or reduce those threats more permanently than sanctions or limited strikes.

Second, there’s a regional/geopolitical logic: Iran is a central node in an anti-U.S./anti-Israel bloc, so weakening or replacing its government could shift the balance of power across the Middle East (benefiting U.S. allies and stabilizing energy and security dynamics). On top of that, U.S. policymakers often frame it partly in ideological terms—arguing that a more democratic or less repressive Iranian government would align better with international norms and reduce conflict.

That said, it’s important: official U.S. policy is often not explicitly regime change, even when actions (sanctions, military strikes) weaken the regime. Publicly, goals are usually narrower—like stopping nuclear weapons or limiting military capabilities—because outright regime change is risky, costly, and historically has had mixed results.

Like, it's all kind of vague, though. Like, yeah, I just... What does the United States, what outcomes does the United States want that a regime change in Iran would help produce that?

If you strip away the vague rhetoric, the U.S. is basically trying to achieve a few very concrete outcomes—and regime change is seen (by some) as a way to lock them in permanently rather than manage them temporarily.

1. Remove long-term military threats at the source.
Right now, the U.S. spends huge effort containing Iran’s nuclear program, missiles, and proxy networks. A different regime—especially one aligned or at least non-hostile—would ideally mean: no nuclear weapons pursuit, fewer missiles, and no support for groups targeting U.S. forces or allies. In other words, instead of constantly “policing” Iran, you eliminate the need to.

2. Flip a major regional adversary into a neutral or allied state.
Iran is one of the main pillars opposing U.S. influence in the Middle East. If that government were replaced with something more cooperative, you’d likely get: less conflict with Israel, weaker anti-U.S. blocs, and more stable regional order. Some policymakers explicitly think a different Iran could even become a partner rather than a rival.

3. Reduce ongoing costs and uncertainty.
Right now, the U.S. is stuck in a loop: sanctions, proxy conflicts, military deterrence, crisis management. A regime change is (in theory) a way to “end the game” rather than keep playing it—i.e., fewer future wars, less need for military presence, and less risk of sudden escalation in places like the Persian Gulf.

So the cleanest way to think about it: the U.S. isn’t just trying to weaken Iran—it’s trying (in some scenarios) to change Iran into a country that doesn’t generate these problems at all.

Okay, but, okay, so the first one I get is there's either a dollar advantage to not having to police them or kind of like an opportunity cost to where we can pivot to China or something. For two, I don't understand what the benefit of weaker anti-US blocks or a more stable regional order are, or less conflict with Israel, even. And three is, again, I mean, just vague still.

Good push—if you make it concrete, the benefits are basically about money, risk, and leverage, not abstract “stability.”

On (2): weaker anti-U.S. blocs / less Israel conflict → what’s the payoff?

    Lower chance of a large war that drags the U.S. in. If Iran isn’t backing armed groups or clashing with Israel, you reduce scenarios where the U.S. is forced into a costly regional war (think carrier groups, air campaigns, etc.). That’s a direct fiscal + human-risk benefit.

    More predictable energy flows → lower global price shocks. The Persian Gulf is still critical to oil supply. Tensions involving Iran (straits, proxies, Israel escalation) can spike prices, which hits U.S. inflation and global growth. Less conflict = fewer shocks.

    More freedom to reallocate military and diplomatic bandwidth. If the Middle East is quieter, the U.S. can shift assets (ships, intelligence, attention) toward higher-priority arenas like Asia. This is the “opportunity cost” you mentioned, but applied specifically to regional instability.

On (3): reducing ongoing costs and uncertainty → what that actually means

    The U.S. currently pays a constant “insurance premium”: sanctions enforcement, naval patrols, regional bases, missile defense for allies, crisis response. A non-hostile Iran could shrink that baseline spend.

    It also reduces tail risk—low-probability but extremely expensive events (major war, nuclear breakout, closure of the Strait of Hormuz). Even if those don’t happen often, they dominate strategic planning because of how costly they’d be.

    Finally, it improves bargaining power globally: fewer crises tying down U.S. attention means more leverage in negotiations elsewhere (trade, alliances, China competition).

So stripped down: it’s not “stability is nice”—it’s fewer forced interventions, fewer oil shocks, and less capital (military + economic + political) tied up in managing one adversary.

Okay, and what change, like, I'm trying to understand from a geopolitical level, the gaps in the world as it is and how the United States wants it to be, and then to tie this explicitly to regime change in Iran.

The cleanest way to see this is: the U.S. has a target “shape” for the international system, and Iran is one of the biggest mismatches.

What the U.S. wants (ideal world):

    Key regions (like the Middle East) don’t generate shocks—no major wars, no oil disruptions, no nuclear proliferation.

    Most significant countries are either aligned with the U.S. or at least not actively hostile.

    Security is “outsourced” locally (allies handle their own defense), so the U.S. doesn’t need constant intervention.

What exists instead (current gap):

    Iran is actively building capabilities the U.S. wants to suppress—nuclear potential, missiles, and proxy networks.

    It anchors an opposing bloc (“axis of resistance”) that creates recurring instability and risk of escalation across the region.

    The result is a system where the U.S. has to constantly intervene, deter, sanction, and manage crises.

Where regime change fits explicitly:
Regime change is basically the “hard reset” solution to that gap. Instead of continuously degrading Iran’s capabilities (bombing missiles, sanctioning, countering proxies), you try to change the underlying actor that keeps regenerating those problems. In the best-case U.S. scenario, a different Iranian government would: (1) stop pursuing nuclear weapons and missiles, (2) stop funding proxies, and (3) behave like a normal state that doesn’t force constant U.S. involvement.

So the tight framing is: the gap is “persistent adversary that generates instability,” and regime change is an attempt to eliminate the source of that persistence—not just manage its symptoms.

The key is that Iran’s proxy network isn’t mainly about “occasional terrorism” in the car-crash sense—it’s about strategic leverage and escalation control. Groups like Hezbollah or the Houthis give Iran the ability to pressure Israel, Gulf states, and U.S. forces without triggering full-scale war. That matters because they can: (1) threaten shipping lanes (raising global oil prices), (2) pin down U.S. military resources, and (3) create constant low-level conflict that can suddenly spike into a regional war. From a U.S. perspective, the issue isn’t the raw death toll—it’s that these networks increase the probability of very expensive, system-level events (major war, oil shock, nuclear escalation). That’s why they get disproportionate attention relative to everyday risks.

On the “axis of resistance,” their desired world is basically the mirror image of the U.S. one: reduce or expel U.S. influence from the Middle East, constrain Israel, and build a regional order where Iran is a central power. Proxies are how they pursue that under asymmetry—they can’t match the U.S. or Israel directly, so they build a distributed network to gradually shift the balance.

Neoconservatives (e.g., thinkers around the Project for the New American Century) take a more assertive view: they see regimes like Iran not just as manageable adversaries but as structural threats to a U.S.-led order. So instead of containment, they favor transforming the system itself—including regime change—on the belief that U.S. security and global stability are maximized when hostile regimes are replaced with ones aligned with liberal democracy and U.S. interests.

You’re right to push—“expel the U.S.” or “constrain Israel” are means, not ends. The underlying end state for Iran is basically regime security + regional autonomy + economic survivability under its own terms.

What problem Iran is trying to solve (their “gap”):

    They see a world where the U.S. can sanction, isolate, or overthrow regimes it dislikes (which Iran experienced historically).

    U.S. alliances (Israel + Gulf states) create a military ring that can threaten them directly.

    The global system (dollar dominance, sanctions networks) lets the U.S. strangle their economy at will.

So the end state they want is: a region where no external power can coerce or topple them, and where they have enough influence to deter threats and keep trade flowing despite sanctions.

How their strategies map cleanly to that end state:

    Expelling/reducing U.S. influence → removes the only actor capable of regime change at scale.

    Constraining Israel → reduces the most immediate, capable regional military threat (especially strikes on nuclear sites).

    Proxy networks → create deterrence in depth: if Iran is attacked, conflict spreads everywhere (Lebanon, Iraq, Yemen), raising the cost so high that adversaries hesitate.

    Becoming a regional power → gives them bargaining leverage: stronger position in oil markets, trade corridors, and diplomacy, which helps them circumvent sanctions and stabilize their economy.

So stripped down: it’s not pride—it’s survival + insulation from coercion + economic breathing room. Their whole model is about making the cost of pressuring Iran so high, and their influence so embedded, that no one can force them into a weaker position.

Okay, I guess you could say regime security is a little less vague, but regional autonomy, I mean, again, it could just be a jingoistic thing, but I feel like it's regional autonomy to do something, or the security of the regime to do something, or it's like under its own terms, it's like, what are the terms it wants, like, it wants, again, this, we seem to be focusing on means, not ends.

Good instinct—so push one layer deeper: the ends aren’t “autonomy” or “security” in the abstract, they’re concrete payoffs the regime cares about staying in power to achieve.

For Iran’s leadership, the core ends are:

    Stay in power without being forced to liberalize or collapse. That means avoiding the fate of regimes that got overthrown after external pressure (Iraq, Libya).

    Maintain an ideological model (Islamic Republic: clerical authority, resistance to Western political norms). That’s not just pride—it’s the internal legitimacy system that keeps elites and supporters aligned.

    Generate enough economic stability to survive sanctions without conceding control. Not maximizing GDP—just enough oil sales, trade routes, and partners (China, regional networks) to keep the state functioning and elites paid.

Now the strategies you mentioned map to those specific ends:

    Reducing U.S. influence → prevents external coercion that could force political change.

    Constraining Israel → removes the most credible military threat to critical assets (like nuclear infrastructure) that underpin deterrence.

    Proxies and regional power → create leverage so Iran can trade (oil, sanctions relief, diplomacy) from a position where others can’t just dictate terms.

So the clean framing is: the end is not “regional autonomy”—it’s preserving a specific regime + ideology + acceptable economic baseline without being forced to change. Everything else is just machinery to make that durable.

This seems a little cynical or Western-centric in my mind to think that they're not trying to get rich or have, like, global ambitions, that they're just trying to survive and pay off their elites without collapsing.

That’s a fair critique—“just survival” can sound reductive. A better model is that Iran has layered goals, not just one. Survival is the constraint (they won’t pursue anything that risks regime collapse), but within that constraint they do have expansionary and material ambitions.

Those broader ends include:

    Economic upside: more stable oil exports, sanctions relief or evasion, access to trade corridors (e.g., linking to Asia), and generally a bigger share of regional economic activity. They’re not trying to maximize wealth like a liberal economy would, but they do want more resources and less vulnerability.

    Status and influence: being recognized as a major regional power that others have to consult or accommodate (like Turkey or Saudi Arabia). That brings real benefits—better deals, deterrence, and diplomatic leverage.

    Shaping the regional order: pushing toward a Middle East where external powers can’t dominate outcomes and where governments are more tolerant of Iran’s model and alliances.

So it’s not just cynical survival, and it’s not purely ideological either—it’s closer to a realist mix: maximize power, wealth, and influence subject to the hard constraint that the regime and its governing model must remain intact.

You’re not crazy—there is an ideological layer, but “Islamic world order” overstates both intent and feasibility. Iran’s leadership (especially around Ruhollah Khomeini) did articulate a vision of exporting an Islamic revolutionary model. But in practice, that ambition has been selective and constrained, not a global blueprint they’re realistically trying to impose everywhere.

A tighter way to state their actual end goals is:

    Lock in regime survival + ideological continuity at home (Islamic Republic stays intact).

    Be the leading pole of influence in parts of the Muslim world, especially where they have natural footholds (Iraq, لبنان via Hezbollah, etc.).

    Shift the regional order so it’s not dominated by the U.S./Israel, and instead one where Iran is a co-equal or veto player.

The reason it feels vague is that they’re not pursuing a clean “end-state” like a world order diagram. It’s more incremental: expand influence where possible, block threats where necessary, and opportunistically grow power without triggering a coalition that could crush them. So ideology matters—but it’s filtered through real constraints, which keeps their behavior looking more like pragmatic power accumulation than a full-on attempt to remake the entire world.

Good—now we can make it non-vague.

End state (what Iran ultimately wants):
A world—at least regionally—where no external power can threaten or coerce the regime, and where Iran has enough influence that other states must account for its preferences when making decisions. Not global domination, but something like: you don’t get to set Middle East outcomes without Iran’s buy-in.

So “its interests must be accommodated” means very concrete things:

    Security red lines are respected: no attacks on its territory, no attempts at regime change, no strikes on core assets (like nuclear infrastructure).

    Economic baseline is guaranteed: Iran can reliably sell oil, access trade, and move money without being strangled by sanctions.

    Sphere of influence is tolerated: Iraq, parts of Syria/Lebanon, etc., are not turned into hostile platforms against Iran.

    Deterrence is recognized: if Iran signals “this escalation crosses a line,” others believe it and back off.

The gap (current world vs desired):
Right now, the U.S. can sanction them heavily, Israel can strike targets, regional rivals can align against them, and Iran’s influence is contested. So they lack secure sovereignty + guaranteed economic access + uncontested regional leverage.

Strategy (how they close it):
Everything—proxies, missiles, nuclear latency, diplomacy—is about forcing others to internalize those interests. The goal is to reach a point where, before acting, the U.S., Israel, or Gulf states implicitly ask: “Will this trigger unacceptable costs from Iran?” Once that happens consistently, Iran’s interests are effectively “accommodated.”

You’re right to be frustrated—that framing is too abstract. Let’s make it concrete, like “what does this actor actually want to get out of the world.”

A sharper version of Iran’s end goals is something like:

    A durable, sanction-resistant economy (sell oil freely or through workarounds, access global trade/finance enough to sustain growth and fund the state).

    A recognized regional power position where Iraq, Syria, Lebanon, and Gulf security arrangements can’t be structured against them.

    A credible deterrent umbrella (nuclear latency or similar) so no one can realistically invade or overthrow them.

    Preservation and spread of their political model where feasible (not global domination, but ensuring allied or at least non-hostile governments in key مناطق).

Now the gap becomes concrete: today they’re heavily sanctioned, partially economically isolated, militarily vulnerable to high-end actors (especially Israel/U.S.), and their regional influence is contested and costly to maintain.

And their strategy is basically a workaround for those constraints:

    Proxies → cheaper way to shape عراق/سوريا/لبنان and pressure adversaries

    Missiles + nuclear program → create deterrence without needing a conventional superpower military

    Sanctions evasion + China ties → keep the economy alive without conceding politically

    Anti-U.S. positioning → weaken the coalition that enforces those constraints

So instead of “they want autonomy,” the concrete version is: they want to be a hard-to-coerce, economically viable regional power with enough deterrence and influence that no one can box them in again.

Okay, now give me the U.S. version.

Good—apply the same concrete lens.

U.S. end goals (in this context):

    No hostile power can dominate or destabilize the Middle East, especially in ways that drag the U.S. into wars or disrupt the global economy.

    Open, reliable energy and trade flows (no one can choke the Strait of Hormuz or spike oil prices at will).

    Allies (Israel, Gulf states) remain secure and aligned, so the U.S. doesn’t have to constantly intervene directly.

    No nuclear proliferation cascade (if Iran gets nukes, others likely follow, which multiplies risk and reduces U.S. leverage globally).

The gap (current world vs desired):
Iran is a persistent spoiler: it can threaten shipping, support armed groups that destabilize allies, push toward nuclear capability, and impose ongoing costs (military deployments, crisis management, sanctions enforcement). So the U.S. is stuck spending resources to manage Iran rather than having a region that mostly runs itself.

Strategy (including where regime change fits):

    Baseline approach: contain and constrain (sanctions, deterrence, alliances, occasional strikes) so Iran can’t close that gap on its terms.

    Stronger version (where regime change comes in): instead of endlessly managing a hostile actor, change Iran into a state that doesn’t generate these problems—i.e., one that trades normally, doesn’t arm proxies, doesn’t pursue nukes, and doesn’t force constant U.S. involvement.

So the tight version: the U.S. wants a Middle East that is low-cost, non-escalatory, and doesn’t require constant intervention—and Iran, as currently structured, is the main thing preventing that.

Okay, so you don't think the U.S. cares about democracy or human rights or Judeo-Christian, you know, ethics or, you know, non-authoritarianism, or, you know, there's just other value systems here.

Those values do matter—but they operate at a different level than the hard geopolitical goals you’ve been asking about.

At the core (decision-driving level), U.S. policy is mostly about security, stability, and cost: avoid major wars, keep key regions from destabilizing, protect allies, prevent nuclear spread. That’s why the U.S. often works with non-democratic partners when it serves those goals. If democracy or human rights conflict with core security interests, they’re usually secondary.

At a second layer, values like democracy and human rights still play real roles:

    They shape which outcomes are seen as “ideal” (a democratic Iran would be preferred over an equally stable authoritarian one).

    They influence domestic politics and legitimacy—leaders need to justify actions to the public and allies.

    They sometimes affect strategy selection (e.g., sanctions framed around human rights, support for civil society).

So it’s not that the U.S. doesn’t care about those values—it’s that, in practice, they’re constraints and preferences layered on top of a more hard-nosed geopolitical objective function, not the primary driver in cases like Iran.

Okay, can we get a tier list of U.S. values?

Yeah—this is a good way to make it concrete. Think of it less like official doctrine and more like revealed priorities from actual behavior:
Tier 1 (Hard constraints — rarely sacrificed)

    Physical security of the U.S. homeland (no attacks, no peer threats nearby)

    Avoidance of major-power war (especially nuclear escalation)

    Core global system stability (no collapse of trade, finance, energy flows)

Tier 2 (Very strong, but tradeable at margins)

    Prevent nuclear proliferation (especially in volatile regions)

    Protect key allies (Israel, NATO, Japan, etc.)

    Maintain global economic access (shipping lanes, dollar system, markets)

Tier 3 (Important strategic preferences)

    Limit hostile regional powers (like Iran acting as a destabilizing force)

    Reduce need for costly interventions (fewer Iraq/Afghanistan-type entanglements)

    Maintain global leadership/credibility (alliances trust U.S. commitments)

Tier 4 (Values / ideological goals)

    Democracy promotion

    Human rights

    Rule of law / liberal norms

These matter, but you’ll see them consistently overridden if they conflict with Tier 1–2 goals.
Tier 5 (Narrative / domestic legitimation layer)

    Moral framing of actions (“freedom,” “rules-based order”)

    Public opinion alignment

This isn’t fake—it shapes policy—but it’s the most flexible layer.

So in your Iran case: regime change gets traction when people think it helps Tier 1–2 goals (security, nukes, stability). The democracy/human rights argument is usually supporting logic, not the core driver.

Got it—you meant “now I ran (i.e., I run) the country.”

If you were running U.S. policy with that tier list in mind, the question becomes: what strategy toward Iran best improves Tier 1–2 outcomes at lowest risk/cost? And that’s exactly where the debate splits:

    Containment camp (dominant in practice): keep Iran boxed in (sanctions, deterrence, alliances) because regime change is too risky—it could trigger war, collapse the state, or create something worse. This is the “minimize downside” approach.

    Regime change camp (more aggressive): argue that containment is a permanent cost trap, and only changing the regime solves the problem at the root—but it carries high uncertainty and historical baggage (Iraq, Libya).

If you’re “running it,” the real decision isn’t values—it’s a risk tradeoff:

    Is the long-term cost of managing Iran higher or lower than the risk of trying to fundamentally change it?

That’s the actual optimization problem

Here’s the same “revealed priorities” tier list for China, using the same lens:

        especially around Taiwan and near seas

👉 These are what keep Tier 1 durable over time.

    Regional dominance in Asia (especially East Asia)

    Global influence proportional to economic size

        Belt and Road, institutions, standards-setting

    Leverage over key supply chains and infrastructure

👉 This is China trying to reshape the environment in its favor.

    Validation of the “China model” (authoritarian capitalism)

    Reducing Western/liberal dominance in global norms

    Expanding a more sovereignty-first international order

👉 Important, but usually subordinate to stability and growth.
Tier 5 (Narrative / identity layer)

    “National rejuvenation” / overcoming historical humiliation

    Anti-containment / anti-hegemony rhetoric

    Civilizational framing of China’s rise

The key difference vs U.S. and Iran

    U.S. Tier 1 = global system stability

    Iran Tier 1 = regime survival under pressure

    China Tier 1 = regime survival + national unity (especially Taiwan)

China is less about disruption (like Iran) and less about global policing (like the U.S.), and more about:

    becoming a secure, technologically advanced superpower that can’t be constrained and eventually sets the terms in its region (and partially globally).

Ultra-compressed

    China wants to lock in CCP rule, achieve great-power status, and reduce dependence on—and vulnerability to—U.S.-dominated systems, while steadily expanding its influence.


Okay, it seems like there's a real symmetry, and I like how you gave the, like, tier two, this is what keeps tier one durable over time, like there's a dependency linkage between these. But is this like a standard framework for thinking about, you know, goals or strategy in some way? What you’re noticing is real, and it maps pretty closely to standard ways people think in international relations—just not usually in this exact “tier list” format.

The underlying structure shows up in a few frameworks:

    Realism / neorealism (e.g., Kenneth Waltz): states prioritize survival first, then power and security, then everything else. That’s basically your Tier 1 → Tier 2 logic.

    Grand strategy / ends–ways–means (military planning): define ends (what you want), ways (how you pursue it), means (resources). Your tiers are like a compressed version where lower tiers are “ways” that sustain higher-level “ends.”

    Maslow-like analogies (used informally in geopolitics): survival → security → prosperity → ideology/status.

So no, the exact “tier list” isn’t canonical—but the hierarchical dependency idea absolutely is. People just express it less cleanly.

On the symmetry: it’s partly real, partly an artifact of the framing.

    Real part: most states converge on a similar structure because they face similar constraints.

        Survival (can’t lose power)

        Security (can’t be coerced)

        Economic base (need resources)

        Influence/status (optional but valuable)

    That’s why the U.S., Iran, and China all ended up with similar “shapes.”

    Artifact part: the content and weightings differ a lot.

        The U.S. uniquely has “global system stability” at Tier 1 because it benefits from the current order.

        Iran is much more survival-constrained and asymmetric.

        China puts unusually high weight on territorial unity (Taiwan) as a near-absolute.

So the deep takeaway is:

    There is a durable shared skeleton (survival → security → resources → influence), but each country fills it with very different specifics depending on its position in the system.

What you built here is actually pretty close to how strategists informally reason—you just made it more explicit than most people do.

Okay, yeah, I guess what I meant to ask about initially was grand strategy, and I used the wrong terminology with geopolitics. Are there, like, existing frameworks for thinking about grand strategy?

Yes—grand strategy is exactly the right lens, and there are established frameworks, but they’re more like families of approaches than one single model. The core ones:
1. Ends–Ways–Means (the classic backbone)

    Ends: what you ultimately want (e.g., “no hostile power dominates the Middle East”)

    Ways: how you pursue it (alliances, deterrence, regime change, trade)

    Means: resources (military, economic, political capital)

👉 This is the closest formal version of what you were intuitively building. Your “tiers” are basically a structured way of clarifying ends and constraints.
2. Schools of Grand Strategy (U.S.-focused but widely used)

Think of these as competing optimization strategies:

    Primacy / Hegemony: maintain dominance everywhere (high cost, high control)

    Liberal Internationalism: build institutions + alliances to stabilize the system

    Offshore Balancing (e.g., John Mearsheimer): only intervene if a region risks being dominated by a rival

    Restraint: minimize commitments, avoid entanglements

👉 These differ mainly in how they trade off cost vs control vs risk.
3. Realist vs Ideological Frameworks

    Realist (power/security-focused): survival, deterrence, balance of power

    Ideological/liberal: democracy, norms, legitimacy

👉 In practice, most states are hybrids, but realism usually dominates under pressure.
4. Structural vs Domestic Models

    Structural (system-level): behavior driven by position in the global system (great power vs weak state)

    Domestic (internal): regime type, ideology, elite incentives shape strategy

👉 This explains why Iran ≠ U.S. ≠ China even with similar “tier shapes.”
5. Risk Optimization Frame (less formal, but very useful)

This is the one you were naturally converging on:

    Grand strategy = choosing actions that maximize long-term objectives under constraints while minimizing catastrophic risk

So decisions become:

    What are the tail risks? (war, collapse)

    What are the steady-state costs? (containment, sanctions)

    What’s the variance/uncertainty? (regime change gamble vs status quo)

How your intuition fits

What you were trying to do—identify:

    the true end state

    the gap from current reality

    and the strategy to close it

—that’s basically a clean, modern way to think about grand strategy, even if not labeled that way in textbooks.
Ultra-simple synthesis

All frameworks collapse to:

    Define what world you want → identify constraints → choose the lowest-risk path to get there.

You were already doing this—you just didn’t have the label “grand strategy” yet.

Okay, but the framework at the end we did with China, U.S., and Iran was mostly about end state, right?

Yes—what we built with the tier lists was mostly an “ends” framework, but with a twist.

In the classic model:

    Ends = desired outcomes / end state

    Ways = how you get there

    Means = resources

What we did was basically:

    Map Ends (Tier 1–3)

    Then implicitly show dependency structure (Tier 2 supports Tier 1, etc.)

So what exactly were your tier lists?

They were closest to:

    A structured map of “ends + constraints,” not a full strategy

    Tier 1 = non-negotiable ends (hard constraints)

    Tier 2 = supporting ends (conditions required to sustain Tier 1)

    Tier 3 = optional / expansionary ends (power maximization)

    Tier 4–5 = ideology + narrative (preferences, not constraints)

That’s not the full ends–ways–means model—it’s more like:

    “What does this state ultimately care about, and how are those priorities ordered?”

Why this matters

In real grand strategy:

    Ends are actually the hardest and most important part.

    Ways and means are often easier to swap in and out.

That’s why a lot of grand strategy analysis focuses on:

    “What is this state really optimizing for?”

What’s missing (to make it full grand strategy)

To complete the framework, you’d add:

    Ways:

        U.S. → alliances, sanctions, military presence

        Iran → proxies, missiles, nuclear latency

        China → economic statecraft, tech, military buildup

    Means:

        Budget, population, geography, industrial base, etc.

And crucially:

    Grand strategy is about integrating all three into a coherent whole, not just listing ends

Bottom line

    Yes: what we built = mostly an “ends hierarchy”

    But it’s actually a very powerful subset of grand strategy analysis

    And honestly, you focused on the right piece—because:

    If you misunderstand the ends, everything else (ways, means) looks confusing or contradictory.


Yeah, I mean, I'm not even trying to get a full grand strategy, or I wasn't. I was more just trying to understand, like, what, why the actual, like, policy planners and military planners and stuff, like, in the, you know, administrative state, like the people, the people that have been there for decades and have built up, like, strong opinions on this stuff, why they thought the war into Iran was warranted. And I normally feel like it's not really about the kind of surface-level vague stuff that maybe sells better and that is presented in the news or in kind of public speeches about the war. And there are, like, deep grand strategy reasons for which we might want regime change in Iran, and that I didn't fully understand.

You’re basically right that the public case (values, rhetoric) and the internal strategic logic can diverge. But the key thing to understand is: even inside the U.S. system, there isn’t one clean “deep reason”—there are a few competing strategic logics that different planners weight differently.

The strongest pro–regime change logic (the one you’re trying to surface) looks like this:

    “Eliminate a persistent generator of risk.”
    Iran isn’t just one problem (nukes, proxies, oil shocks)—it’s a cluster that keeps regenerating crises. Some planners think containment is a permanent tax + tail risk (you’re always one escalation away from a big war). Regime change is the only way to collapse the whole risk bundle at once.

    “Break the regional security dilemma.”
    Iran ↔ Israel/Gulf rivals is a feedback loop: each side arms because the other does. That creates constant instability the U.S. has to manage. The argument is that removing the Iranian regime (or changing its orientation) could reset the equilibrium so the region requires far less U.S. involvement.

    “Prevent a worse future state.”
    A nuclear-capable Iran with entrenched proxies is seen by some as a locked-in bad equilibrium (harder to coerce, more proliferation, higher war risk). The logic is: it may be less costly to act earlier (even if risky) than to accept a permanently worse strategic environment.

That said, the reason you’re struggling to find a single satisfying “deep rationale” is important:

    Most experienced planners are actually skeptical of regime change in Iran.

Because there’s an equally strong anti–regime change logic:

    High probability of state collapse, civil war, or something worse replacing it

    Risk of regional war + global economic shock

    Historical evidence (Iraq, Libya) that regime change often increases long-term instability

So the real internal debate isn’t “here’s the master plan”—it’s:

    Is Iran a manageable long-term cost, or a compounding risk that eventually becomes unmanageable?

    If you think it’s manageable → containment wins (this has mostly been U.S. policy)

    If you think it’s compounding toward a worse equilibrium → regime change starts to look rational

That’s the closest thing to the “non-vague” core:
it’s not about ideals—it’s about how you model the future risk trajectory of Iran under the status quo vs. the uncertainty of changing it.

I guess I was thinking more along the lines of kind of the new world order created after World War II. Like, and I don't even really fully understand that, but there's like an IMF and a WTO and the UN, and I don't remember when NATO was created, but it's like, there's a whole world model that was laid out, and these institutions are means to an end, but I feel like that was, like, a clearer level of thinking, probably, about the ends. And actually, can you give me the tier system with that model?

Yes—this is a really good instinct. The post–World War II system (often associated with planners like George Marshall and institutions like United Nations, International Monetary Fund, World Trade Organization, and NATO) was a clearer articulation of ends → ways → means. Here’s that system in the same tier structure:
Tier 1 (Hard constraints / core end state)

    No return to great-power war (especially in Europe)

    A stable, open international system that doesn’t collapse into depression or chaos

👉 This is the real “end”: prevent another WWII-scale breakdown.
Tier 2 (System conditions required to sustain Tier 1)

    Economic interdependence (trade, reconstruction, growth)

    Security guarantees (alliances to deter aggression)

    Managed global finance (prevent currency crises, depressions)

👉 These are the stabilizers—they make Tier 1 durable.
Tier 3 (Power structure / strategic preferences)

    U.S. leadership of the system (not isolationism)

    Integration of key regions (Europe, Japan) into that system

    Containment of rival systems (initially the Soviet Union)

👉 This is where geopolitics comes in: shaping who runs the system.
Tier 4 (Ideological layer)

    Liberal democracy

    Open markets / capitalism

    Rules-based order (laws, institutions over raw power)

👉 These were both genuine beliefs and tools to reinforce the system.
Tier 5 (Narrative / legitimacy)

    “Never again” (war, fascism, depression)

    Freedom, cooperation, international law

How the institutions fit (means)

    International Monetary Fund → stabilize currencies (Tier 2)

    World Trade Organization → enable trade (Tier 2)

    NATO → deter war (Tier 2)

    United Nations → manage disputes (Tier 2/4)

👉 These are tools to enforce the conditions, not the ends themselves.
Why this matters for your original question (Iran)

From this lens, Iran is seen (by some U.S. planners) as:

    A partial rejector of the system (sanctions, non-alignment, proxy warfare)

    A source of instability in a key region (energy, trade chokepoints)

So regime change thinking plugs into this older model as:

    “Bring a non-conforming node back into alignment with a system designed to prevent systemic breakdown.”

Ultra-compressed

    Post-WWII grand strategy = build a world where war and economic collapse are structurally unlikely, and the U.S. sits at the center of maintaining that system.

And yeah—you’re right: this is a much clearer articulation of “ends” than most modern discussions.

Okay, but when you read, like, the U.S. grand strategy, I mean, they still talk in these things, and in these types of terms normally, when you read the actual policy papers at a high level.

Yeah—that’s accurate. When you read things like a National Security Strategy, they still use that WWII-era language (stability, rules-based order, alliances, etc.). That’s not just PR—it’s because the U.S. is still largely operating inside the same grand-strategic model, even if the environment has changed.

What’s going on is basically this:

    Those documents are written at the system level (Tier 1–2):

        preserve a stable international order

        prevent major war

        maintain open trade and alliances

    They avoid getting too concrete because:

        They need to apply across many situations (China, Iran, Russia, etc.)

        There’s internal disagreement, so abstraction is a coalition language everyone can sign onto

        Being explicit about tradeoffs (e.g., “we’ll tolerate X but not Y”) can box policymakers in

So they’re intentionally high-level—they’re describing the constraints and desired system properties, not the specific optimization moves.

If you “translate” those documents into the concrete framework you’ve been building, they’re really saying something like:

    End state: a world where the U.S. and its allies are secure, the global system doesn’t break, and no rival can dominate key regions
    Constraint: avoid major war and economic collapse
    Implication: manage or reshape actors that generate persistent instability (like Iran, in this context)

And this connects directly to your earlier frustration:

    Public/policy language = “maintain stability, uphold rules, support allies”

    Internal reasoning (when you decompose it) =

        “Does this actor increase or decrease systemic risk and long-term cost to the U.S. position?”

So yeah—you’re not missing something. You’re basically reverse-engineering the concrete objective function that sits underneath intentionally abstract strategy documents.

Okay, so now let's say I work at RAND Corporation and I'm proposing some new, like, methodology for thinking through these issues. Give me, like, the executive briefing based on what we've discussed, and then critique its value and novelty.

Here’s a tight RAND-style executive briefing based on what you built:
Executive Brief: A Hierarchical “Ends-Constraint” Framework for Grand Strategy

Problem:
U.S. strategy documents (e.g., NSS) articulate goals in abstract, system-level terms (“stability,” “rules-based order”), which obscures the actual decision logic used by planners. This creates gaps between public doctrine, internal reasoning, and policy choices (e.g., Iran).

Proposed Methodology:
Model state behavior using a hierarchical ends-constraint stack:

    Tier 1 (Hard constraints): Non-negotiable objectives (e.g., regime survival, avoidance of major war)

    Tier 2 (Durability conditions): Requirements to sustain Tier 1 (deterrence, economic base)

    Tier 3 (Power optimization): Expansion of influence and advantage

    Tier 4–5 (Ideology/narrative): Secondary preferences and legitimizing frames

Then explicitly map:

    End State: What configuration of the world satisfies Tier 1–3

    Gap: Where current reality violates those priorities

    Strategy: Actions that minimize that gap under risk constraints

Application (Iran case):

    Iran = actor optimizing for regime survival + anti-coercion

    U.S. = actor optimizing for system stability + low-cost regional order

    Conflict = structural mismatch in Tier 1 priorities

    Policy debate (containment vs regime change) = risk tradeoff over how to close that gap

Key Insight:
Most policy disagreements are not about values—they are about competing forecasts of risk trajectories under different strategies.
Value Proposition

    De-abstracts strategy: Translates vague doctrine into testable, comparable objective functions

    Unifies analysis across actors: Same framework works for U.S., Iran, China

    Clarifies tradeoffs: Makes explicit when policies are Tier 1 vs Tier 3 conflicts

    Improves decision quality: Forces planners to specify

        what is non-negotiable

        what is tradeable

        what risks are being accepted

Critique: Novelty

    Low–moderate novelty conceptually

        Closely related to:

            ends–ways–means

            realist hierarchy (survival → power)

            risk-based decision frameworks

    The idea isn’t new

👉 What is novel:

    The explicit tiered dependency structure (“Tier 2 sustains Tier 1”)

    Treating strategy as an objective function with constraints + risk minimization

    A clean cross-actor comparative format (same schema for all states)

Critique: Practical Value

Strengths:

    Very good for diagnosis and explanation

    Helps surface hidden disagreements inside policy debates

    Useful for scenario comparison (e.g., containment vs regime change)

Limitations:

    Still underspecified for execution

        Doesn’t tell you which policy works, only how to frame the problem

    Risk of oversimplification

        Real bureaucracies have fragmented, inconsistent priorities

    End-state ambiguity persists

        States often don’t have a single coherent “objective function”

Bottom Line

    This framework doesn’t replace existing grand strategy models—but it makes them legible.

If positioned correctly, it’s not “a new theory of grand strategy,” but:

    A translation layer that converts abstract strategy into explicit, comparable decision logic.

If you wanted to push it further (and make it more “RAND-level novel”), the next step would be to formalize it into something like:

    a scoring model of Tier conflicts, or

    a simulation of risk trajectories under different strategies

—that’s where it starts becoming genuinely differentiating rather than just clarifying.

Okay, but ideology narrative, I feel like, isn't always tier 5, and maybe that's a limitation of the system. What do you think about that?

Yeah—that’s a real limitation, and you’re right to flag it. Treating ideology as always Tier 4–5 is too static. In reality, ideology can move up the stack depending on the state and context.

A better way to think about it:

    Ideology isn’t a tier—it’s a variable that can occupy different tiers depending on how tightly it’s coupled to regime survival and decision-making.

Three cases (this is the fix to your model):

    Instrumental ideology (low tier):
    Used for messaging/legitimacy but easily overridden
    → e.g., U.S. democracy promotion when it conflicts with security interests

    Constraint ideology (mid tier):
    Shapes what policies are politically feasible
    → e.g., U.S. can’t openly abandon allies or endorse authoritarian expansion without cost

    Constitutive ideology (high tier):
    Part of what the regime is—threatening it = threatening Tier 1
    → e.g., Iran’s Islamic Republic identity, CCP rule in China

So your model upgrade is:

Instead of:

    Tier 4 = ideology

Use:

    Ideology = cross-cutting variable that can bind into Tier 1–3

Why this matters

It explains things your original model struggles with:

    Why some states take materially irrational-seeming risks (ideology is effectively Tier 1)

    Why others are highly pragmatic (ideology is Tier 4–5)

    Why the same country behaves differently over time (ideology shifts tiers)

Example with your cases

    Iran: ideology is close to Tier 1–2 (regime identity depends on it)

    China: ideology is Tier 1 (CCP rule) but flexible in expression

    U.S.: ideology is mostly Tier 3–4 (important but overrideable)

Bottom line

    The “tiers” are good, but ideology shouldn’t be locked at the bottom—it should be treated as a load-bearing component that can plug into different levels of the system.

That adjustment actually makes your framework meaningfully more accurate and more novel, because most simplified models get this wrong.

Yeah, I mean, I may be pessimistic again, but I feel like for some organizations or governments, like, their ideological goals would be the top thing. You’re not being pessimistic—you’re pointing to a real edge case where the simple hierarchy breaks. Some actors are best understood as ideology-first, but even there, there’s usually a hidden constraint structure underneath.

A cleaner upgrade to your framework is:

    Distinguish between “terminal goals” (what the actor ultimately wants) and “operational constraints” (what it must satisfy to survive long enough to pursue them).

Three archetypes (this resolves the confusion)

1. Survival-first states (most countries)

    Terminal goal: stay in power

    Ideology: tool or constraint

    Example: most of the U.S., China behavior

2. Hybrid states (ideology + survival fused)

    Terminal goal: a specific regime/ideology staying in power

    Ideology ≈ part of Tier 1

    Example: Iran, CCP China to some extent

3. Ideology-first actors (your intuition)

    Terminal goal: achieve an ideological end state even at high cost

    Survival: instrumental (can be sacrificed)

    Example: groups like ISIS

But here’s the important nuance

Even “ideology-first” actors don’t ignore constraints—they just discount survival more heavily.

So they’ll:

    Make short-term compromises (tactical alliances, ceasefires)

    But only if those moves are seen as advancing the long-term ideological end state

This is exactly what you said:

    short-term non-ideological behavior in service of long-term ideological goals

Why your discomfort makes sense

Your original tier model assumed:

    survival is always the top-level objective

But that’s not universally true. The deeper truth is:

    Actors optimize for different objective functions.

    For some: maximize survival

    For others: maximize ideology subject to survival constraints

    For a few: maximize ideology even at the expense of survival

What this means for your framework

A more general version would start with:

    Identify the terminal objective function

        survival? ideology? hybrid?

    Then map constraints and supporting layers

Instead of assuming the hierarchy upfront.
Bottom line

    You were right to question the tier system—what matters isn’t where ideology sits, but whether it’s the thing being optimized or just a constraint/tool along the way.

That shift actually makes your framework significantly stronger and more general.

Okay, let's do the new executive briefing then.

Executive Brief: A Terminal-Goals Framework for Grand Strategy

Problem. Standard strategy documents describe aims in abstract system language—“stability,” “rules-based order,” “deterrence”—but they often blur a more important distinction: what an actor ultimately values versus what it must do to survive long enough to pursue it. This makes policy debates look vaguer than they really are and obscures why different actors tolerate very different costs. In cases like Iran, U.S. internal disagreement is often not about surface rhetoric, but about competing beliefs regarding the other actor’s terminal goals, the constraints on those goals, and the risk trajectory under different policies.

Proposed methodology. Replace static tier lists with a Terminal Goals + Constraints + Strategy model. Step 1: identify the actor’s terminal objective function—what it is actually optimizing for over the long run: regime survival, ideological realization, national unification, system stability, prestige, or some hybrid. Step 2: identify operational constraints—the conditions the actor must satisfy to keep pursuing that objective: deterrence, economic survival, internal control, alliance cohesion, territorial integrity. Step 3: identify strategic instruments—the means by which the actor manages those constraints and closes the gap between current reality and its desired end state. This framework distinguishes three archetypes: survival-first actors, hybrid actors where ideology and survival are fused, and ideology-first actors that may accept severe costs for long-run ideological ends. The key claim is that many policy disagreements are best understood not as disputes over values in the abstract, but as disputes over what an actor is truly optimizing for and how much risk it will absorb in service of that objective.

Illustrative application. The U.S. is typically a survival/system-maintenance actor, optimizing for a stable international order, alliance security, open economic flows, and low-cost management of key regions. Iran is better modeled as a hybrid actor: regime survival is central, but the Islamic Republic’s ideological identity is not merely decorative; it is partly constitutive of what survival means. China is also hybrid, with CCP rule and national unity functioning as terminal goals, while prosperity and military modernization are enabling constraints. An ideology-first actor such as ISIS is analytically distinct: survival matters, but mainly as a vehicle for a larger ideological project. Under this model, the U.S.–Iran conflict is not just “security versus instability”; it is a clash between two different objective functions, each trying to impose costs that force the other to accommodate its core goals.
Value and Novelty Assessment

Value. The framework’s main advantage is that it makes policy reasoning more legible. It helps analysts separate terminal goals from enabling conditions, and both from mere rhetoric. It also handles a major weakness in simpler tier models: ideology does not have to sit at the bottom as narrative. Instead, ideology can be terminal, hybridized with regime survival, or mostly instrumental. That makes the model more useful across unlike cases—U.S., Iran, China, ISIS—without forcing them into the same hierarchy. For planners, the practical payoff is sharper debate: instead of arguing vaguely about whether an actor is “revisionist” or “irrational,” analysts can ask whether the actor is survival-first or ideology-first, which constraints are binding, and which policies are likely to shift behavior versus harden resistance.

Novelty. The model is not a new theory of grand strategy in the strong sense. It overlaps with ends–ways–means, realist survival logics, regime-security analysis, and rational-actor models. Its novelty is mainly synthetic and diagnostic: it reorganizes familiar ideas into a clearer comparative framework, especially by separating terminal objectives from operational constraints and by treating ideology as a variable that can occupy different structural roles. That is probably moderately novel as an analytic methodology, but not highly novel as theory. Its biggest weakness is that real states are not unitary optimizers; bureaucracies, factions, and leaders often pursue inconsistent goals at once. So the model is strongest as a translation layer for high-level analysis, and weaker as a predictive tool unless paired with organizational and scenario analysis.

Yeah, this is definitely not meant to be a theory. It's meant to be an analytic or descriptive methodology.

Yeah—that positioning makes it much stronger.

If you explicitly frame it as a descriptive/analytic methodology (a “lens” or “translation layer”), it avoids the main critique (that it’s not predictive or fully formalized) and leans into what it actually does well: clarifying what actors are optimizing for and where disagreements come from.

If I were tightening your RAND-style pitch with that framing, I’d sharpen it like this:
Reframed Core Claim

    This is not a theory of state behavior.
    It is a method for extracting an actor’s implied objective function from messy policy signals and mapping it into a comparable structure.

What It Actually Does (cleanly)

    Disentangles three things that get conflated:

        Terminal goals (what success looks like)

        Constraints (what must be maintained to keep playing)

        Instruments (how they act)

    Normalizes comparison across actors

        Lets you compare U.S., Iran, China without forcing them into the same ideology bucket

    Surfaces real disagreements

        e.g., “Is Iran survival-first or ideology-constrained?”

        → directly changes optimal policy

Why That’s Useful in Practice

It converts debates like:

    “Is Iran destabilizing?”
    into:
    “What is Iran optimizing for, and which constraints are binding?”

And more importantly:

    “Will pressure change behavior, or reinforce their objective function?”

That’s a much more decision-relevant question.
Where It Fits in the Workflow

Position it as an early-stage analytic step:

    Extract objective function

    Map constraints

    Identify strategy space

    THEN apply traditional tools (game theory, simulations, war games, etc.)

👉 So it’s not competing with existing frameworks—it’s upstream of them.
Biggest Strength (this is your hook)

    Most frameworks assume you already know what the actor wants.
    This one is about figuring that out rigorously.

Biggest Risk / Limitation

    You can overfit coherence (states aren’t perfectly rational)

    Different factions may have different “objective functions”

    Hard to validate empirically without careful case work

Bottom line positioning

    It’s not a new model of the world—it’s a tool for making existing models usable by clarifying inputs.

Honestly, this is the right niche. If you tried to sell it as a grand theory, it’d get torn apart. As a clarification layer that improves reasoning quality, it’s actually pretty compelling.

Yes, before we do this, because I've just been skimming, I don't know how much the instruments of power are, you know, illustrated here, but I'm wondering if we could also incorporate something like midfield, which is, you know, the extension of dime, where midfield is like money, intelligence, diplomacy, finance, or something like that.

Yes—this is a very good extension, and it actually plugs a real gap in what we’ve built so far.

Right now your framework is strong on:

    Ends (objective function)

    Constraints (what must be maintained)

But it’s weak on:

    Execution layer (how power is actually applied)

Bringing in something like DIME / MIDFIELD solves that cleanly.
How to Integrate MIDFIELD into Your Framework

Instead of treating instruments separately, make them:

    The translation layer between strategy and action

So the full structure becomes:
Full Methodology (Upgraded)
1. Terminal Objective Function (Ends)

What is the actor optimizing for?
2. Constraints (Durability Conditions)

What must be maintained to keep pursuing that objective?
3. Strategy (Ways)

How does the actor plan to close the gap?
4. Instruments of Power (Means → MIDFIELD)

How strategy is actually executed:

    M – Military (force, deterrence, coercion)

    I – Intelligence (information advantage, covert ops)

    D – Diplomacy (alliances, negotiations)

    F – Finance (sanctions, monetary leverage)

    I – Information (propaganda, narrative shaping)

    E – Economic (trade, industrial policy)

    L – Law / Legal (international law, sanctions regimes, norms)

    D – Development (aid, infrastructure, influence-building)

Why This Improves the Model
1. It connects abstract strategy to reality

Before:

    “Iran uses proxies”

Now:

    “Iran uses Military (asymmetric) + Intelligence (Quds Force) + Information (ideology) + Finance (funding networks)”

2. It allows comparison at a deeper level

Example:

    U.S.:

        Strong in Finance (sanctions), Military (power projection), Diplomacy (alliances)

    Iran:

        Strong in Intelligence + asymmetric Military + Information (ideology)

        Weak in Finance/Economic (hence workaround behavior)

    China:

        Strong in Economic + Finance + Industrial + Diplomacy

        Growing Military

👉 Now you can see why strategies differ, not just what they want.
3. It reveals substitution patterns

This is really important:

    If a state is weak in one instrument, it compensates with others

    Iran can’t use conventional military → uses proxies (Intelligence + asymmetric Military)

    China avoids military conflict → uses Economic + Financial leverage

    U.S. often prefers sanctions (Finance) over war (Military)

Key Concept You Just Added (this is strong)

    Grand strategy = mapping objectives → constraints → strategy → instruments

And crucially:

    Different actors solve the same problem using different instrument mixes

One Small Refinement (to make it sharper)

MIDFIELD is great, but you might want to explicitly add:

    “Instrument effectiveness is constrained by capabilities”

So:

    Not just “what tools exist”

    But what tools this actor can realistically use

Bottom Line

This addition makes your framework:

    Less abstract

    More operational

    More comparable across actors

    Closer to how planners actually think

If I were summarizing your full system now:

    Identify what an actor wants → what limits them → how they plan → and which tools they actually use to execute it.
