Great—this is exactly the right kind of pushback. You’re basically asking:

    Are we inventing redundant node types?

    Where are the clean boundaries?

    How do we avoid infinite complexity?

Let’s tighten this.
1. Do we really need StrategicConfiguration?
Short answer:

    No, not as a separate ontology primitive.
    But you do need to distinguish types of world-state variables.

Cleaner solution (preferred)

Instead of:

    WorldState

    StrategicConfiguration

Use:

    WorldState with typed substructure

Define:
WorldState = vector of state variables

Each variable has a type
Types:

    Structural (slow-moving, persistent)

        alliance systems

        nuclear arsenals

        industrial base

        proxy ecosystems

    Positional (relative, actor-specific)

        nuclear threshold status

        deterrence credibility

        regional influence

    Flow (fast-changing)

        current military deployment

        sanctions level

        conflict intensity

So your earlier intuition becomes:

    StrategicConfiguration = structural world variables

    StrategicPosition = positional world variables

👉 Not new nodes—just typed attributes of W
Why this is cleaner

You avoid:

    node proliferation

    redundancy

    weird edge duplication

But you still capture:

    persistence

    structure

    relative positioning

2. Configuration vs Position (clean definition)
Structural variable (Configuration-like)

    A world variable that exists independently of a specific actor’s perspective

Examples:

    “Number of nuclear warheads in system”

    “Existence of alliance network”

    “Presence of proxy networks”

Positional variable

    A world variable defined relative to an actor

Examples:

    “Iran is nuclear-threshold state”

    “U.S. has escalation dominance”

    “Iran has regional deterrent depth”

Key distinction
Type    Depends on actor?    Example
Structural    No    nuclear arsenal exists
Positional    Yes    actor has deterrence advantage
So your question:

    “Is nuclear threshold state just a world state?”

Answer:

    Yes—but specifically a positional world variable, not a structural one.

3. Do we care how structures were caused?

You asked:

    does it matter whether they came from strategy?

Answer:

    Usually no, unless you're modeling dynamics or attribution

So:

    For static analysis → ignore origin

    For dynamic analysis → track:

        Action → WorldState change

👉 You don’t need a special node for “caused by strategy”
4. Revised ontology simplification
Keep nodes minimal:

    Actor

    Goal

    Belief

    Constraint

    Capability

    Strategy

    Action

    WorldState

    Outcome

Extend WorldState with schema:

WorldState = {
  structural: {...},
  positional: {...},
  flow: {...}
}

Example

WorldState = {
  structural: {
    "nuclear_arsenal": true,
    "proxy_networks": true
  },
  positional: {
    "iran_nuclear_threshold": true,
    "us_regional_dominance": partial
  },
  flow: {
    "sanctions_level": high,
    "military_tension": medium
  }
}

5. Non-unitary actors (tractability vs realism)

You nailed the core tradeoff.

    Infinite realism vs usable model

Solution: Hierarchical modeling with cutoffs
Level 1 (default)

    Actor = unitary approximation

Level 2 (when needed)

    Add SubActors selectively:

        IRGC

        Supreme Leader

        US executive vs Congress

Level 3 (rare)

    Network actors / identity groups

Add one rule:

    Only split an actor when it changes predictions

Example

If:

    IRGC and Iranian government behave similarly → keep one node

If:

    IRGC drives proxy behavior differently → split

So:

Actor can be:

Actor
 ├── SubActor (optional)

But not required.
6. Your preference-region insight (refined)

You were right:

    Preferences don’t change much
    Acceptability does

So define:
Ideal utility

From goals:
Ui(W)
Ui​(W)
Acceptance constraint

From constraints + beliefs:
W∈Ai⊆W
W∈Ai​⊆W
So:

    Ideal = maximize utility

    Acceptable = feasible + survivable

That means:

You don’t need a new node necessarily—just:

    AcceptanceRegion = derived from (Constraint, Belief)

7. Final clean ontology (simplified)
Nodes:

    Actor

    Goal

    Belief

    Constraint

    Capability

    Strategy

    Action

    WorldState

    Outcome

WorldState types:

    structural

    positional

    flow

Actor structure:

    optional SubActors

Key insight (this is your clean resolution)

    You don’t need more nodes—you need better typing of world-state variables and controlled decomposition of actors

8. Why this is better than adding nodes

    Keeps model tractable

    Avoids redundancy

    Keeps graph interpretable

    Still captures:

        deterrence

        signaling

        structure

        positioning

9. Bottom line

You were right to question:

    StrategicConfiguration → redundant

    StrategicPosition → better as typed world-state variable

    Non-unitarity → must be optional and controlled

Final distilled rule:

    If something exists in the world → it’s WorldState (typed)
    If it depends on perspective → it’s positional
    If it’s persistent → it’s structural
    If it’s fast-changing → it’s flow

If you want next, we can:

    re-run the U.S.–Iran graph with this simplified ontology (it’ll be much cleaner), or

    formalize the WorldState schema + variable taxonomy, which is probably the next critical piece.

Strategy → WorldState (THIS IS KEY)
US

        flow: sanctions intensity ↑

        frustrates → US system_stability

4. Where the Model Works
A. Strategy → World linkage is now clean

You correctly identified this earlier.

We can now say:

    Iran doesn’t just “use proxies”

    It increases proxy_network_presence (structural variable)

This is a big improvement.
B. Positional vs structural works

Example:

    proxy networks = structural

    regional depth = positional

That distinction holds.
C. Beliefs → strategy is doing real work

The model clearly shows:

    same world → different strategies because of beliefs

5. Stress Test: Where It Breaks

Now the important part.
Problem 1: “Conflict” still not well represented

Right now we have:

    proxy networks ↑ → bad for US

    sanctions ↑ → bad for Iran

But the ontology does NOT clearly represent:

    These are mutually reinforcing antagonistic dynamics

We’re missing:
Needed:

    feedback loop edges

Example:

    sanctions ↑ → Iran deterrence reliance ↑

    deterrence ↑ → US threat perception ↑

    threat perception ↑ → sanctions ↑

Fix:

Add edge type:

FEEDBACKS_TO

or better:

    AMPLIFIES

    COUNTERACTS

Problem 2: “Means are unacceptable” still weak

You identified this earlier.

Right now:

    proxies → world_state change

    US doesn’t like outcome

But in reality:

    US objects to the method itself, not just the outcome

Missing concept:

    Normative constraint on methods

Example:

    proxy warfare = illegitimate method

    sanctions = illegitimate coercion (from Iran perspective)

Fix:

Add:

VIOLATES_NORM

    Strategy -> Norm

    Instrument -> Norm

Problem 3: Beliefs are too coarse

We still have:

    belief_us_iran_is_threat

But real belief structure is:

    about intentions

    about capabilities

    about thresholds

    about future behavior

Fix:

Beliefs should be structured:

Belief:
  type: deterrence_model
  target: actor_iran
  claim: "proxy escalation raises war probability"

So:

    beliefs need internal schema, not just nodes

Problem 4: Non-unitary issue shows up immediately

Example:

    US Congress vs Pentagon vs President

    IRGC vs Iranian government

These actors:

    have different goals

    have different beliefs

    produce different actions

Problem:

Current graph:

    assumes coherent strategy

Reality:

    strategy is negotiated output

Fix:

You don’t need to always model it, but:

    Must support optional decomposition

Problem 5: Time / sequencing is missing

Example:

    sanctions → economic pressure → political response → escalation

Right now:

    edges are static

But reality is:

    order matters

Fix:

Edges need:

    temporal ordering

    lag structure

Problem 6: Acceptance vs preference still not explicit

We implicitly used:

    Iran tolerates sanctions

    US tolerates proxy activity

But:

    This is not modeled explicitly

Fix:

Need explicit:

    ACCEPTABLE_WORLDSTATE

    derived from:

        constraints

        beliefs

Problem 7: Strategic substitution is under-modeled

Example:

    Iran cannot use conventional force → uses proxies

    US avoids war → uses sanctions

We don’t explicitly represent:

    instrument substitution

Fix:

Add:

SUBSTITUTES_FOR

    Strategy -> Strategy

    Instrument -> Instrument

6. Biggest Missing Pieces (Ranked)
1. Feedback loops (critical)

Without this, escalation dynamics are invisible.
2. Norms / legitimacy layer (important)

Explains “means are unacceptable”
3. Belief structure (important)

Beliefs are currently too shallow
4. Time dynamics (important)

Needed for real analysis
5. Non-unitary actors (conditional)

Needed in complex cases
6. Acceptance vs ideal distinction (important but manageable)
7. What Held Up Well

    WorldState typing (big success)

    Strategy → world linkage

    Goal / constraint separation

    Belief → strategy mapping

8. Bottom Line

This second stress test shows:

    The ontology is structurally sound, but missing dynamic and normative layers

Specifically:

    feedback

    time

    belief structure

    method legitimacy

If we continue (recommended next step)

We should:

    Add feedback + norm + time extensions
    AND THEN
    re-run the graph one more time

After that, we move to:

    formal variable taxonomy for WorldState

That’s the last big piece before this becomes truly operational.

I noticed you didn't fit capabilities into the midfield model. Not that I think midfield is comprehensive, but is there a reason for that, or was it an oversight, or what? It would be cool, I mean, this is, like, I don't wanna get into scope creep before we stabilize, but it would be cool to view this as a cybernetic system in some way in the long term, where we not only get a map of, you know, these things, but a dynamic sense of how things might change over time. Good catch—that was an oversight, not a principled omission.

Capabilities should absolutely be fit into MIDFIELD, at least as a first-pass taxonomy. The clean way to do it is:

    Capability = what an actor can do

    MIDFIELD domain = the category that capability belongs to

    Instrument = how that capability is operationalized

    Action = a specific use of the instrument

So for example:

    cap_us_financial_sanctions → Finance

    cap_us_power_projection → Military

    cap_us_alliance_coordination → mostly Diplomacy

    cap_iran_proxy_networks → mixed Military / Intelligence / Information

    cap_iran_missiles → Military

    cap_iran_regional_messaging → **Information / Diplomacy`

That also exposes a useful issue: some capabilities are cross-domain, so MIDFIELD is a good organizing scheme but not a perfectly exclusive partition. That’s fine; it just means capability nodes should allow either one primary domain plus secondary tags, or multiple domain memberships.

And yes, the cybernetic direction makes a lot of sense later. The ontology is starting to want three layers: state description, control/feedback structure, and dynamics over time. But I agree with your instinct not to let that explode yet. So for this next rerun, let’s keep it tight and just add:

    MIDFIELD-tagged capabilities

    feedback edges

    norm/legitimacy edges

    slightly better belief typing

    DEPLOYS_INSTRUMENT(strategy_us_deterrence, instrument_us_force_posture)

    ALTERS_WORLDSTATE(instrument_iran_financial_workarounds, ws_flow_sanctions_intensity)
    Issue: awkward; really it alters effective sanctions pressure, not sanctions intensity itself.

World state to goals

    FRUSTRATES_GOAL(ws_flow_proxy_activity_level, goal_us_system_stability)

    FRUSTRATES_GOAL(ws_flow_sanctions_intensity, goal_iran_regime_survival)

    SUPPORTS_GOAL(ws_pos_iran_regional_depth, goal_iran_non_coercion)

    SUPPORTS_GOAL(ws_pos_us_regional_influence, goal_us_protect_allies)

Norm/legitimacy edges

    VIOLATES_NORM(instrument_iran_proxy_support, norm_us_no_proxy_destabilization)

    VIOLATES_NORM(instrument_us_sanctions, norm_iran_no_external_coercion)

    SUPPORTS_NORM(strategy_us_containment, norm_us_nonproliferation)

    SUPPORTS_NORM(strategy_iran_forward_deterrence, norm_iran_sovereign_independence)

Feedback edges

    AMPLIFIES(ws_flow_sanctions_intensity, belief_iran_intentions_us_coercive)

    AMPLIFIES(ws_struct_proxy_network_presence, belief_us_intentions_iran_revisionist)

    AMPLIFIES(belief_iran_intentions_us_coercive, strategy_iran_forward_deterrence)

    AMPLIFIES(belief_us_intentions_iran_revisionist, strategy_us_coercive_pressure)

This gives the feedback loop:

    U.S. pressure ↑

    Iranian coercion belief ↑

    forward deterrence ↑

    proxy structure/activity ↑

    U.S. revisionist-threat belief ↑

    pressure ↑ again

That loop is now visible.
What improved

The MIDFIELD omission is fixed cleanly. Capabilities now sit in recognizable domains, and that makes substitution easier to see:

    U.S. substitutes Finance for Military when avoiding war

    Iran substitutes proxy-based Military/Intelligence for conventional weakness

The feedback structure also makes the graph much more realistic. It stops looking like a static dependency map and starts looking like an escalation system.

The norm layer helps with your earlier point that actors often reject not just outcomes but methods.
What still breaks

The biggest remaining issue is that some world-state variables are still too blunt.

For example, ws_flow_sanctions_intensity is different from:

    formal sanctions level

    effective economic pain

    perceived coercion

    elite pain

    public pain

Those are not the same variable, and the graph currently compresses them too hard.

Second, beliefs still want more structure. We really want something like:

    belief type = intention model

    target = actor_us

    content = coercive
    instead of one monolithic belief node.

Third, norms may not be enough. Some “unacceptable means” are not moral norms so much as stability norms, legal norms, or strategic taboos. So “norm” may be too narrow a label.

Fourth, the optional non-unitarity issue is still unresolved. This graph is usable, but it is still probably too unitary for serious analysis of either state.
Main gaps exposed by v3

    World-state taxonomy needs refinement, especially distinguishing formal conditions from effective conditions and perceived conditions.

    Beliefs need internal fields rather than only labels.

    Norms may need to broaden into a rule/taboo/legitimacy layer.

    Counterfactual and unavailable strategies need representation, because constraints often matter by blocking options that are not currently chosen.

    Actor decomposition needs a stopping rule, so we know when to split and when not to.

This is in good shape for the next step: a cleaner WorldState variable taxonomy, because that is now the main bottleneck.

Okay, let's proceed as you recommended then.

Good. The next bottleneck is the WorldState variable taxonomy, because too many important distinctions are getting collapsed into single variables.
WorldState variable taxonomy v1.0
1. Core rule

A WorldState variable is any feature of the environment that can matter strategically.

Each variable should be tagged on three axes:
A. Ontological type

What kind of thing is it?

    Structural: slow-moving background conditions

    Positional: actor-relative standing

    Flow: fast-changing activity or pressure

    Institutional: formal rules, commitments, legal architectures

    Perceptual: actor-specific representation of the world

B. Causal role

How does it function?

    Resource

    Constraint

    Exposure

    Leverage

    Signal

    Vulnerability

C. Epistemic status

Whose version is this?

    Actual

    Perceived by actor i

    Disputed

    Latent / inferred

That gives you a cleaner schema than just structural / positional / flow.
2. Definitions
Structural

Persistent features of the environment that usually change slowly.

Examples:

    alliance architecture

    industrial base

    proxy ecosystem

    sanctions regime architecture

    nuclear force structure

Test:

    If no one acts this week, does it mostly remain?

If yes, structural.
Positional

An actor’s relative place within a structure.

Examples:

    Iran’s regional depth

    U.S. alliance credibility

    deterrence advantage

    nuclear threshold status

    bargaining leverage

Test:

    Is this inherently relative to a specific actor?

If yes, positional.
Flow

Short-run levels of activity, intensity, or pressure.

Examples:

    sanctions pressure this quarter

    proxy attack frequency

    military tension

    diplomatic engagement

    capital flight

Test:

    Can this move materially in days, weeks, or months?

If yes, flow.
Institutional

Formalized rules, commitments, and recognized arrangements.

Examples:

    treaty obligations

    sanctions law

    IAEA inspection arrangements

    basing agreements

    constitutional restrictions

Why separate this from structural?
Because institutions are not just persistent facts; they are rule systems.
Perceptual

How the world is represented by a specific actor.

Examples:

    perceived U.S. willingness to escalate

    perceived Iranian resolve

    perceived effectiveness of sanctions

    perceived legitimacy of a method

This is where actual vs perceived world gets handled cleanly.
3. Recommended schema

A world variable should look like this:

{
  "var_id": "ws_flow_effective_sanctions_pressure_iran",
  "label": "Effective sanctions pressure on Iran",
  "ontology_type": "flow",
  "causal_role": ["constraint", "vulnerability"],
  "epistemic_status": "actual",
  "target_actor": "actor_iran",
  "timescale": "short-medium",
  "domain": ["finance", "economic"],
  "description": "Net coercive economic pressure after accounting for evasion and adaptation."
}

4. Important distinction rules

These are the distinctions your stress test showed you need.
Rule 1: Formal vs effective

Do not collapse:

    formal sanctions level

    effective economic pain

    perceived coercion

These are different variables.

Example:

    ws_inst_sanctions_regime

    ws_flow_effective_sanctions_pressure_iran

    ws_perc_iran_perceived_us_coercion

Rule 2: Structural vs positional

Do not collapse:

    proxy network exists

    Iran has regional depth because of that network

These are different variables.

Example:

    ws_struct_proxy_network_presence

    ws_pos_iran_regional_depth

Rule 3: Actual vs perceived

Do not collapse:

    actual war risk

    perceived war risk

Example:

    ws_flow_actual_escalation_risk

    ws_perc_us_escalation_risk

    ws_perc_iran_escalation_risk

Rule 4: Ideal vs effective capability environment

Do not collapse:

    nominal military capability

    capability available under current constraints

This may be partly capability and partly world state.

Example:

    capability: cap_iran_missile_force

    world variable: ws_flow_iran_usable_missile_readiness

5. Canonical variable families

These are the families I’d recommend as the basis set.
Security family

    alliance structure

    force posture

    escalation risk

    territorial control

    deterrence balance

    force readiness

Economic family

    sanctions regime

    effective sanctions pressure

    trade access

    oil revenue flow

    inflation / fiscal stress

    capital mobility

Political family

    internal legitimacy

    elite cohesion

    public unrest

    alliance cohesion

    diplomatic isolation

Informational family

    narrative dominance

    perceived resolve

    intelligence visibility

    attribution confidence

    signaling credibility

Technological / strategic family

    nuclear capability distribution

    nuclear latency

    missile survivability

    command-and-control resilience

    industrial replenishment capacity

Normative / institutional family

    legal authorization environment

    taboo status of methods

    treaty compliance state

    legitimacy of coercive tools

    recognized sovereignty constraints

6. Minimal U.S.–Iran variable set

Here is a cleaner set for the case.
Structural

    ws_struct_regional_alliance_architecture

    ws_struct_proxy_network_presence

    ws_struct_formal_sanctions_regime

    ws_struct_nuclear_capability_distribution

Positional

    ws_pos_us_regional_influence

    ws_pos_iran_regional_depth

    ws_pos_iran_nuclear_latency

    ws_pos_deterrence_balance

    ws_pos_us_alliance_credibility

Flow

    ws_flow_effective_sanctions_pressure_iran

    ws_flow_proxy_activity_level

    ws_flow_military_tension_level

    ws_flow_diplomatic_engagement_level

    ws_flow_internal_economic_stress_iran

Institutional

    ws_inst_nonproliferation_commitment_environment

    ws_inst_sanctions_legal_authority

    ws_inst_regional_security_commitments

Perceptual

    ws_perc_iran_perceived_us_coercion

    ws_perc_us_perceived_iran_revisionism

    ws_perc_us_perceived_proxy_attribution_confidence

    ws_perc_iran_perceived_deterrence_credibility

This is already much better than v3.
7. How edges should use the taxonomy
Example 1: U.S. sanctions

Not:

    sanctions instrument → sanctions intensity

Better:

    instrument_us_sanctions
    → ALTERS_WORLDSTATE
    → ws_inst_sanctions_legal_authority

    and
    → ALTERS_WORLDSTATE
    → ws_flow_effective_sanctions_pressure_iran

Then:

    ws_flow_effective_sanctions_pressure_iran
    → AMPLIFIES
    → ws_perc_iran_perceived_us_coercion

That is much cleaner.
Example 2: Iranian proxy strategy

    instrument_iran_proxy_support
    → ALTERS_WORLDSTATE
    → ws_struct_proxy_network_presence

    ws_struct_proxy_network_presence
    → SUPPORTS
    → ws_pos_iran_regional_depth

    ws_pos_iran_regional_depth
    → SUPPORTS_GOAL
    → goal_iran_non_coercion

That cleanly separates structure from position.
8. What this still does not solve

A few things remain open even with the taxonomy.

First, time is still weak. This taxonomy helps, but sequencing and lag are still not explicit.

Second, non-unitarity is still optional. The taxonomy works either way, but it does not decide when to split actors.

Third, belief nodes vs perceptual world variables now overlap a bit. That is manageable, but the distinction should be:

    Belief = stable model or proposition

    Perceptual variable = current perceived state

Example:

    belief: “the U.S. is coercive”

    perceptual variable: “current perceived U.S. coercion level is high”

That distinction should hold.
9. Tight revised rule set

Use this as the operating guide:

    Belief = how an actor interprets causal structure

    WorldState = what condition holds in the environment

    Perceptual WorldState = the actor’s estimate of that condition

    Structural variable = persistent environment

    Positional variable = actor-relative standing

    Flow variable = current intensity/pressure

    Institutional variable = formalized rule environment

That’s a stable foundation.
10. Recommendation

The next best move is to create a naming convention + variable template, so this becomes usable and doesn’t drift semantically.

For example:

ws_[type]_[domain]_[object]_[actor if needed]

Like:

    ws_flow_econ_effective_sanctions_pressure_iran

    ws_pos_sec_deterrence_balance_us_iran

    ws_perc_pol_perceived_us_coercion_iran

That will make the ontology much easier to scale.

Okay, proceed.

Good. A naming convention is what keeps this from turning into mush.
Variable naming convention + template
1. Naming convention

Use this pattern:

ws_[epistemic]_[ontology]_[domain]_[phenomenon]_[scope]_[actor?]

Where each slot is controlled.
2. Slot definitions
ws

Marks the node as a world-state variable.
epistemic

Whose version of the variable this is.

Allowed values:

    act = actual

    per = perceived

    dis = disputed

    lat = latent / inferred

ontology

What kind of variable it is.

Allowed values:

    str = structural

    pos = positional

    flo = flow

    ins = institutional

domain

What substantive area it belongs to.

Allowed values:

    sec = security

    econ = economic

    pol = political

    info = informational

    tech = technological / strategic

    norm = normative / legal

phenomenon

The thing being measured.

Examples:

    regional_alliance_architecture

    effective_sanctions_pressure

    deterrence_balance

    proxy_activity_level

    nuclear_latency

    perceived_us_coercion

scope

What relationship or arena it refers to.

Examples:

    middle_east

    us_iran

    iran_domestic

    gulf

    regional

actor

Optional. Use only when the variable is specifically centered on one actor.

Examples:

    iran

    us

3. Examples
Structural actual

ws_act_str_sec_regional_alliance_architecture_middle_east

Positional actual

ws_act_pos_sec_deterrence_balance_us_iran

Flow actual

ws_act_flo_econ_effective_sanctions_pressure_iran_domestic

Perceived flow

ws_per_flo_pol_perceived_us_coercion_us_iran_iran

That last one is ugly, which tells us something useful: we should refine perceptual naming.
4. Better rule for perceptual variables

For perceived variables, use:

ws_per_[observer]_[ontology]_[domain]_[phenomenon]_[scope]

So instead of:

ws_per_flo_pol_perceived_us_coercion_us_iran_iran

Use:

ws_per_iran_flo_pol_us_coercion_us_iran

Meaning:

    perceived by Iran

    flow variable

    political domain

    U.S. coercion

    in the U.S.–Iran relationship

This is much cleaner.
5. Final naming rules
Actual variables

ws_act_[ontology]_[domain]_[phenomenon]_[scope]

Perceived variables

ws_per_[observer]_[ontology]_[domain]_[phenomenon]_[scope]

Disputed variables

ws_dis_[ontology]_[domain]_[phenomenon]_[scope]

Latent variables

ws_lat_[ontology]_[domain]_[phenomenon]_[scope]

6. Variable template

Each variable should have a standard record.

{
  "var_id": "ws_act_flo_econ_effective_sanctions_pressure_iran_domestic",
  "label": "Effective sanctions pressure on Iran",
  "epistemic_status": "actual",
  "ontology_type": "flow",
  "domain": "economic",
  "phenomenon": "effective_sanctions_pressure",
  "scope": "iran_domestic",
  "observer": null,
  "target_actor": "actor_iran",
  "timescale": "short-medium",
  "unit_type": "ordinal",
  "description": "Net coercive economic pressure after accounting for evasion and adaptation.",
  "parent_variables": [
    "ws_act_ins_norm_formal_sanctions_regime_us_iran"
  ],
  "related_capabilities": [
    "cap_us_sanctions_architecture",
    "cap_iran_sanctions_evasion_channels"
  ]
}

7. Recommended metadata fields

Every variable should have:

    var_id

    label

    epistemic_status

    ontology_type

    domain

    phenomenon

    scope

Strongly recommended:

    observer

    target_actor

    timescale

    unit_type

    description

    parent_variables

    related_capabilities

Optional:

    measurement_method

    confidence

    notes

8. Unit types

Don’t force everything into numbers. Use a typed field.

Allowed values:

    binary

    ordinal

    categorical

    continuous

    probabilistic

    text-analytic

Examples:

    nuclear threshold status = binary or ordinal

    alliance cohesion = ordinal

    sanctions pressure = continuous or ordinal

    perceived coercion = ordinal

    war risk = probabilistic

9. Controlled vocab for phenomena

This matters a lot. Otherwise the graph drifts.
Security

    regional_alliance_architecture

    force_posture

    deterrence_balance

    escalation_risk

    territorial_control

    proxy_network_presence

Economic

    formal_sanctions_regime

    effective_sanctions_pressure

    trade_access

    oil_revenue_flow

    internal_economic_stress

Political

    internal_legitimacy

    elite_cohesion

    public_unrest

    diplomatic_engagement

    alliance_credibility

Informational

    attribution_confidence

    signaling_credibility

    narrative_dominance

    intelligence_visibility

Technological / strategic

    nuclear_latency

    missile_survivability

    command_resilience

    industrial_replenishment_capacity

Normative / institutional

    legal_authority

    method_legitimacy

    taboo_strength

    treaty_compliance_environment

10. Edge naming convention

Do the same thing for edges.

edge_[type]_[source]_[target]

Examples:

    edge_alters_instrument_us_sanctions_ws_act_flo_econ_effective_sanctions_pressure_iran_domestic

    edge_amplifies_ws_act_flo_econ_effective_sanctions_pressure_iran_domestic_belief_iran_intentions_us_coercive

This may feel verbose, but it is worth it.
11. Stress-tested examples for U.S.–Iran
Actual structural

ws_act_str_sec_proxy_network_presence_middle_east
ws_act_str_sec_regional_alliance_architecture_middle_east
ws_act_str_tech_nuclear_capability_distribution_middle_east

Actual positional

ws_act_pos_sec_deterrence_balance_us_iran
ws_act_pos_sec_regional_depth_iran_middle_east
ws_act_pos_tech_nuclear_latency_iran_us_iran
ws_act_pos_pol_alliance_credibility_us_middle_east

Actual flow

ws_act_flo_econ_effective_sanctions_pressure_iran_domestic
ws_act_flo_sec_proxy_activity_level_us_iran
ws_act_flo_sec_military_tension_level_us_iran
ws_act_flo_pol_diplomatic_engagement_level_us_iran
ws_act_flo_econ_internal_economic_stress_iran_domestic

Institutional

ws_act_ins_norm_sanctions_legal_authority_us_iran
ws_act_ins_norm_nonproliferation_commitment_environment_middle_east
ws_act_ins_sec_regional_security_commitments_middle_east

Perceived

ws_per_iran_flo_pol_us_coercion_us_iran
ws_per_us_str_sec_iran_revisionism_us_iran
ws_per_us_info_attribution_confidence_proxy_attacks_us_iran
ws_per_iran_pos_sec_deterrence_credibility_us_iran

12. What this naming system still doesn’t solve

Three things.

First, it still does not tell you when to create a new variable versus reuse an old one. That will require a modeling rulebook.

Second, some phenomena are composites. For example, regional_depth may itself depend on proxies, geography, political ties, and missile reach. So eventually you may want derived variables.

Third, perceived variables can get ugly because perception has both an observer and a target. The current convention is workable, but not elegant.
13. Recommended modeling rules

Use these rules to keep the graph sane.
Rule 1

Create a new variable only if it changes strategy, constraints, or evaluation.
Rule 2

Do not collapse formal and effective variables.
Rule 3

Do not collapse actual and perceived variables.
Rule 4

Prefer reusable generic phenomena names over case-specific ones.

Bad:

    iran_feels_boxed_in

Good:

    us_coercion

    perceived_us_coercion

Rule 5

Only create composite variables when the composite itself matters strategically.
14. Best next step

The strongest next move is to create a minimal codebook with:

    node types

    edge types

    variable naming rules

    15–20 canonical phenomena

That would make this system stable enough to use repeatedly.

After that, the natural move is a worked U.S.–Iran mini-codebook with maybe 25 nodes and 40 edges.

I'm just kind of inherently skeptical of, and I feel like it might bias the graph. I would be more amenable to linking concepts to, like, models or theoretical paradigms that justify them with various levels of epistemic reliability than just deciding, um, but that may be scope creep and overcomplicating.

That is not scope creep. It is probably a core correction.

Right now the graph risks implying:

    these nodes are real

    these edges are true

    this structure is “the world”

But at this level, a lot of it is really:

    model-dependent

    analyst-constructed

    contestable

    useful only under certain paradigms

So I think your instinct is right: the graph should not just contain claims about the world. It should contain claims as viewed through models.
Clean fix

Do not make the ontology fully realist by default. Instead add an epistemic wrapper:

    Observation / datum

    Interpretation / construct

    Model or paradigm

    Confidence / reliability

    Competing model links

So instead of saying:

belief_iran_us_coercive -> implies -> strategy_iran_forward_deterrence

you’d say something more like:

    under Model A (realist deterrence model), this edge is strong

    under Model B (bureaucratic politics model), this edge is weaker

    under Model C (ideological/constructivist model), this edge may mean something else entirely

That makes the graph less “this is true” and more “this is the structured output of a modeling choice.”
Minimal addition without blowing up scope

Add just two things:
1. Model

A node for the interpretive frame.
Examples:

    realism

    bureaucratic politics

    public choice

    constructivism

    crisis bargaining

    regime security model

2. Edge metadata

Every important edge gets:

    supported_by_model

    confidence

    evidence_type

    contestability

So your graph becomes:

    not a truth graph

    but a model-annotated analytic graph

Why this helps

It solves three problems:

First, it avoids false objectivity.
Second, it lets multiple interpretations coexist.
Third, it makes disagreement explicit instead of hidden.
The tradeoff

Yes, this adds complexity. But it is the right kind of complexity, because it sits above the graph rather than exploding the graph itself.

So I would not make every node model-relative. I’d do this selectively:

    keep nodes and core types simple

    annotate edges and variable constructions with model provenance

Best practical version

Use three epistemic levels:

    Observed: directly measured or documented

    Inferred: analytic construction with moderate contestability

    Theoretical: depends heavily on paradigm

That alone would go a long way.
Bottom line

I think you are right. The graph should not pretend to represent neutral reality. It should represent:

    a structured analytic interpretation of reality, with explicit model dependence and epistemic status

That is a real improvement, not a distraction.

The next clean step is to add a small epistemic annotation layer to the ontology.

Well, I guess there's maybe like two kinds of epistemics here. There is what is actually true and what, like, an actor believes to be true. Although I'm not sure whether we really need to separate them because we don't know what is true. We just have claims, and in general, there is data interpreted through models that leads to some belief. Although I'm not sure data always needs to go through a model, but maybe that is a cleaner way to do it.

Yes — and that suggests a cleaner move:

    Don’t make the ontology about “truth” vs “belief.”
    Make it about claims, with different provenance and different holders.

That is probably the right abstraction.
Cleaner epistemic stack

Instead of:

    actual truth

    actor belief

Use:
1. Data / signal

Raw or relatively raw inputs.
Examples:

    troop movements

    oil exports

    missile tests

    speeches

    sanctions text

These are not theory-free in a deep philosophical sense, but they are the closest thing to low-level inputs.
2. Model / interpretive frame

A mapping from data to meaning.
Examples:

    deterrence model

    bureaucratic politics model

    regime security model

    constructivist model

3. Claim

A proposition produced or supported by interpreting data through a model.
Examples:

    “Iran seeks deterrent depth”

    “U.S. sanctions are coercive rather than purely preventive”

    “Proxy activity increases escalation risk”

4. Belief

A claim attributed to an actor.
Examples:

    Iran believes the U.S. seeks coercion

    the U.S. believes Iran is revisionist

5. Analyst assessment

A claim the analyst tentatively endorses, with uncertainty.

So the key unit becomes:

    Claim = something asserted, supported, contested, or believed

That lets you avoid pretending you know “ground truth.”
Why this is better

It separates:

    what is observed

    how it is interpreted

    who believes it

    how strongly it is supported

That is much cleaner than “truth vs belief,” because at this level most of the graph is really about:

    interpreted signals

    rival claims

    attributed beliefs

Practical ontology addition

Add these node types:

    Datum

    Model

    Claim

And then beliefs become a relation, not a separate primitive if you want to simplify:

    ACTOR_ENDORSES_CLAIM

    MODEL_SUPPORTS_CLAIM

    DATUM_SUPPORTS_CLAIM

    CLAIM_CONTRADICTS_CLAIM

Then you can say:

    datum: missile buildup

    model: deterrence theory

    claim: Iran seeks forward deterrence

    actor endorses claim: U.S. intelligence community

    actor endorses claim: Iran leadership, maybe partially or implicitly

Even cleaner distinction

There are really two epistemic axes:
A. Provenance

Where does the claim come from?

    observation

    inference

    doctrine

    rhetoric

    model-based interpretation

B. Ownership

Who holds or advances the claim?

    actor

    analyst

    institution

    model tradition

That is better than truth/belief.
Minimal version if you want to stay tractable

You do not need to fully rebuild the ontology. Just add fields to relevant nodes and edges:

    claim_status: observed / inferred / theoretical / disputed

    held_by: actor / analyst / institution

    supported_by_data: yes/no/partial

    supported_by_model: model ids

    confidence

    contestability

That may be enough.
Tight recommendation

I’d define:

    Belief = a claim endorsed by an actor
    Assessment = a claim endorsed by the analyst
    Datum = an observation that may support or weaken claims
    Model = an interpretive schema that maps data into claims

That gives you a very clean epistemic architecture without requiring any commitment to ultimate truth.
Bottom line

Your ontology should probably not represent “the world as true.” It should represent:

    data, models, claims, and who endorses which claims

That is both more honest and more robust.

Yes — that is exactly the cleaner formulation.

You’ve found the right abstraction:

    Belief should usually not be a node type.
    It should be:
    Actor —[ENDORSES / BELIEVES / TREATS_AS_TRUE]→ Claim

That is much cleaner than making “belief” its own object in most cases.
Clean epistemic chain

What I’m thinking is basically:

Data → Model → Claim → Actor endorsement

So:

    Data = observations, signals, reports, measurements

    Model = interpretive frame that maps data into meaning

    Claim = proposition about the world, causality, intentions, etc.

    Actor endorsement = this actor believes or acts as if this claim is true

That means:

    a claim can exist without anyone endorsing it

    multiple actors can endorse the same claim

    different actors can endorse contradictory claims

    analysts can also endorse claims separately from actors

Why this is better

Because then you avoid duplication.

Instead of:

    belief_iran_us_coercive

    belief_analyst_us_coercive

    belief_us_iran_coercive

you just have one claim like:

    claim_us_is_coercive_toward_iran

and then:

    actor_iran — ENDORSES → claim_us_is_coercive_toward_iran

    analyst_x — ENDORSES → claim_us_is_coercive_toward_iran

    actor_us — REJECTS → claim_us_is_coercive_toward_iran

That is much better.
Revised ontology move
Nodes

    Actor

    Datum

    Model

    Claim

    plus your strategic nodes like Goal, Constraint, Capability, Strategy, Action, WorldState, Outcome

Edges

    SUPPORTS_CLAIM(Datum -> Claim)

    INTERPRETS(Model -> Datum) or better MODEL_SUPPORTS_CLAIM(Model -> Claim)

    ENDORSES(Actor -> Claim)

    REJECTS(Actor -> Claim)

    CONTESTS(Claim -> Claim) or CONTRADICTS(Claim -> Claim)

One subtlety

You may still occasionally want a “belief state” as a convenience object if you’re modeling bundles of claims held together by one actor. But that should be optional and secondary.

For example:

    Iran’s worldview could be represented as a set of endorsed claims, not a separate “belief node”

So default rule should be:

    Use claims as nodes. Use actor-to-claim edges for beliefs.

Example
Data

    missile tests

    sanctions actions

    proxy attacks

    speeches

Model

    deterrence model

    regime security model

Claim

    claim_us_seeks_to_coerce_iran

    claim_proxy_depth_improves_deterrence

    claim_sanctions_raise_internal_pressure

Endorsements

    actor_iran — ENDORSES → claim_us_seeks_to_coerce_iran

    actor_us — ENDORSES → claim_proxy_depth_improves_deterrence

    analyst — ENDORSES → claim_sanctions_raise_internal_pressure

That’s very clean.
Best compact formulation

You can define:

    Claim = a proposition

    Belief = a claim endorsed by an actor

    Assessment = a claim endorsed by an analyst

    Doctrine = a claim or bundle of claims institutionally endorsed

    Narrative = a claim strategically promoted regardless of sincerity

That gives you useful distinctions without adding too many primitives.
Bottom line

Yes:

    Data feeds models

    Models help generate or justify claims

    Actors attach to claims via endorsement edges

    Belief is not usually a standalone node

That is the cleaner version. The next step is probably to rewrite the ontology around Claim-centered epistemics.

At this point with the data model claim actor thing, I think we really need some kind of like entity relationship because a claim, and if I'm thinking about this right, is really like a, trying to think of what this would be, but I think it's, again, I'm guessing I'm missing what the reified node is, but it seems like, it's like for, maybe the reified node is a belief that has roles of data model claim and actor in roles.

Yes — you’re circling the key issue: once “claim” depends on who asserts it, from what data, through what model, with what confidence, and in what role, plain node-edge starts to get awkward. That usually means you want a reified assertion object.

The clean version is not “belief” exactly, but something like an Assertion or Assessment node. Then:

    Claim = the proposition itself

        example: “U.S. seeks to coerce Iran”

    Assertion / Assessment = an event or object saying

        actor X, using model Y, based on data Z, endorses claim C with confidence p in context t

So yes, the reified node is something like:

    Assertion

        has claim

        has asserter or holder

        has model

        has supporting_data

        has confidence

        has time

        optionally has purpose like sincere belief, doctrine, signaling, propaganda, analytic assessment

That solves the problem cleanly, because otherwise you get ugly many-to-many relationships with metadata hanging nowhere. In ER terms, Assertion is the associative entity joining Actor, Claim, Model, and Datum.

So I would revise it like this:

    Claim: proposition

    Actor: who may hold or reject propositions

    Model: interpretive frame

    Datum: evidence/input

    Assertion: reified object linking them

Then “belief” is just a subtype of assertion:

    Belief = actor internally endorses claim

    Assessment = analyst endorses claim

    Doctrine = institution officially endorses claim

    Narrative = actor publicly promotes claim, whether or not sincerely believed

That is probably the cleanest ontology turn you’ve made so far. The next step should be writing the ER schema explicitly with cardinalities.

Okay, let's see that.

Yes — here’s the clean ER-style schema.

The main move is:

    Claim is the proposition. Assertion is the reified relationship object.

That lets you attach:

    who is asserting

    what model they’re using

    what data supports it

    confidence

    time

    purpose

    whether it’s private belief, public narrative, doctrine, etc.

ER schema v1.0
Core entities
1. Actor

A person, state, institution, faction, network, or analyst that can hold, promote, reject, or act on claims.

Key fields:

    actor_id

    name

    actor_type

    scope

    coherence_level

Examples:

    U.S.

    Iran

    IRGC

    White House

    RAND analyst

    transnational Shia network

2. Claim

A proposition that can be endorsed, rejected, or contested.

Key fields:

    claim_id

    claim_text

    claim_type

    subject

    predicate

    object

    scope

    timescale

Examples:

    “The U.S. seeks to coerce Iran”

    “Proxy depth improves deterrence”

    “Sanctions increase internal economic stress”

You can store these either:

    as free text, or

    as structured triples/quads

3. Model

An interpretive frame or theoretical schema used to derive or justify claims.

Key fields:

    model_id

    name

    model_type

    description

    paradigm_family

Examples:

    deterrence theory

    bureaucratic politics

    regime security model

    constructivist identity model

    bargaining model

4. Datum

An observed or reported input that may support or weaken claims.

Key fields:

    datum_id

    datum_type

    description

    source

    timestamp

    reliability

    scope

Examples:

    missile test

    sanctions announcement

    proxy attack

    speech by leader

    troop movement

5. Assertion

This is the reified associative entity.

Definition:

    An instance of an actor relating to a claim, optionally through a model and based on data.

Key fields:

    assertion_id

    assertion_type

    confidence

    contestability

    timestamp

    visibility

    purpose

    notes

This is the key table/object.
Why Assertion is necessary

Because this is what you really care about:

    Actor A, using Model M, on the basis of Data D, endorses Claim C with confidence p at time t.

That is not just an edge. It is a first-class object.
Relationships and cardinalities
A. Actor ↔ Assertion

    Actor 1 —— N Assertion

Meaning:

    one actor can make many assertions

    each assertion has one primary asserting actor

Edge name:

    MAKES_ASSERTION
    or

    ASSERTED_BY

B. Assertion ↔ Claim

    Claim 1 —— N Assertion

Meaning:

    many assertions can concern the same claim

    each assertion points to one claim

Edge name:

    ASSERTS_CLAIM

C. Assertion ↔ Model

    Model 0..N —— 0..N Assertion

Meaning:

    an assertion may use zero, one, or several models

    a model may support many assertions

Practically, this is many-to-many.

So you may want a join table:

    AssertionModel

D. Assertion ↔ Datum

    Datum 0..N —— 0..N Assertion

Meaning:

    an assertion may be supported by many data points

    one datum may support many assertions

Again, many-to-many.

So you may want:

    AssertionDatum

E. Claim ↔ Claim

Self-relations:

    SUPPORTS

    CONTRADICTS

    REFINES

    DEPENDS_ON

This lets claims relate to other claims.
F. Actor ↔ Actor

Self-relations:

    PART_OF

    INFLUENCES

    COMPETES_WITH

    ALIASED_WITH

Useful for sub-actors and networks.
Minimal relational structure
Tables / entities
Actor

    actor_id

    name

    actor_type

    scope

    coherence_level

Claim

    claim_id

    claim_text

    claim_type

    subject

    predicate

    object

    scope

    timescale

Model

    model_id

    name

    model_type

    paradigm_family

    description

Datum

    datum_id

    datum_type

    description

    source

    timestamp

    reliability

Assertion

    assertion_id

    actor_id

    claim_id

    assertion_type

    confidence

    contestability

    timestamp

    visibility

    purpose

    notes

AssertionModel

    assertion_id

    model_id

    role

    weight

AssertionDatum

    assertion_id

    datum_id

    support_type

    weight

Assertion types

This is where “belief” gets recovered without becoming a primitive node.

Allowed values for assertion_type:

    belief

    assessment

    doctrine

    narrative

    hypothesis

    prediction

    attribution

    rejection

So:

    belief = actor privately or genuinely endorses claim

    assessment = analyst endorses claim

    doctrine = institution formally endorses claim

    narrative = actor promotes claim publicly

    rejection = actor explicitly denies claim

Example
Claim

claim_001

    “The U.S. seeks to coerce Iran”

Actor

actor_iran
Model

model_regime_security
Datum

    sanctions escalation

    U.S. military deployments

    official rhetoric

Assertion

assertion_001

    actor_id: actor_iran

    claim_id: claim_001

    assertion_type: belief

    confidence: 0.85

    visibility: private/public_mixed

    purpose: strategic worldview

    timestamp: t

AssertionModel

    assertion_001 uses model_regime_security

AssertionDatum

    assertion_001 supported by datum_sanctions_escalation

    assertion_001 supported by datum_force_posture

That is very clean.
ER diagram in text

Actor (1) --------< Assertion >-------- (1) Claim
                       |
                       |
                      / \
                     /   \
                    v     v
                 Model   Datum
                 (M:N)   (M:N)

And then optionally:

Claim -------- Claim
  |              |
supports / contradicts / depends_on

How this fits the larger ontology

This does not replace your strategic ontology. It slots into it.

    Actor stays

    Goal, Constraint, Capability, Strategy, Action, WorldState, Outcome stay

    epistemics get rebuilt around:

        Datum

        Model

        Claim

        Assertion

So instead of:

    Belief node

You now have:

    Claim node

    Assertion object linking actor to claim

Clean rule set

Use this:

    Claim = proposition

    Assertion = who says/believes what, using what basis

    Belief = assertion_type

    Datum = evidence-like input

    Model = interpretive frame

That is the cleanest formulation so far.
Main benefit

This solves your original problem:
you no longer have to pretend that claims are simply true or false at the ontology level.

Instead, you represent:

    propositions

    endorsements

    evidence

    models

    contestability

which is much more honest.

show this as a full schema with the strategic ontology integrated, or to
do a worked U.S.–Iran micro-example with 5–10 assertions.

Worked U.S.–Iran micro-example
1. Actors
actor_us

    type: state

actor_iran

    type: state/regime

actor_analyst

    type: analyst

2. Claims
claim_001

Text: The U.S. seeks to coerce Iran.

    type: intention claim

    subject: U.S.

    predicate: seeks_to_coerce

    object: Iran

claim_002

Text: Forward deterrence through regional proxies reduces Iran’s vulnerability to U.S. or Israeli pressure.

    type: causal-strategic claim

    subject: proxy_depth

    predicate: reduces

    object: Iran_vulnerability

claim_003

Text: U.S. sanctions increase economic pressure on Iran.

    type: causal claim

    subject: U.S. sanctions

    predicate: increases

    object: Iran economic pressure

claim_004

Text: Iranian proxy activity increases regional escalation risk.

    type: causal claim

    subject: Iranian_proxy_activity

    predicate: increases

    object: regional_escalation_risk

claim_005

Text: Sanctions and proxy deterrence interact in a reinforcing cycle.

    type: system-dynamics claim

    subject: sanctions_plus_proxy_deterrence

    predicate: creates

    object: reinforcing_feedback_loop

3. Models
model_001

Name: Regime security model
model_002

Name: Deterrence model
model_003

Name: Crisis bargaining / escalation model
4. Data
datum_001

    type: policy action

    description: U.S. sanctions measures targeting Iran

datum_002

    type: force posture / regional activity

    description: Iranian support to regional armed partners

datum_003

    type: rhetoric / doctrine

    description: Iranian official rhetoric emphasizing resistance to coercion

datum_004

    type: analyst observation

    description: Episodes of U.S.–Iran escalation linked to proxy activity and retaliation cycles

5. Assertions
assertion_001

    actor: actor_iran

    claim: claim_001

    assertion_type: belief

    confidence: 0.85

    visibility: public_mixed

    purpose: worldview / threat framing

Interpretation: Iran believes the U.S. seeks coercion.

Links

    uses model: model_001

    supported by data: datum_001, datum_003

assertion_002

    actor: actor_iran

    claim: claim_002

    assertion_type: belief

    confidence: 0.80

    visibility: mixed

    purpose: strategic doctrine

Interpretation: Iran believes proxy depth improves deterrence.

Links

    uses model: model_002

    supported by data: datum_002, datum_003

assertion_003

    actor: actor_us

    claim: claim_004

    assertion_type: belief

    confidence: 0.80

    visibility: mostly_public

    purpose: threat assessment

Interpretation: The U.S. believes Iranian proxy activity raises escalation risk.

    supported by data: datum_002, datum_004

    confidence: 0.90

Interpretation: The analyst assesses that sanctions increase pressure on Iran.

    claim: claim_005

    confidence: 0.70

Links
