    supported by data: datum_001, datum_002, datum_004

6. Strategic ontology links

Now connect these assertions to the rest of the system.
Goals

    actor_iran holds goal_iran_regime_survival

    actor_iran holds goal_iran_non_coercion

    actor_us holds goal_us_system_stability

    actor_us holds goal_us_protect_allies

Strategies

    strategy_iran_forward_deterrence

    strategy_us_coercive_pressure

    strategy_us_deterrence

Key links

    assertion_001 supports adoption of strategy_iran_forward_deterrence

    assertion_002 supports adoption of strategy_iran_forward_deterrence

    assertion_003 supports adoption of strategy_us_deterrence

    assertion_004 supports adoption of strategy_us_coercive_pressure

World-state variables

    ws_act_flo_econ_effective_sanctions_pressure_iran_domestic

    ws_act_str_sec_proxy_network_presence_middle_east

    ws_act_flo_sec_proxy_activity_level_us_iran

    ws_act_flo_sec_military_tension_level_us_iran

    ws_per_iran_flo_pol_us_coercion_us_iran

    ws_per_us_flo_sec_proxy_escalation_risk_us_iran

Key state links

    strategy_us_coercive_pressure alters ws_act_flo_econ_effective_sanctions_pressure_iran_domestic

    strategy_iran_forward_deterrence alters ws_act_str_sec_proxy_network_presence_middle_east

    ws_act_flo_econ_effective_sanctions_pressure_iran_domestic amplifies ws_per_iran_flo_pol_us_coercion_us_iran

    ws_act_str_sec_proxy_network_presence_middle_east and ws_act_flo_sec_proxy_activity_level_us_iran amplify ws_per_us_flo_sec_proxy_escalation_risk_us_iran

That produces the loop:

    U.S. pressure increases

    Iran perceives coercion more strongly

    Iran leans harder into forward deterrence

    proxy structure and activity increase

    U.S. perceives more escalation risk

    U.S. leans harder into pressure and deterrence

7. Why this example is useful

This shows the epistemic layer cleanly:

    Claim is not the same as belief

    the same claim can be endorsed by different actors for different reasons

    data does not directly create belief; it is filtered through a model

    the analyst can endorse a claim without pretending it is ontologically “the truth”

It also shows a nice division:

    claims explain why actors choose strategies

    strategies alter world-state variables

    world-state changes feed back into new assertions

8. Where this still feels awkward

Three things still stand out.

First, assertion_001 and assertion_002 are doing a lot of work that might really belong to a doctrinal bundle or worldview bundle, not isolated assertions.

Second, the line between datum and already-interpreted report is fuzzy. “Iranian support to regional armed partners” is not pure raw data; it is already somewhat processed.

Third, the relation between assertion and strategy is still underspecified. Right now I said “supports adoption of,” but that probably wants a typed relation like:

    JUSTIFIES_STRATEGY

    RATIONALIZES_STRATEGY

    PREDICTS_EFFECTIVENESS_OF

Those are importantly different.
9. Main gaps exposed

This micro-example suggests four likely upgrades:

    add Assertion → Strategy edge types with cleaner semantics

    maybe add Doctrine / Worldview bundles

    distinguish raw datum from report / interpreted observation

    decide whether claims should be stored as free text or structured triples

This actually looks pretty promising. The next best move is probably to define the Assertion-related edge types and cardinalities more precisely.

Yeah, I feel like we dropped all of the other layers we had talked about previously in this conversation, which was not really my intent. Do you remember the previous layers, and what is your thought?

Yes — and I think your reaction is correct. In the micro-example, we collapsed too hard into the epistemic layer and temporarily lost the rest of the stack.

The earlier layers we had built were roughly:

    Actor layer
    Who the relevant actors are, with optional sub-actors / networks when needed.

    Goal layer
    What they are optimizing for.

    Constraint layer
    What limits or conditions their feasible action.

    Capability layer
    What they can do, ideally tagged with MIDFIELD domains.

    Strategy layer
    The “ways” — containment, forward deterrence, sanctions evasion, etc.

    Instrument / action layer
    How strategy manifests operationally.

    World-state layer
    Structural, positional, flow, institutional, and perceptual variables.

    Outcome / dynamics layer
    Effects, feedback loops, escalation cycles, adaptation over time.

    Epistemic layer
    Data, models, claims, assertions, endorsement.

My view is that the epistemic layer should not replace the others. It should sit more like a cross-cutting annotation and justification layer on top of them. In other words, the ontology is still mainly about strategic interaction, and epistemics tells you how confidently and through what interpretive frame you are representing each node, edge, or variable. So the right integrated picture is not “Data → Model → Claim → Actor” by itself, but something more like:

Actor / Goals / Constraints / Capabilities / Strategy / WorldState / Outcomes
with an epistemic wrapper that says:

    what claims define these objects

    what data supports those claims

    what models justify them

    who endorses them

So I think the correction is: go back to the full multi-layer strategic ontology, and treat Claim/Assertion as the epistemic machinery for representing uncertainty and contestability within that larger system. The next move should probably be to redraw the full layered stack with epistemics integrated, not isolated.

I guess I'm not sure, but I feel like we may have moved in a different direction than I was initially thinking, where I think initially what we were discussing was like the, what I think was set us off was like, you can have a goal of economic maximization, but, you know, if one thinks the economy is a zero-sum game and one thinks it's, you know, a rising tide lifts all boats type of situation, then it's like, I think I was more trying to get to, like, how models of the world are kind of these interfaces between the goals and the strategies and capabilities and the world and like all of this stuff. And then maybe like data gets into the cybernetically, which we may not want to integrate until we've stabilized the other thing. But I think that's kind of where this came from, but what do you think is the most valuable to work?

Yes — I think that’s the better direction.

We drifted from models as mediating structures inside strategy toward a more general epistemic/knowledge-representation system. That latter move is interesting, but it is probably one layer too abstract too early for the problem you actually care about.

What seems most valuable now is to recenter on this:

    Goals do not map directly to strategies. They are mediated by models of how the world works.

So the core architecture should probably be:

    Actors

    Goals

    World models

    Constraints

    Capabilities

    Strategies

    World-state variables

    Outcomes / feedback

And the key relation is:

    World model = the interface that maps goals into strategies given constraints, capabilities, and perceived world state

That gets at your original zero-sum / positive-sum example perfectly:

    same goal: maximize economic welfare

    different world models:

        trade is positive-sum

        trade is zero-sum

    therefore different strategies:

        integration

        protectionism / coercion

So if I were choosing the most valuable next step, I would pause the claim/data/assertion machinery and define the World Model layer cleanly:

    what a world model is

    how it differs from goals, beliefs, and world-state variables

    how it mediates strategy choice

    how multiple competing world models can coexist inside one actor

Then later, once that stabilizes, we can decide whether:

    data updates world models directly

    or data supports claims that instantiate parts of a world model

My recommendation is:

    Next, define the World Model entity and its relations to goals, strategies, and world-state variables.

That feels like the highest-value move and most faithful to where your original intuition was going.

Yeah, I think that's right. And I think why initially we got into this epistemic conversation, and correct me if I'm wrong here, was that I didn't want to say, you know, this world model is right and this world model is wrong. It's more I just wanted to make this explicit into the modeling.

Yes — that’s exactly right.

The epistemic turn happened because you were resisting a hidden assumption that the framework could just say:

    this is the real world

    this actor has the wrong view of it

And your point was:

    No — different actors operate through different world models, and the framework should represent that explicitly rather than quietly privileging one model as “the truth.”

That is a very important correction.

So the cleaner architecture is not:

    Goal → Strategy

but:

    Goal → World Model → Strategy

more fully:

    Actor + Goals + World Model + Constraints + Capabilities + Perceived World State → Strategy

And then:

    the world model encodes assumptions like:

        zero-sum vs positive-sum

        deterrence works vs doesn’t work

        escalation is controllable vs not

        trade creates mutual gain vs dependence/vulnerability

    the framework does not need to decide up front which model is “correct”

    it just needs to represent:

        which model an actor appears to use

        how that model changes strategy choice

        where different actors’ models diverge

That is probably the highest-value thing to formalize next. The key move would be to define World Model cleanly as distinct from:

    Goal: what the actor wants

    Belief/claim: specific propositions the actor may endorse

    World state: conditions in the environment

    Strategy: chosen way of acting

A good compact definition would be:

    World Model = a structured set of assumptions about how actions, actors, and world conditions produce outcomes.

That gives you exactly the interface you were looking for, without forcing the ontology to declare one model objectively true.

How do you think this relates to strategic culture?

I’m tying this back to the architecture we just stabilized rather than reopening the whole epistemic branch. The key question is whether strategic culture is the same thing as a world model, or a different layer.

Pretty closely, but not identically. In the framework we were converging on, strategic culture is best understood as a higher-level, slower-moving source of an actor’s world models, plus some of its default goal-weightings and accepted methods. Johnston’s classic formulation treats strategic culture as an integrated set of symbols and assumptions about the role of war and the efficacy of force, while later work often treats it more broadly as a meaning-making context around strategic behavior. So in our stack, I would place it roughly like this: strategic culture → shapes world models, default claims, legitimacy rules, and perhaps some baseline goals → those then shape strategy.

So I would not equate strategic culture with a world model one-to-one. A world model is more like a specific operative map of causality: “trade is zero-sum,” “depth deters attack,” “escalation can be controlled.” Strategic culture is broader and stickier: it helps determine which such models feel natural, credible, or legitimate to elites and institutions in the first place. It also helps explain why two actors facing similar material conditions may choose different strategies, or why one actor keeps returning to the same family of strategies over time. That also connects to your non-unitarity point: strategic culture is often not perfectly uniform inside a state; analysts often talk about subcultures, bureaucratic cultures, and organizational mentalities rather than one single coherent national mindset.

The most useful way to encode it in your system is probably as a slow-changing latent layer that conditions, but does not mechanically determine, world models and strategy. So: Goals say what an actor wants; World models say how it thinks outcomes are produced; Strategic culture says which goals, models, instruments, and taboos are seen as normal, honorable, plausible, or legitimate over long periods. That makes strategic culture more like a deep prior than an immediate decision rule.

My assumption here is that this is not really definable enough to really incorporate at this point. And so I'm kind of assuming that world models affect strategic culture and that it's the kind of set of world models plus kind of like norms and stuff like that, which in a sense could also come down to like world models, not from a causal materialistic layer, but at a moral, ethical, spiritual layer, because these are kind of intertwined.

I think that’s a reasonable working assumption.

At this stage, I would not try to formalize strategic culture as its own clean primitive. It is probably too fuzzy, overloaded, and contested to carry much analytic weight unless you spend a lot of effort defining it. And if you do that now, it will likely blur the cleaner architecture you were converging on.

Your proposed move is stronger:

    treat “strategic culture” as an emergent bundle of
    world models + norms + legitimacy intuitions + taboo structures + identity-laden defaults

That seems right. In that framing, strategic culture is less a basic building block and more a compressed label for a cluster of persistent model- and norm-like structures.

So I’d treat it like this:

    World models = explicit or implicit assumptions about how the world works

    Norms / legitimacy / taboo structures = assumptions about what is acceptable, honorable, sacred, forbidden, etc.

    Strategic culture = the relatively durable pattern formed by those together over time

And yes, I agree with your deeper point: those “normative” parts can still be represented as world-model-like structures, just not purely material ones. For example:

    “Martyrdom increases legitimacy”

    “Compromise with X is dishonorable”

    “Trade creates dangerous dependence”

    “Resistance produces spiritual or civilizational strength”

Those are still models in the broad sense: they map actions and states of the world to valued consequences. They just operate partly in a moral / symbolic / spiritual causal register, not only a material one.

So for now, I’d probably do this:

    keep world model broad enough to include

        material causal assumptions

        social causal assumptions

        moral / symbolic assumptions

    keep norms/taboos as explicit if they matter

    treat strategic culture as an optional summary label over recurring patterns, not a core ontology node

That preserves tractability and avoids reifying a fuzzy term too early.

The cleanest compact formulation is:

    Strategic culture is not a primitive; it is a durable pattern in an actor’s world models, norms, and legitimacy structures.

I think that’s the right place to park it for now.

Yeah, let's leave world models out. Or sorry, I mean, let's leave strategic culture out. Um, but I guess now what I'm, I, I do think kind of this idea of norms, taboos, um, moral, ethical, spiritual, um, layers are important, but I'm wondering to what extent um they are goals and what extent they are um world models. I guess if you want like an ethical society is one of your goals and you have a world model that, um, you know, homosexuality is, um, your ethical world model that is wrong, maybe that is um kind of the layer where world models, um, encode the dynamics of things for which um you might care about, whether material or ethical or whatever.

Yes — I think that is the right way to cut it.

The clean distinction is:

    Goals are about what outcomes are valued

    World models are about how states of the world, actions, and institutions produce those valued outcomes

So in your example:

    Goal: have an ethical society

    World model: homosexuality undermines ethics, or alternatively does not undermine ethics, or improves social flourishing, etc.

That means the disagreement is not necessarily at the goal layer. Two actors can share the same broad goal — “ethical society” — while having radically different world models about what actually constitutes ethics and what causes or degrades it.

That also suggests “moral / ethical / spiritual” content does not need a separate primitive layer yet. It can often be represented as:

    Goal layer
    what is valued

        purity

        justice

        holiness

        dignity

        liberty

        flourishing

    World model layer
    what causes, sustains, threatens, or realizes those values

        martyrdom strengthens legitimacy

        compromise breeds corruption

        pluralism improves justice

        homosexuality degrades morality

        capitalism erodes solidarity

So the moral or spiritual layer enters in two places:

    as terminal values

    as causal theories about how those values are produced

That’s probably the cleanest formulation so far.

The one thing to watch is that some moral claims are not purely causal. Some are partly constitutive. For example:

    “X is unjust”
    is not always saying “X causes injustice”; sometimes it is saying “X itself is a violation of the valued order.”

So there are really two kinds of world-model-like moral content:

    causal moral models
    “X leads to corruption”

    constitutive moral models
    “X is corruption” or “X is sin”

You probably do not need a whole new layer for that yet, but it is worth noting. If needed later, world models could be split into:

    causal models

    constitutive models

So the compact answer is:

    Norms, taboos, and moral/spiritual ideas can often be decomposed into goals plus world models.
    Goals capture what is valued; world models capture what is believed to realize, threaten, or constitute those values.

That seems like the most tractable and expressive place to land.

On the causal moral models, first constituted moral models, I'm not sure how you could really clearly define these in ways for which these could be cleanly, like if separated. I guess this is maybe like a, I mean, this is probably overdoing it as far as how complicated I need to be able to say this, but like a structured equation model or like a Pearl causal network where there you have a moral framework. So it might be sin is bad, and then actually it's the corruption that actually it's the sin is not bad in and of itself, it's bad because of its tendency to cause the thing that is bad, which I think does kind of cleanly map onto our ideas of world models as the dynamics of causal dynamics changes in the goal layer. So if X is sin, and that's just a definition, and sin has some place in the goal layer, then it's just equivalent to sin, basically, so it's not really something we need to worry about, right? Other than like handling the support for sin.

Yes — I think that’s basically right, and it’s a good simplification.

If “sin is bad” is just part of the actor’s value structure, then you do not need a separate constitutive layer for it. You can treat it as:

    Goal/value layer: sin is disvalued, purity is valued, justice is valued, etc.

    World model layer: what causes sin, what sin causes, what prevents it, what institutions amplify or suppress it

Then a statement like “X is sin” is mostly a classification move: it maps some phenomenon into a goal-relevant category. After that, the rest can stay in the normal architecture:

    actor values avoiding sin

    actor classifies X as sin

    actor chooses strategies accordingly

So yes, a lot of what looked like “constitutive” content may just reduce to:

    a goal-category

    a mapping/classification rule

    possibly a causal model downstream of that

That is much cleaner than creating a whole extra primitive.

The only place you might still want a distinction is when the model needs to separate:

    X is bad because it causes bad outcomes
    from

    X is bad even if it causes no downstream bad outcomes

But even then, you can still keep it inside the same framework:

    in the first case, badness is mediated through causal links in the world model

    in the second case, badness is just directly attached in the goal/value layer

So the clean rule would be:

    If something is bad instrumentally, represent it in the world model.
    If something is bad intrinsically, represent it in the goal/value layer.
    If something is “sin,” “corruption,” or “purity,” often the main extra thing you need is a classification rule from observed phenomena into those valued/disvalued categories.

That means you probably do not need a separate constitutive-moral primitive right now. The framework can stay simpler:

    Goals/values

    World models

    optionally classification/mapping rules

I think that’s the right level of complexity.

Okay, I think maybe we should redo our glossary at this point and cleanly define these terms in a way that is useful.

Yes. We’ve changed enough that the old glossary is no longer clean. Here’s a tighter version built around the direction we actually settled on.
Glossary v2.0
Core principle

This framework is for describing how actors choose strategies without assuming one actor’s model of the world is objectively correct.

The central architecture is:

Actor + Goals/Values + World Models + Constraints + Capabilities + World State -> Strategies -> Instruments/Actions -> Outcomes
Actor

A decision-making unit being modeled.

An actor can be:

    a state

    a regime

    an institution

    a faction

    a network

    an analyst

An actor does not have to be perfectly unitary. It is treated as unitary only to the extent that doing so is analytically useful.
Goal

A valued end state or valued property of the world.

A goal answers:

    What does this actor want?

Examples:

    regime survival

    economic welfare

    regional influence

    justice

    purity

    liberty

    prestige

A goal is about what is valued, not about how it is achieved.
Value

A goal treated as intrinsically desirable or undesirable.

This is basically the deepest part of the goal layer.

Examples:

    sin is bad

    justice is good

    sovereignty is good

    humiliation is bad

Useful rule:

    if something is good or bad in itself, it belongs in the value/goal layer

    if it is good or bad because of what it causes, it belongs in the world-model layer

World Model

A structured set of assumptions about how actions, actors, and world conditions produce outcomes.

A world model answers:

    How does this actor think the world works?

Examples:

    trade is positive-sum

    trade is zero-sum

    proxy depth improves deterrence

    sanctions cause regime weakening

    pluralism increases justice

    compromise causes corruption

A world model does not say what is wanted. It says how outcomes are believed to arise.
Classification Rule

A rule that maps some observed or conceptual phenomenon into a goal-relevant category.

A classification rule answers:

    What kind of thing is this?

Examples:

    X counts as sin

    Y counts as aggression

    Z counts as coercion

    A counts as deterrence

This is useful when an actor’s strategy depends not just on causal beliefs, but on how it categorizes phenomena.
Constraint

A condition that limits what an actor can feasibly do or sustain.

A constraint answers:

    What restricts this actor’s options?

Examples:

    budget limits

    domestic unrest

    conventional military weakness

    alliance obligations

    sanctions pressure

    constitutional restrictions

A constraint is not the same as a goal. A constraint limits action whether or not it is desired.
Capability

A capacity the actor possesses that can be used strategically.

A capability answers:

    What can this actor do?

Capabilities are best tagged with MIDFIELD domains:

    Military

    Intelligence

    Diplomacy

    Finance

    Information

    Economic

    Legal

    Development

Examples:

    missile force

    sanctions architecture

    alliance coordination

    covert networks

    propaganda capacity

MIDFIELD

A taxonomy for categorizing capabilities and instruments of power.

MIDFIELD is not itself a theory of strategy. It is a way of organizing the means available to actors.

Some capabilities are cross-domain, so MIDFIELD should be treated as a practical classification scheme, not a perfectly exclusive ontology.
Strategy

A coherent way of pursuing goals given world models, constraints, capabilities, and perceived world conditions.

A strategy answers:

    How is the actor trying to get what it wants?

Examples:

    containment

    coercive pressure

    forward deterrence

    sanctions evasion

    balancing

    economic integration

A strategy is more general and durable than a single action.
Instrument

A concrete mode of applying capability in support of strategy.

An instrument answers:

    Through what operational mechanism is the strategy expressed?

Examples:

    sanctions package

    naval deployment

    proxy support

    diplomatic coalition-building

    public messaging campaign

Capability is what an actor has. Instrument is how it uses it.
Action

A specific act taken at a particular time.

An action answers:

    What did the actor actually do?

Examples:

    sanction bank X

    move carrier group Y

    transfer missiles to partner Z

    make speech A

Strategy is broader than action. Instrument sits between them.
World State

A strategically relevant condition of the environment.

A world state answers:

    What condition holds in the environment?

World-state variables can be typed as:
Structural

Persistent background conditions.

Examples:

    alliance architecture

    proxy network presence

    sanctions regime structure

Positional

Actor-relative standing within the environment.

Examples:

    regional depth

    deterrence balance

    alliance credibility

    nuclear latency

Flow

Fast-changing intensity or pressure variables.

Examples:

    sanctions pressure

    proxy activity level

    military tension

    economic stress

Institutional

Formalized rules, commitments, or legal environments.

Examples:

    treaty obligations

    legal sanctions authority

    inspection regime

Perceptual

An actor’s representation of a world-state condition.

Examples:

    perceived U.S. coercion

    perceived war risk

    perceived proxy attribution confidence

Outcome

A resulting effect of actions and changing world states.

An outcome answers:

    What resulted from what happened?

Examples:

    deterrence success

    escalation

    alliance strain

    economic pain

    regime stabilization

An outcome is not the same thing as a world-state variable, though outcomes often alter world states.
Feedback Loop

A recurrent causal pattern in which the effects of strategy alter later inputs into strategy.

A feedback loop answers:

    How do actions today change future beliefs, constraints, or world conditions?

Examples:

    sanctions -> perceived coercion -> forward deterrence -> proxy activity -> higher threat perception -> more sanctions

This is the beginning of the cybernetic layer, but it can be kept simple for now.
Claim

A proposition that can be endorsed, rejected, or contested.

A claim answers:

    What proposition is being asserted?

Examples:

    the U.S. seeks to coerce Iran

    proxy depth improves deterrence

    sanctions increase internal stress

Claims are not automatically true or false in the ontology. They are representable propositions.
Model

An interpretive framework used to relate observations and assumptions to claims.

A model answers:

    Through what theory or logic is this claim being interpreted?

Examples:

    deterrence model

    regime security model

    bureaucratic politics model

    crisis bargaining model

A model is broader than a single world model in some uses, but for our purposes the important point is that models mediate interpretation.
Datum

An observed, reported, or measured input used in support of interpretation.

A datum answers:

    What input is being used as evidence?

Examples:

    missile test

    sanctions announcement

    troop movement

    official speech

A datum is not pure truth. It is just the closest thing to an input signal.
Assertion

A reified object linking an actor to a claim, optionally through models and supported by data.

An assertion answers:

    Who is endorsing what, on what basis, and how strongly?

This is the key epistemic object.

An assertion can include:

    actor

    claim

    model(s)

    supporting data

    confidence

    visibility

    purpose

    timestamp

Belief

A claim endorsed by an actor.

Belief is not usually a separate node type. It is best represented as:
Actor -> endorses -> Claim
or as an Assertion with assertion_type = belief.
Assessment

A claim endorsed by an analyst.

Usually represented as an assertion with assertion_type = assessment.
Doctrine

A claim or bundle of claims institutionally endorsed.

Examples:

    formal strategic doctrine

    constitutional commitment

    alliance doctrine

Doctrine is usually more public and durable than a private belief.
Narrative

A claim publicly promoted for strategic, political, or legitimacy purposes.

A narrative may or may not reflect sincere belief.

This matters because actors can strategically advance claims they do not privately endorse.
Norm

A rule or standard about what is acceptable, legitimate, honorable, or forbidden.

A norm answers:

    What methods or conditions are considered acceptable?

Examples:

    nonproliferation

    sovereignty

    anti-coercion

    prohibition on certain methods

Norms can shape both goals and strategy selection.
Taboo

A strongly disfavored or prohibited category of action, often more rigid than an ordinary norm.

Examples:

    nuclear taboo

    taboo on certain forms of violence

    taboo on betrayal of sacred commitments

Taboos can be treated as strong norms unless finer distinction is needed.
Strategic Culture

A compressed label for a durable pattern of world models, norms, legitimacy intuitions, and default strategic tendencies.

We are not treating strategic culture as a core primitive in the ontology right now.

It is a summary concept, not a foundational node.
Perceived World State

The actor’s current representation of some condition in the environment.

This is distinct from:

    the analyst’s representation

    any assumed “actual” condition

This helps avoid forcing the ontology to decide what is objectively true.
Acceptance Set

The set of world states an actor is willing to accept given its constraints and world models.

This is different from ideal goals.

Example:

    an actor may ideally want total dominance

    but be willing to accept limited influence under constraints

Ideal Preference

The world states the actor would most prefer if unconstrained.

This is the cleanest representation of what the actor most wants.

Acceptance set and ideal preference should not be conflated.
Working distinction rules
Goals vs world models

    Goal: what is wanted

    World model: how outcomes are believed to arise

Values vs world models

    Value: intrinsically good or bad

    World model: instrumentally causal or classificatory

Capability vs instrument vs action

    Capability: what the actor has

    Instrument: how it applies it

    Action: what it specifically does

World state vs outcome

    World state: condition in the environment

    Outcome: effect produced by change

Claim vs belief

    Claim: proposition

    Belief: a claim endorsed by an actor

Datum vs claim

    Datum: input signal

    Claim: interpreted proposition

Compact architecture

The cleanest short version is:

    Actor: who is acting

    Goals/Values: what is wanted

    World Models: how the actor thinks outcomes are produced

    Constraints: what limits action

    Capabilities: what the actor can do

    Strategies: how it tries to pursue its goals

    Instruments/Actions: how strategy is operationalized

    World State: conditions in the environment

    Outcomes/Feedback: what results and feeds back

    Claims/Assertions/Data/Models: epistemic machinery for representing contested interpretation

If you want, the next best move is to
turn this glossary into a compact ontology table with one row per term: definition, excludes, examples, and relation to adjacent terms.

I'm wondering if strategy and also if capability constraints and instruments, I'm wondering if those should be really separated. Again, I don't have a strong opinion on that. Yes — this is exactly the right reaction. We have too many terms, and some of them are probably not earning their keep.

I think the best move is not to defend every distinction, but to collapse the ontology to a smaller v1 and only keep separations that do real work.
My recommendation: collapse to a lean v1
1. Goals vs values

I agree these should probably not be separate in v1.

The distinction is philosophically real:

    values = what is intrinsically good/bad

    goals = concrete desired end states

But for this framework, that distinction is usually not worth the complexity.

So for v1:

    Use one term: Goals
    and let it include both:

    abstract values like justice, purity, sovereignty

    concrete ends like regime survival, economic growth, regional influence

If later you need to distinguish intrinsic vs instrumental, you can add a field:

    goal_type = intrinsic | instrumental

But no separate Value node.
2. Capabilities vs constraints

I think these should remain separate.

Because they answer opposite questions:

    capability = what enables action

    constraint = what limits action

Even when the same underlying thing can look like both, the distinction is still useful.

Example:

    missile force = capability

    lack of air superiority = constraint

So I would keep them separate, but only as simple categories, not over-elaborate them.
3. Capability vs instrument vs strategy

These should also stay separate, but only if we define them tightly.

    Strategy = overall approach

    Capability = what resources/capacities are available

    Instrument = which capability channel is being used

Example:

    strategy: coercive pressure

    capability: sanctions architecture

    instrument: sanctions package

That said, if you want a leaner v1, I think the most defensible collapse is:

    Collapse instrument into strategy/action

So v1 could be:

    Strategy

    Action

and drop Instrument as its own primitive.

Then:

    capability enables action

    strategy organizes actions

That is probably simpler and good enough.
4. Strategy vs world model

These should stay separate. This is one of the most important distinctions.

    World model = how the actor thinks the world works

    Strategy = what the actor chooses to do given that model

Your original example depends on this distinction:

    same goal: maximize economic welfare

    different world models:

        trade is positive-sum

        trade is zero-sum

    different strategies:

        integration

        protectionism

If you collapse world model into strategy, you lose the exact thing that motivated this whole project.

So this one I would strongly keep.
5. Institutional as its own world-state type

I agree this is probably unnecessary in v1.

A treaty obligation can be represented as either:

    a constraint

    a goal-adjacent commitment

    or just a world-state variable if needed

So I would drop Institutional as a special world-state type for now.

Then world-state types become just:

    structural

    positional

    flow

    maybe perceived as a separate epistemic tag, not a type

That’s much cleaner.
6. Model vs world model

Yes, this is too confusing.

I think we should pick one.

Given where you wanted to go, I would keep:

    World Model

and drop the generic term Model from the strategic ontology.

If later we want an epistemic layer for analyst frameworks, we can add it back. But for now:

    World model = the actor’s assumptions about how the world works

That is enough.
7. Doctrine and narrative

I think these should be dropped from the core glossary.

They may be useful later, but they are not foundational.

For v1:

    if an actor publicly promotes a claim, that can just be an assertion

    if a claim is institutionally durable, that can just be tagged in metadata

No need for separate primitive terms yet.
8. Strategic culture

Agreed: leave out.
Lean v1 glossary structure

Here is what I think survives.
Core strategic terms

    Actor

    Goal

    World Model

    Constraint

    Capability

    Strategy

    Action

    World State

    Outcome

    Feedback Loop

Epistemic support terms

    Claim

    Datum

    Assertion

That’s it.
Lean v1 definitions
Actor

A decision-making unit.
Goal

A desired or disfavored end state or property of the world.
World Model

A structured set of assumptions about how actions and conditions produce outcomes.
Constraint

A condition that limits feasible or sustainable action.
Capability

A capacity the actor can use strategically.
Strategy

A coherent approach for pursuing goals given the actor’s world model, constraints, capabilities, and perceived world state.
Action

A specific move taken by the actor.
World State

A strategically relevant condition of the environment.

Types:

    structural

    positional

    flow

Optional epistemic tag:

    perceived by actor X

Outcome

A resulting effect of actions and changing world states.
Feedback Loop

A recurring causal pattern in which outputs affect later inputs.
Claim

A proposition about the world, causality, intentions, or effects.
Datum

An observed or reported input that may support or weaken claims.
Assertion

A reified object linking an actor to a claim, optionally with support and confidence.
My strong recommendations

Keep separate:

    goal / world model

    capability / constraint

    strategy / action

Collapse:

    values into goals

    instrument into action or strategy-action link

    institutional into world state or constraint

    model into world model

    doctrine, narrative, strategic culture out of v1

That gets you to something much more stable.

The next best step is probably to rewrite the glossary one more time as a lean v1 only, with just these 12 terms.

Yeah — I think you’re identifying two real issues:

    “Goal” may be overloaded

    “Capability” vs “instrument” vs “world model/model” still isn’t clean enough

So I’d tighten it like this.
1. Goals vs values

I think your critique is basically right. If we want the cleanest architecture, then what we earlier called “goals” should probably split into:

    Values = intrinsically desired ends

    Objectives = states of the world pursued because, under a world model, they are believed to realize those values

So:

    freedom, justice, holiness, happiness = values

    economic abundance, regime survival, regional influence = often objectives

Because, as you said, abundance is usually not terminal in itself; it matters because it is believed to produce something else that is valued.

That means “instrumental goals” are not really terminal goals. They are better treated as objectives. And strategy is then one layer further down: the way you pursue the objectives.

So the cleaner chain is:

Values -> World Model -> Objectives -> Strategy -> Action

That is better than collapsing everything into “goals.”
2. Capability vs instrument

Your saxophone example is good, and it suggests a clean distinction:

    Instrument = a tool, channel, or mechanism available in principle

    Capability = the actor’s usable competence or capacity to effectively employ instruments

    Strategy = the plan for using capabilities/instruments toward objectives

So:

    saxophone = instrument

    ability to play saxophone well = capability

    play jazz at the club tonight = strategy/action context

In geopolitics:

    sanctions law / financial plumbing / legal authorities = instruments

    the ability to coordinate and enforce sanctions effectively = capability

    coercive pressure campaign = strategy

So I think the distinction can be made cleanly after all:

    Instruments are tools; capabilities are effective usable power over tools.

3. Model vs world model

You’re right to push on this. I do not think we should keep both in the core framework.

The cleanest move is:

    keep World Model

    drop Model

And define World Model broadly enough to include both:

    causal assumptions

    interpretive assumptions

    moral/social assumptions

So:

    “trade is positive-sum”

    “proxy depth improves deterrence”

    “pluralism increases justice”

    “compromise causes corruption”

These are all world-model components.

The separate word “model” only becomes useful later if you want to talk about analyst frameworks or meta-models. But for v1, it is just confusing.

So I’d say:

    World Model is the only model term we keep in the core ontology.

4. What I think the leaner architecture now is

I think we should revise the stack to:

    Actor

    Values

    World Model

    Objectives

    Constraints

    Instruments

    Capabilities

    Strategy

    Action

    World State

    Outcome

    Feedback Loop

And epistemic support:

    Datum

    Claim

    Assertion

That feels much cleaner to me than the previous version.
5. Tight definitions

Here’s the cleaned-up distinction:

    Values: what is intrinsically good or bad

    World Model: how the actor thinks the world works

    Objectives: states of the world pursued because the world model says they realize values

    Instruments: tools or channels of influence

    Capabilities: effective ability to use instruments

    Strategy: plan for using capabilities/instruments to pursue objectives under constraints

That seems to match your intuition much better.

The next best move is probably to
rewrite the glossary one more time using Values / World Model / Objectives instead of the old goal language.

Yes — I think you’ve found the real issue: the arrow from values to world model is too strong or at least misleading.

I’d revise it.
Cleaner picture

Values and world models are mostly separate inputs.

    Values answer: what outcomes matter?

    World models answer: how do actions and world states lead to outcomes?

So your happiness example works like this:

    Value: happiness is good

    World model A: trade is positive-sum

    World model B: trade is zero-sum

The value does not generate the world model.
Rather, the same value, combined with different world models, produces different preferred objectives and strategies.

So the structure is more like:

Values + World Model + Current World State -> preferred objectives / acceptable world states -> strategy -> action

That’s much better.
On the anti-homosexuality example

You’re right that some formulations become tautological if we’re sloppy.

Bad version:

    value: anti-homosexuality

    world model: homosexuality is bad

That collapses everything.

Cleaner version:

    Value: ethical purity, obedience to divine law, social order, holiness, whatever the deeper terminal value is

    Classification rule: homosexuality counts as impurity/sin/violation

    World model: tolerating homosexuality causes corruption, disorder, divine punishment, weakened family structure, etc.

So:

    terminal value

    classification

    causal story

Those are different things, even if in practice they are entangled.
Objective as world state

Yes — I think this is right.

An objective is probably best understood as:

    a desired world state, or a desired change in world-state variables

So things like:

    higher economic output

    lower sanctions pressure

    greater regional depth

    less proxy activity

    more alliance credibility

are all basically world-state targets.

That means “objective” is not really a totally separate primitive. It is more like:

    World State + desirability relation

So you may be right that this wants reification.
Likely clean solution

Instead of treating Objective as a plain node, treat it as something like:
Objective

a reified object with fields like:

    actor

    target_world_state_variable

    desired_direction or target_value

    priority

    time_horizon

So for example:

    actor: U.S.

    target: proxy activity level

    desired direction: down

    priority: high

or

    actor: Iran

    target: regional depth

    desired direction: up

    priority: high

That’s much cleaner than treating “objective” as a free-floating concept.
Revised architecture

I’d now write it as:

Values

    World Models

    Perceived Current World State

    Constraints / Capabilities
    -> Objectives (reified desired changes in world state)
    -> Strategy
    -> Action
    -> World State / Outcomes
    -> feedback

Why this is better

It fixes exactly what was bothering you:

    values do not need to “cause” world models

    objectives are no longer vague middle-layer wishes

    objectives become actor-specific desired movements in world-state variables

    strategy becomes a response to a structured decision context, not a simple chain

On n-ary relations

Yes, I think this is one of those places where a plain graph starts to strain.

Because strategy choice is not really:

    value -> objective

    objective -> strategy

It is more like:

    Given
    values,
    world model,
    perceived world state,
    capabilities,
    constraints,
    the actor selects a strategy to move some target world-state variables in preferred directions.

That is naturally a reified decision context or n-ary relation.

So you might want something like a DecisionFrame or StrategicChoiceContext object later.

But I would not jump there yet unless you need it. For now, it may be enough to say:

    Objective = actor-specific desired world-state change

    strategy selection depends on multiple inputs, not one clean arrow chain

Tightest correction

So I’d revise the earlier chain from:

Values -> World Model -> Objectives -> Strategy

to:

Values + World Model + Perceived World State -> Objectives
Objectives + Constraints + Capabilities + World Model -> Strategy

That is much closer to what you seem to be reaching for.

And yes: objective is basically a desired world state or desired shift in world-state variables. That seems like the right way to think about it.

So, if you say ethical purity is a value, homosexuality is by definition impure, then there is no dynamic thing. It's just pathology that homosexuality is impure, and therefore fundamentally like value negative. It's saying that it causes corruption. It's only relevant if corruption, anti-corruption, is a positive value or corruption is a negative value. That makes sense, I'm not sure I'm thinking about this right.

Yes — I think that’s right, and it suggests an important cleanup.

You’re pointing to a distinction between:

    classification / typology

    dynamics / causality

And I agree that we were blurring them.

If:

    ethical purity is valued

    homosexuality is defined as impure

then you do not need a dynamic world-model statement like “homosexuality causes impurity.” That would be redundant or confused. In that case, the relevant structure is just:

    Value: purity is good

    Classification rule: homosexuality counts as impurity

That’s enough to make homosexuality disfavored, with no causal story required.

Then a separate statement like:

    “homosexuality causes corruption”

only matters if:

    corruption is itself disvalued, or

    corruption is believed to causally threaten something disvalued

So I think the clean split is:
1. Values

What is intrinsically good or bad.
2. Classification rules

What category something belongs to.

Examples:

    X counts as impurity

    Y counts as aggression

    Z counts as sovereignty violation

These are not dynamic.
3. World models

Causal or structural assumptions about how things lead to other things.

Examples:

    sanctions increase regime pressure

    trade creates mutual gains

    proxy depth improves deterrence

    corruption weakens social order

These are dynamic or at least relational in a causal/structural sense.

So I think you’re right that world models should probably be restricted to dynamic/causal/structural mappings, not general typology. Otherwise they become too broad and swallow everything.

That means the architecture likely becomes:

    Values

    Classification rules

    World models

    Constraints

    Capabilities

    Strategies

    Actions

    World states / outcomes

Where:

    classification tells you what a thing is

    world model tells you what a thing does or leads to

That feels much cleaner. The impurity example is then not a world model at all; it’s a classification rule attached to a value structure.

So yes, I think this is a real correction:

    Not all evaluative structure belongs in world models.
    Some of it belongs in classification rules that connect observed phenomena to valued or disvalued categories.


Okay, well, time for another glossary, do you think, or is there something else we can do?

Yes — I think it is time for another glossary, but a much smaller one.

What’s happened is useful: we’ve identified terms that were doing different jobs and getting conflated. The biggest gains were:

    world models should be about causal/structural dynamics

    classification rules should handle what category something counts as

    values should handle what is intrinsically good or bad

    objectives are probably best treated as desired world-state changes, not free-floating primitives

So I would not do a giant glossary rewrite again. I’d do a lean corrective glossary for just the terms that were unstable:

    value

    classification rule

    world model

    objective

    world state

    constraint

    capability

    strategy

    action

That said, there is one other high-value thing we could do first, which might be even better than a glossary:

    make a dependency map of which terms depend on which others

For example:

    values do not depend on world models

    classification rules connect phenomena to value-relevant categories

    world models connect actions/states to outcomes

    objectives depend on values + world models + perceived world state

That would likely surface remaining confusion faster than prose definitions alone.

My recommendation:

    do a mini dependency map

    then rewrite a lean glossary v3

That is probably the most efficient next step.

That is approved.

Good. Here’s the mini dependency map first.
Dependency map
1. Value

Independent primitive

Answers:

    what is intrinsically good or bad

Examples:

    happiness is good

    purity is good

    humiliation is bad

A value does not depend on a world model.
2. Classification rule

Depends on:

    value structure

    conceptual scheme / typology

Answers:

    what kind of thing is this?

Examples:

    X counts as impurity

    Y counts as coercion

    Z counts as aggression

A classification rule is not causal. It maps phenomena into categories.
3. World model

Independent primitive, though often culturally shaped.

Answers:

    how do actions and states lead to outcomes?

Examples:

    sanctions increase pressure

    trade is positive-sum

    proxy depth improves deterrence

A world model is about causal or structural dynamics, not typology.
4. World state

Input to decision-making.

Answers:

    what condition holds now?

Examples:

    sanctions pressure is high

    regional depth is low

    alliance credibility is medium

World state is neither value nor model. It is the condition being evaluated.
5. Objective

Depends on:

    values

    classification rules

    world models

    perceived world state

Answers:

    what change in the world does the actor want next?

Examples:

    reduce sanctions pressure

    increase regional depth

    decrease proxy attacks

    increase moral conformity

So yes, an objective is basically a desired world-state change.
6. Constraint

Independent input.

Answers:

    what limits feasible action?

Examples:

    low budget

    domestic unrest

    weak conventional military

    treaty obligations

Constraint is not the same as objective.
7. Instrument

Independent input.

Answers:

    what tool/channel exists?

Examples:

    sanctions mechanism

    missile force

    proxy network

    diplomacy channel

Instrument is the tool, not the skill in using it.
8. Capability

Depends on:

    instruments

    organization / competence / readiness

Answers:

    what can the actor effectively do with its tools?

Examples:

    ability to enforce sanctions

    ability to coordinate proxies

    ability to project force

So:

    instrument = saxophone

    capability = ability to play it

9. Strategy

Depends on:

    objectives

    world models

    constraints

    capabilities

    perceived world state

Answers:

    what overall approach should the actor use?

Examples:

    containment

    forward deterrence

    coercive pressure

    integration

10. Action

Depends on:

    strategy

    capabilities

    immediate conditions

Answers:

    what specific move is taken now?

Examples:

    sanction bank X

    deploy ship Y

    arm proxy Z

11. Outcome

Depends on:

    actions

    world state

    other actors’ actions

Answers:

    what resulted?

Examples:

    more deterrence

    more escalation

    more economic stress

12. Feedback loop

Depends on:

    outcomes feeding back into

        world state

        perceived world state

        objectives

        strategy

Answers:

    how do outputs become future inputs?

Clean flow

The cleanest version now is:

Values

    Classification Rules

    World Models

    Perceived World State
    -> Objectives

Then:

Objectives

    Constraints

    Instruments

    Capabilities

    World Models
    -> Strategy
    -> Action
    -> Outcome
    -> Feedback

That, I think, is much cleaner than where we were before.
Lean glossary v3
Value

An intrinsically desired or undesired property or end.
Classification rule

A non-causal rule that places some phenomenon into a value-relevant category.
World model

A causal or structural account of how actions and states produce outcomes.
World state

A condition of the environment relevant to strategy.
Objective

A desired change in world-state variables, given values, classification rules, world models, and perceived conditions.
Constraint

A condition that limits feasible or sustainable action.
Instrument

A tool, channel, or mechanism available for influence.
Capability

The effective ability to use instruments.
Strategy

A coherent approach for pursuing objectives under constraints using capabilities, given a world model.
Action

A specific move taken by an actor.
Outcome

A resulting effect of actions and changing world states.
Feedback loop

A recurring causal pattern in which outcomes affect later inputs into decision-making.
What I think is still most fragile

The weakest terms are probably:

    classification rule

    objective

    maybe instrument vs capability

But they now feel much cleaner than before.

one moral/social, one geopolitical.

I guess I'm not really sure what the world state, if it, and maybe we do need this, but to say that the world state is the condition that holds now, I guess I was thinking about the world state, actually, I think that would be bad terminology now that I'm saying this, but I was thinking about world state is kind of like the configuration space of world state, but you're probably right. And then I guess objective is kind of a point or a state within that configuration space. And then I'm wondering whether feedback loop is really deprimitive and not something more like stocks and flows. Yeah, what do you think about that? I don't have a strong opinion on that yet.

Yes, I think that’s a good correction.

“World state” is probably better used to mean the current realized state, while the larger thing you were reaching for is more like the state space or configuration space: the set of possible world states. So the clean distinction would be:

    State space: all possible configurations

    World state: one actual or perceived point in that space

    Objective: a desired point, region, or direction of movement in that space

That is cleaner and more standard. And yes, an objective is often not just a single point, but a target region or directional preference like “less sanctions pressure,” “more regional depth,” “lower escalation risk.”

On feedback loop, I agree it may not deserve primitive status. It is probably better treated as a pattern that emerges from the system of variables and causal relations, rather than a base term. If you later go more cybernetic, then stocks and flows may be the better primitive distinction: stocks are accumulated states or capacities, while flows are rates of change into or out of them. In that setup, a feedback loop is something you detect in how stocks and flows connect. So for now I’d probably demote “feedback loop” from the glossary and keep:

    state space

    world state

    maybe stock

    maybe flow

Then loops are derived, not primitive.

Okay, so the one, and this is maybe not worth doing, but in the same way, we move back from saying here are the true models and here are false models to say that this is the world state right now is also an unknowable thing, right? It's like, does Iran have, you know, are they a month away from having a nuclear bomb? Like that's impossible to know for me. So, yeah, I'm just not sure, like, it's not really a world state, it's kind of, yeah, what do you think about that?

Yes — I think that’s right, and it suggests the same move we made with world models:

    don’t treat “world state” as privileged ground truth in the framework.

A better formulation is probably:

    State space = set of possible configurations

    State estimate = a representation of where the world is in that space

    Actor state estimate = where a given actor thinks the world is

    maybe analyst state estimate = where the modeler thinks it is

So instead of saying:

    “Iran is one month from a bomb” as world state

you’d say:

    this is a claim about the state

    held with some confidence

    by some actor or analyst

    possibly under some model

That means the decision architecture becomes something like:

Values + Classification Rules + World Models + State Estimate -> Objectives -> Strategy

not

Values + World Models + True World State -> Strategy

I think that is much cleaner and more honest.

So I’d revise the terminology like this:

    State space: all possible world configurations

    State estimate: an attributed estimate of current location in that space

    Objective: desired point, region, or direction in that space

Then:

    “world state” can either be dropped

    or reserved for shorthand when precision is not needed

If you want the strictest version, I would probably replace world state in the glossary with state estimate and keep state space as the broader object. Then you can always say:

    actor A’s state estimate

    actor B’s state estimate

    analyst’s state estimate

That seems like the right correction.

Okay, what next, do you think? Another glossary, or is there, what do you think next?

I think the next best move is not another glossary yet.

We’ve changed the primitives enough times that another glossary right now would likely just freeze confusion rather than resolve it. The highest-value next step is to make a small dependency / architecture diagram with the updated terms only, and use that to see what still feels wrong.

Specifically, I’d now lock a candidate v1 core set and test it:

    Actor

    Values

    Classification Rules

    World Models

    State Space

    State Estimate

    Objectives

    Constraints

    Instruments

    Capabilities

    Strategy

    Action

    Outcome

Then write the smallest possible dependency map, something like:

    values + classification rules + world models + state estimate -> objectives

    objectives + constraints + instruments + capabilities + world models + state estimate -> strategy

    strategy -> action

    action -> outcome

    outcome -> updated state estimate

That will surface the remaining problems faster than another prose glossary. After that, do a very short glossary v4 only for the terms that survive. So my recommendation is: dependency diagram first, glossary second.

Candidate v1 architecture
Core set

    Actor

    Values

    State Space

1. Dependency structure
A. What the actor wants next

Values

    Classification Rules

    World Models

    State Estimate
    → Objectives

Interpretation:

    Values say what matters

    Classification Rules say what counts as what

    World Models say how things lead to other things

    State Estimate says where the actor thinks things are now

    Objectives are then desired movements in state space

B. How the actor tries to get it

Objectives

    Constraints

    Instruments

    Capabilities

    State Estimate
    → Strategy

Interpretation:

    same objective can produce different strategies under different models or constraints

    same instruments can produce different strategies depending on capability

C. What actually happens

Strategy
→ Action
→ Outcome
D. Learning / updating

Outcome
→ State Estimate

    Outcome may also update world models, but that is probably v2

3. More explicit version

Actor
 ├─ holds Values
 ├─ uses Classification Rules
 ├─ uses World Models
 ├─ has State Estimate
 ├─ faces Constraints
 ├─ has access to Instruments
 ├─ has Capabilities
 └─ selects Strategy

State Space
 └─ defines the possible configurations over which
    State Estimates and Objectives are expressed

Objectives
 └─ are desired directions / regions / targets in State Space

Strategy
 └─ organizes Actions

Actions
 └─ produce Outcomes

Outcomes
 └─ update State Estimates

4. What already looks better

This fixes a few earlier problems:

    no privileged “true world state”

    world models are clearly separate from values

    objective is no longer mystical; it is a target in state space

    feedback loop is derived from outcome updating state estimate, not a primitive

    strategic culture stays out

5. What still looks fragile

These are the parts I’d watch.
A. Classification Rules

This still feels a little unstable. It may survive, but it is the most likely term to collapse into either:

    values

    or world models

B. Instruments vs Capabilities

This distinction is cleaner than before, but still a likely stress point.
C. Outcome

Outcome may just be:

    realized state transition

    or observed consequence

We may later want to collapse outcome into updated state estimate / state transition.
D. State Estimate

This is good philosophically, but it may get cumbersome unless we’re disciplined.
6. My current best guess

I think the most likely stable v1 is:

    Actor

    Values

    World Models

    State Space

    State Estimate

    Objectives

    Constraints

    Instruments

    Capabilities

    Strategy

    Action

And maybe:

    drop Outcome as primitive

    treat it as the observed result of action and updated state estimate

And maybe:

    keep Classification Rules only if it proves useful in examples

7. Best next move

I think the best next step is to stress-test this dependency map with two tiny examples:

    economic welfare / zero-sum vs positive-sum

    moral purity / homosexuality or similar classification-heavy case

That will tell us quickly whether:

    classification rules survive

    outcome survives

    instruments vs capabilities survives

That is probably more informative than another glossary right now.

Okay, proceed.

Good. These two examples should tell us which terms are actually doing work.
Stress test 1: economic welfare, zero-sum vs positive-sum
Setup

Two actors:

    Actor A

    Actor B

Both have the same value:

    prosperity / welfare is good

They differ in world model:

    WM1: trade is positive-sum

    WM2: trade is zero-sum

They share roughly similar:

    state estimate: both are economically middling

    instruments: tariffs, trade agreements, investment policy

    capabilities: moderate industrial and trade capacity

    constraints: domestic politics, budget, rivals

Dependency walk
Values

    welfare is good

World models

    A uses WM1

    B uses WM2

State estimate

    current growth is weak

    foreign competition is rising

Objectives

Given the same value and similar state estimate, but different world models:

    A’s objective:

        increase trade access

        increase cross-border investment

        stabilize interdependence

    B’s objective:

        reduce dependence

        increase domestic share

        weaken rival leverage

So the objective is not just “more wealth.”
It becomes a different target in state space because of the world model.
Strategy

    A chooses integration

    B chooses protectionism / industrial rivalry

Action

    A signs trade agreements

    B raises tariffs and subsidizes domestic industry

What this tests
What survives

    Values clearly survive

    World models clearly survive

    State estimate survives

    Objectives survive and are useful

    Strategy survives

What looks weaker

    Classification rules do almost no work here

    Instruments vs capabilities matter a bit, but not centrally

    Outcome still looks optional

Result

This strongly supports:

    values

    world models

    state estimate

    objectives

    strategy

It weakens:

    classification rules as universal primitive

Stress test 2: moral purity case
Setup

One actor:

    Actor C

Value

    purity is good

Candidate issue

The actor regards homosexuality as impure.

The question is: does this require:

    a world model

    or only a classification rule?

Version A: purely classificatory
Values

    purity is good

Classification rule

    homosexuality counts as impurity

State estimate

    level of visible homosexuality is rising

Objective

    reduce impurity in society

Strategy

    censorship, punishment, exclusion, moral policing

This works without any world model beyond the basic setup.
Version B: causal extension

Now add:

    tolerance of homosexuality causes broader moral corruption

    moral corruption weakens social order

Now we have a world model.
Values

    purity is good

    disorder is bad, maybe

Classification rule

    homosexuality counts as impurity

World model

    tolerance -> corruption -> disorder

Objective

    reduce homosexuality

    prevent social disorder

Strategy

    repression

    propaganda

    institutional enforcement

What this tests
What survives

    Classification rule clearly does real work here

    Values survive

    State estimate survives

    Objectives survive

What becomes conditional

    World model is not always needed

        only needed if there is a causal story

Result

This suggests:

    classification rules are not universal, but they are important in some domains

    world models should be restricted to causal/structural assumptions, not typology

What these two examples tell us
Strong survivors

These seem worth keeping in v1:

    Actor

    Values

    World Models

    State Space

    State Estimate

    Objectives

    Constraints

    Strategy

    Action

Conditional survivors

These may stay, but not as always-needed:

    Classification Rules

        important in moral / legal / ideological cases

        less important in many material cases

    Instruments

        useful, but maybe collapsible later

    Capabilities

        useful, especially when competence differs from tool possession

Weakest primitive

    Outcome

        still looks like it may be better treated as:

            state transition

            or updated state estimate
            rather than a core primitive

My recommendation after the stress tests

I would now define the likely v1 core as:
Core

    Actor

    Values

    World Models

    State Space

    State Estimate

    Objectives

    Constraints

    Instruments

    Capabilities

    Strategy

    Action

Optional / situational

    Classification Rules

Probably derived, not primitive

    Outcome

    Feedback loop

That feels much tighter.
Best next move

Now I think it is time for a lean glossary v4 with only:

    core terms

    optional terms

    derived terms

That glossary should be much more stable than the earlier ones.

Yeah, I mean, I don't think the criteria for inclusion is whether it has to be used in every example. It's rather for reasonable examples, can you reasonably express them without these? I mean, I think we still need classification rules in some sense, or I guess you could get rid of it as long as you proliferate the number of things in the layer above. So it's like, you could say sin is bad and have a classification rule for all the things that are sin, or you could promote all of those things that are sins to the value layer. I'm actually not sure which of those results in a cleaner model. I guess if, even though you have five things for sin, like corruption and homosexuality, but you had a world model that, you know, said something increases corruption, but not necessarily homosexuality, then you kind of want the corruption to be an individual node.
