Yes, I agree. “Used in every example” is too strict a test. The better test is:

    Does the term let us represent important classes of cases cleanly, without distorting the model or exploding another layer?

By that standard, I think classification rules probably do survive, at least as an optional but legitimate part of the architecture. Your corruption example is exactly why: if you collapse everything into the value layer, you lose the ability to say:

    purity is good

    corruption is bad

    homosexuality counts as impurity

    some other thing causes corruption
    without turning the value layer into a junk drawer of terminal values, categories, and labels.

So I think the cleaner approach is:

    Values = what is intrinsically good/bad

    Classification rules = what counts as what

    World models = what causes what

That keeps “corruption” available as a node that can be:

    intrinsically bad, if that’s how the actor sees it

    caused by certain things, under a world model

    distinct from “homosexuality,” which may or may not be classified as corruption or impurity

And yes, I agree the best next move is another worked example, especially the U.S.–Iran case, because that’s where a lot of these distinctions first mattered.
What I’d do in that stress test

Take a compact U.S.–Iran case and explicitly fill in:

    Values

    Classification rules

    World models

    State estimate

    Objectives

    Constraints

    Instruments

    Capabilities

    Strategy

    Action

Then ask, term by term:

    what feels redundant?

    what feels missing?

    what gets forced unnaturally?

That should tell us much more than abstract debate.
My current expectation before doing it

I suspect:

    classification rules will survive

    outcome will probably collapse into state transition / updated state estimate

    instrument vs capability will survive but remain a frequent stress point

    state estimate will prove useful but cumbersome

So yes — I think the right next step is:

    run a fourth U.S.–Iran stress test using the current candidate architecture, explicitly including classification rules


Okay, proceed.

I’m going to keep this one compact and use the U.S.–Iran case mainly as an ontology stress test, not as a factual brief on current events. The goal is to see which terms actually carry weight and which ones feel forced.
U.S.–Iran stress test v4

Using the current candidate architecture:

    Actor

    Values

    Classification Rules

    World Models

    State Space

    State Estimate

    Objectives

    Constraints

    Instruments

    Capabilities

    Strategy

    Action

And treating:

    outcome as mostly a state transition / updated state estimate

    feedback as derived, not primitive

1. Actor layer
Actor A: U.S.
Actor B: Iran

This is already a simplification, but good enough for the stress test.
Immediate issue

The actor layer is still too unitary, but not fatally so for a first pass.
2. Values
U.S. candidate values

    security of self and allies is good

    regional/system stability is good

    nuclear proliferation is bad

    low-cost order is good

Iran candidate values

    regime survival is good

    non-subordination / sovereignty is good

    ideological continuity is good

    regional influence or dignity is good

Stress-test question

Do these look like values, or are some of them actually objectives?
Problem

“Regime survival” and “regional influence” feel partly instrumental, not purely terminal.
Likewise “low-cost order” for the U.S. feels more like an objective than a deep value.
Provisional judgment

The value layer still wants compression toward more intrinsic terms, like:

    security

    sovereignty

    ideological integrity

    prestige/dignity

    stability/order

Then:

    regime survival becomes an objective under a world model

    regional influence becomes an objective under a world model

So this stress test suggests the value/objective distinction is real and useful.
3. Classification rules
Possible U.S. classification rules

    proxy warfare counts as destabilizing aggression

    nuclear latency counts as proliferation risk

    partner security commitments count as credibility tests

Possible Iran classification rules

    sanctions count as coercion

    foreign pressure counts as domination attempt

    allied proxy depth counts as deterrent depth rather than mere militancy

What this shows

Classification rules do real work here.

Without them, we would have to bake a lot of contested meanings directly into:

    values

    or world models

That would be messier.
Stress-test result

Classification rules survive.
4. World models
U.S. candidate world models

    proxy activity increases escalation risk

    sanctions increase pressure on Iran

    pressure can reduce hostile capability growth

    unchecked regional influence compounds long-run threat

Iran candidate world models

    forward deterrence reduces vulnerability

    U.S. pressure tends toward coercion or subordination

    conventional weakness requires asymmetric methods

    sanctions resistance improves survival

What this shows

This layer clearly survives and is central.

This was the original motivation for the framework:
same or similar broad values can yield different strategies because of different world models.
Stress-test result

World models are indispensable.
5. State space and state estimate
State space

Possible configurations involving:

    sanctions pressure

    proxy network presence

    regional influence

    deterrence balance

    alliance credibility

    nuclear latency

    escalation risk

U.S. state estimate

    Iranian proxy network is substantial

    escalation risk is elevated

    Iranian regional influence is too high

    sanctions still exert meaningful pressure

Iran state estimate

    U.S. coercive pressure is high

    conventional vulnerability is high

    regional deterrent depth is necessary

    sanctions pain is serious but survivable

What this shows

This layer is useful, especially because:

    it avoids claiming a privileged “true state”

    it lets different actors occupy different estimated locations in the same state space

Stress-test result

State space and state estimate survive.
6. Objectives

Now define objectives as desired movements in state space.
U.S. objectives

    reduce proxy activity

    reduce Iranian nuclear latency

    reduce Iranian regional leverage

    maintain or raise alliance credibility

    reduce risk of major war

Iran objectives

    reduce vulnerability to coercion

    increase deterrent depth

    reduce effective sanctions pressure

    preserve ideological-regime continuity

    avoid direct war while preserving leverage

What this shows

This layer works well if treated as:

    actor-relative

    directional

    state-space-targeted

It works much better than treating objectives as vague nouns.
Stress-test result

Objectives survive strongly.
7. Constraints
U.S. constraints

    avoid large regional war

    domestic tolerance for sustained costs is limited

    alliance commitments constrain flexibility

Iran constraints

    conventional military weakness

    sanctions pressure

    internal fragility / legitimacy pressures

    risk of direct overwhelming attack

What this shows

Constraint is clearly distinct from:

    values

    objectives

    capabilities

This layer survives easily.
8. Instruments
U.S. instruments

    sanctions machinery

    alliance diplomacy

    force posture

    intelligence / covert channels

Iran instruments

    proxy support channels

    missile signaling

    sanctions-evasion channels

    ideological messaging

What this shows

The instrument layer is somewhat useful, but this is still where things feel least stable.

Because “proxy support channels” could be:

    instrument

    capability

    or even part of strategy, depending on framing

Likewise “sanctions machinery” could be:

    instrument

    or capability substrate

Stress-test result

Instrument is useful, but still the shakiest layer besides maybe value.
9. Capabilities
U.S. capabilities

    ability to enforce sanctions

    ability to coordinate allies

    ability to project force

    ability to gather and process intelligence

Iran capabilities

    ability to sustain proxy relationships

    ability to impose regional costs asymmetrically

    ability to absorb and route around sanctions

    ability to maintain internal control

What this shows

Capability becomes clearer if defined as:

    effective usable competence
    rather than mere possession of tools

This helps the saxophone distinction:

    sanctions law is not yet sanctions capability

    a proxy network on paper is not yet real deterrent capability

Stress-test result

Capabilities survive.
10. Strategy
U.S. strategies

    containment

    coercive pressure

    deterrence without full war

Iran strategies

    forward deterrence

    asymmetric retaliation

    sanctions resistance / endurance

What this shows

Strategy is clearly distinct from world model.

For example:

    world model: proxy depth deters attack

    strategy: build and rely on forward deterrence

That distinction remains valuable.
Stress-test result

Strategy survives strongly.
11. Action
U.S. actions

    sanction specific entities

    move assets

    coordinate diplomatic pressure

    conduct selective strikes or interdictions

Iran actions

    fund or arm proxies

    missile demonstrations

    reroute commerce and financing

    rhetorical signaling

Stress-test result

Action survives easily.
What broke, what held, what remains unclear
Strong survivors

These feel clearly worth keeping:

    World Models

    State Space

    State Estimate

    Objectives

    Constraints

    Capabilities

    Strategy

    Action

Likely survivors

    Classification Rules

    Values

Most fragile

    Instruments

Main lessons from this stress test
1. Classification rules are real

This example needs them.

Because a lot of U.S.–Iran conflict is partly about what actions count as:

    deterrence

    coercion

    aggression

    proliferation

    credibility loss

Without classification rules, those judgments get hidden inside world models or values.
2. Values still need tightening

The value layer still tends to drift upward into semi-objectives.

For example:

    regime survival

    alliance credibility

    regional influence

often feel better treated as objectives derived from deeper values plus world models.

So values survive, but their definition should be strict.
3. Instrument remains the least stable term

The hardest distinction is still:

    instrument vs capability

Because in practice these are deeply intertwined:

    a proxy network can be a tool, a capacity, and part of a strategy all at once

This may be where v1 eventually either:

    keeps both with strict definitions

    or collapses instrument into capability or action-supporting mechanism

4. Outcome can still stay derived

Nothing in this example really forces outcome to be primitive.
The framework still works if outcome is treated as:

    realized consequences

    state transition

    updated state estimate

My current best candidate v1 after this test
Core

    Actor

    Values

    Classification Rules

    World Models

    State Space

    State Estimate

    Objectives

    Constraints

    Capabilities

    Strategy

    Action

Optional / possibly collapsible

    Instruments

Derived

    Outcome

    Feedback loop

Best next move

I think the highest-value next step is not another full glossary yet.

It is to do one targeted thing:

    Resolve instrument vs capability with a dedicated mini stress test and decision rule.

Because that is now the biggest remaining ambiguity.

do that next with 3–4 examples:

    sanctions

    proxy networks

    missiles

    diplomacy


Yes, proceed.

Good. This is the main ambiguity left, so a focused test is the right move.
Instrument vs capability mini stress test
Proposed definitions
Instrument

A tool, channel, mechanism, or asset through which influence can be exerted.

Question it answers:

    What means exists to act through?

Capability

The effective ability to use one or more instruments to produce intended effects.

Question it answers:

    What can the actor actually do with the means available?

That suggests:

    instrument = means substrate

    capability = usable competence / effective power

Test 1: sanctions
Candidate objects

    sanctions law

    Treasury access

    SWIFT leverage

    compliance networks

    designation process

    ability to coordinate allies on sanctions

Clean cut
Instruments

    sanctions authorities

    financial chokepoints

    designation mechanisms

    enforcement channels

Capability

    ability to impose effective sanctions pressure on the target

Why this seems right

The U.S. can possess sanctioning instruments but still have weak sanctions capability if:

    allies defect

    evasion networks are strong

    enforcement is poor

    legal or political coordination fails

So here the distinction is very useful.
Result

Instrument and capability should stay separate in the sanctions case.
Test 2: proxy networks

This is harder.
Candidate objects

    relationships with militias

    funding pipelines

    logistics routes

    ideological affinity

    command links

    ability to activate proxies effectively

Clean cut
Instruments

    proxy channels

    logistics/funding links

    liaison structures

    affiliated organizations

Capability

    ability to generate deterrence, coercion, or disruption through those proxies

Why this is harder

Because the proxy “tool” is already partly organizational and relational, so it feels closer to a capability than a discrete instrument.

But the distinction still works if you ask:

    does the channel exist?

    and separately, can the actor reliably use it to get intended results?

Example:

    a proxy relationship may exist

    but command/control may be weak

    so instrument exists, capability is partial

Result

The distinction survives, but is messier.
Test 3: missiles
Candidate objects

    missile stockpile

    launchers

    basing

    targeting systems

    training

    maintenance

    readiness

Clean cut
Instruments

    missile systems

    launch platforms

    delivery assets

Capability

    ability to impose credible strike threat or actual strike effect

Why this works

A state can have missiles on paper but weak real capability because:

    poor readiness

    weak accuracy

    vulnerable launchers

    poor command/control

So missiles are a strong case for separating:

    possession of instruments

    effective capability

Result

The distinction clearly survives.
Test 4: diplomacy
Candidate objects

    embassies

    diplomatic channels

    alliance forums

    summit access

    international institutions

    negotiating skill

    coalition management ability

Clean cut
Instruments

    diplomatic channels

    institutional venues

    alliance forums

    envoys

Capability

    ability to coordinate, persuade, bargain, and maintain coalitions

Why this works

A country can have lots of formal diplomatic instruments but weak diplomatic capability.
Result

Distinction survives strongly.
What the tests show
Strongest pattern

The distinction works best when framed as:

    Instrument = available means/channel/tool

    Capability = effective usable power through those means

That seems stable across:

    sanctions

    missiles

    diplomacy

    even proxies, though proxies are the messiest case

Decision rule

Here is the cleanest operational test.
Ask of a thing X:
It is an instrument if:

X is primarily a means, channel, platform, asset, or mechanism through which action can be taken.
It is a capability if:

X is primarily an effective competence, readiness, or power to use means to achieve effects.
Useful diagnostic questions
Instrument test

    Can I point to the tool/channel/asset itself?

    Could two actors possess similar instruments but use them differently?

If yes, likely instrument.
Capability test

    Does this depend on competence, coordination, readiness, organization, or effectiveness?

    Could an actor possess the instrument but lack the capability?

If yes, likely capability.
Clean examples
Sanctions

    instrument: sanctions authorities and financial chokepoints

    capability: ability to impose effective pressure

Missiles

    instrument: missile systems

    capability: credible strike power

Diplomacy

    instrument: diplomatic channels/forums

    capability: coalition-management ability

Proxies

    instrument: proxy channels/networks

    capability: ability to turn them into reliable strategic leverage

Where it still gets fuzzy

The fuzziness comes when the “thing” is partly:

    asset

    organization

    and effective power

Proxy networks are the best example.

A proxy network can be:

    an instrument as a channel

    a capability as a durable influence mechanism

    part of strategy as a chosen approach

So the ontology needs a rule for ambiguous cases.
Best ambiguity rule

When something is ambiguous, represent it at the lowest level that preserves useful distinctions.

So for proxy networks:

    if you need to distinguish existence vs effective use, keep both instrument and capability

    if you do not need that distinction in a given analysis, collapse them locally

That gives you tractability without losing expressive power.
Recommendation

I think the ontology should keep both instruments and capabilities, but with this rule:

    Keep them separate by default, but allow local collapse when the distinction is not analytically important.

That seems better than forcing one global answer.
Updated candidate v1
Core

    Actor

    Values

    Classification Rules

    World Models

    State Space

    State Estimate

    Objectives

    Constraints

    Instruments

    Capabilities

    Strategy

    Action

Derived

    Outcome

    Feedback loop

This still looks right to me.

The next best move now is probably a lean glossary v4 for just these surviving terms, since we’ve finally stress-tested the shakiest remaining distinction.

Yeah, so I guess I'm feeling like there's missing models in some of these things. It's like whether the capability exists, it's probably given an instrument. It is like probably a kind of, it's like the intermix between the world model and strategy, often in a game theoretic sense. So like when you say evasion networks are strong, now we are at like a model of how the cat and mouse game is structured and then the strategies that both sides are going to take.

Yes — that’s a very good catch, and I think it points to the next real refinement.

What you’re noticing is that capability is not purely “objective possession”. It is often:

    capability under a model of interaction

So when I said “evasion networks are strong,” that was not just:

    instrument exists

    capability exists

It was really:

    given a model of the sanctions-enforcement game,

    given likely strategies on both sides,

    the effective capability to evade is high.

So I think the cleaner formulation is:

    Instrument = available means/tool/channel

    Capability = expected effectiveness of using instruments within an interaction model

That means capability is not just sitting there statically. It depends on:

    your own instruments

    your own competence

    the opponent’s instruments and competence

    and the world model of how the interaction works

In other words, capability is already partly relational and strategic.

So I’d revise the dependency map like this:

Values + Classification Rules + World Models + State Estimate -> Objectives

Then:

Objectives + Constraints + Instruments + World Models + State Estimate -> Strategy

And maybe more importantly:

Capabilities = a derived assessment from Instruments + World Models + State Estimate (+ opponent interaction assumptions)

So capability may not be a fully primitive thing after all. It might be better treated as a derived property:

    sometimes stable enough to model as a node

    but conceptually downstream of tools plus an interaction model

That would fit your cat-and-mouse point very well:

    sanctions instrument exists

    evasion instrument exists

    capability on either side depends on the strategic game between them

So I think the real distinction becomes:

    Instrument = what channels/means are available

    Capability = how effective those means are expected to be in the relevant strategic environment

And that strategic environment is described partly by:

    world models

    state estimates

    opponent responses

This suggests two options:
Option 1: keep capability, but redefine it

Capability = model-relative effective power
Option 2: demote capability

Treat capability as a derived analytic assessment, not a core primitive

I think Option 1 is probably better for usability, but with the explicit note that capability is not raw possession.

So the cleanest revised definition would be:

    Capability: the expected ability of an actor to use available instruments effectively, given its world model, state estimate, organizational competence, and anticipated interaction with others.

That is more complex, but much more accurate.

And yes, this means some of these layers are not a simple chain. They are more like:

    values

    classification

    world models

    state estimates

    instruments
    all feeding into both:

    objective formation

    capability assessment

    strategy choice

So I think your point is right: the framework is drifting away from a pure layered stack and toward a decision context where some things are inputs, some are derived assessments, and strategy is chosen from that whole context.

The next best move is probably to identify which terms are truly primitive inputs versus derived constructs. That would clear up a lot.

Okay, let's think through that.

Yes. I think this is exactly the right next step.

Instead of asking “what layers are there?”, we should now ask:

    Which terms are primitive inputs, which are derived constructs, and which are just convenience summaries?

That will clean up a lot of confusion.
Primitive vs derived pass
Candidate primitive inputs

These are things the framework probably has to take as given, at least locally.
1. Actor

You need some decision-making unit, even if imperfectly unitary.
2. Values

These are the terminal evaluative commitments.
They do not seem reducible inside the framework.
3. Classification rules

These may also be primitive, or at least primitive-ish, because they are not causal. They tell you what counts as what.
4. World models

These are primitive inputs in the sense that the framework should not assume one true model. It should represent which model the actor is using.
5. State estimate

This is also primitive from the actor’s point of view. The actor acts on its estimate, not on ground truth.
6. Instruments

These are plausible primitives: the means/channels/tools available.
Candidate derived constructs

These seem increasingly like outputs of the above, not true primitives.
7. Objectives

These look derived from:

    values

    classification rules

    world models

    state estimate

So objective is probably:

    a derived target over state space

8. Capability

This now really looks derived from:

    instruments

    world models

    state estimate

    organizational competence

    expected interaction with others

So capability is less a primitive possession and more an assessed effectiveness.
9. Strategy

Definitely derived. It is selected given the full decision context.
10. Action

Derived from strategy plus immediate conditions.
11. Outcome

Derived from action + interaction + state transition.
Ambiguous / mixed case
Constraint

This one is tricky.

Some constraints feel primitive:

    geography

    treaty obligations

    budget ceilings

    domestic political facts

But some “constraints” are really state estimates or capability assessments in disguise.

So I think constraint should stay, but understood as:

    a primitive or externally imposed limitation in the decision context

Not everything limiting action should be called a constraint. Only the relatively hard or binding ones.
Provisional structure
Primitive-ish inputs

    Actor

    Values

That already feels cleaner.
Why this helps

Because then we stop pretending everything is the same kind of ontology object.

For example:

    Capability is not something you just “have” the same way you have a missile or a sanctions law.

    Objective is not something floating out in the world; it is computed from values + models + estimates.

    Strategy is obviously a choice function output, not a primitive.

Decision context inputs

That may be the cleanest way to talk about it.

Yes — I think you’re tightening it in the right direction.

First, on values: yes, what I meant by “terminal evaluative commitments” is basically exactly what you just said: things that are good in themselves, not because they lead to something else. So that phrasing is fine.

Second, on classification rules: yes, that’s basically right. They are close to ontology / type / is-a / counts-as relations. Not causal, just categorical. I think that survives clearly.

Third, I agree on world model versus model. “World model” is starting to sound too physical/material. The cleaner term is probably just Model, with an explicit definition that it can include:

    material dynamics

    strategic interaction dynamics

    social dynamics

    moral/ethical dynamics

So: rename world model to model.

Fourth, I think your point about objectives is right, and it means they are even more derived than we were saying. They are not just “values + model + state estimate.” They are also partly a function of strategy or planning. In other words:

    Values define what ultimately matters

    Model defines how movement through state space works

    Strategy/policy defines a broad path through that space

    Objectives are intermediate target states or subgoals along that path

So objectives are more like planned intermediate targets, not primitive desiderata. That matches your China example well.

This starts to look a lot like control / planning language from ML and decision theory:

    state space

    state estimate

    transition model

    policy / strategy

    subgoals / objectives

    actions

The closest formal family is probably MDPs / POMDPs / model-based planning, except you’re adding explicit values and classification structure on top of that.

On capabilities and constraints: yes, I think your intuition is right that constraints may also be partly derived. Some are primitive-ish:

    geography

    legal prohibitions

    budget ceilings

But many are really model-relative or interaction-relative:

    “air superiority is impossible”

    “sanctions are binding”

    “escalation is too risky”

Those are partly derived from:

    model

    state estimate

    opponent behavior

    instrument effectiveness

So I’d now say:

More primitive

    Actor

    Values

    Classification rules

    Model

    State space

    State estimate

    Instruments

More derived

    Constraints

    Capabilities

    Strategy

    Objectives

    Actions

And maybe the cleanest distinction is:

    Capabilities = perceived actionable strengths in the current modeled environment

    Constraints = perceived actionable limits in the current modeled environment

So they are almost duals.

My best suggestion for the next step is to stop trying to force a pure layer cake and instead make a primitive / derived table with dependencies. That will probably stabilize this faster than more glossary work.

    Strategy

Important correction

I now think objectives should come after strategy, or at least be co-determined with it, just as you suggested.

There are really two possibilities:
Option A: objectives before strategy

This treats objectives as chosen target states, then strategy as path.
Option B: objectives and strategy co-determine each other

This treats strategy as shaping which intermediate objectives are even worth selecting.

I think Option B is more accurate.

So a better dependency picture is:

Values + Classification rules + Model + State estimate + Instruments
→ Capability / Constraint assessments
→ Strategy
→ Objectives
→ Action

Or maybe even:

Values + Classification rules + Model + State estimate + Instruments
→ Capability / Constraint assessments
→ Strategy–Objective package
→ Action

That may be the cleanest.
Why constraints are partly derived

Your suspicion was right. Many constraints are not just “out there.” They are assessed.

Examples:

    “war is too risky”

    “sanctions are binding”

    “air superiority is unavailable”

    “escalation will backfire”

These depend on:

    model

    state estimate

    interaction assumptions

So constraints and capabilities are close cousins:

    capability = expected room to act effectively

    constraint = expected limit on acting effectively

Yeah, I agree with option B that the objectives and strategy are like co-determined by models and game theory and state estimates. And it really seems like we are kind of getting into, I think you called it like control plane theory, ML stuff, model-based planning, or whatever that is.

Yes — I think that’s exactly where this is heading.

Once you say:

    actors have terminal values

    they have models of how the environment and other actors behave

    they have a state estimate

    they choose plans / strategies / subgoals under constraints

you are very close to the language of:

    model-based planning

    control theory

    decision theory

    game theory

    and, if uncertainty is explicit, POMDP-like reasoning

The important difference is that your framework is trying to make explicit some things those formal frameworks often treat as hidden or given:

    values

    classification rules

    competing models rather than one assumed true transition model

    strategic interaction among actors with different internal representations

So I think the cleanest way to say it is:

    We are moving from a static ontology of geopolitical concepts toward a decision architecture or planning architecture.

And that architecture looks roughly like:

Values + Classification Rules + Model + State Estimate + Instruments
→ Capability / Constraint assessments
→ Strategy–Objective package
→ Action
→ State transition
→ Updated state estimate

That is much closer to model-based planning than to a traditional IR typology.

I also think this helps explain why some earlier terms felt unstable. Terms like:

    objective

    capability

    constraint

felt slippery because they are not really “things in the world.” They are more like derived planning constructs inside a decision process.

So I think the next useful move is probably not another large glossary, but to explicitly define this as a planning architecture and then decide which terms are:

    environmental primitives

    agent primitives

    derived planning constructs

That would stabilize the framework a lot.

And I'm wondering if kind of values are the axes in a multi-dimensional space, although we probably also need axes or something related to states which models, say, has a causal effect on values.

Yes — that’s a good way to think about it.

Values can be the axes of a value space: security, purity, dignity, prosperity, freedom, holiness, stability, prestige, and so on. Then a model says how features of the state of the world load onto those axes.

So you might have:

    a state space: possible configurations of the world

    a value space: dimensions of intrinsic good/bad

    a model: a mapping from states, actions, and transitions into expected effects on value dimensions

Then the actor is not just asking “which state do I want,” but more like:

    given my model, which trajectories through state space best realize value in value space?

That means the state axes and value axes are different kinds of things:

    state axes = descriptive dimensions

    value axes = evaluative dimensions

    model = bridge between them

So if prosperity is not intrinsically valuable but matters because it increases happiness and freedom, then “prosperity” may belong more to the state space, while “happiness” and “freedom” belong more to the value space. The model then says:

    more prosperity → more happiness

    more prosperity → more freedom
    or maybe not, depending on the actor’s model.

That suggests a pretty clean architecture:

    Value space: what matters intrinsically

    State space: what the world can be like

    Model: how states/actions/transitions affect value

    State estimate: where the actor thinks it is

    Planning layer: strategy/objectives chosen to move through state space in ways that score well in value space

So yes, I think your instinct is right: values are probably closer to axes of one space, while state variables are axes of another, and models are what connect them. That is cleaner than making everything live in one giant undifferentiated ontology.

The next useful step is probably to sketch this explicitly as two spaces plus a mapping:

    state space

    value space

    model as mapping between them


And given my model, which trajectory through state space best realized value and value space are also, this is also like game theoretic dependent, which frames the strategy.

Yes — exactly. And that means the picture is not just:

state space → value space

but:

    given my model of the environment and other players, which policy/trajectory through state space maximizes expected value?

So the missing ingredient is the interaction model or game model. That is what turns this from single-agent planning into strategic planning.

A cleaner architecture is:

    Value space: what is intrinsically good/bad

    State space: possible world configurations

    Interaction model: how actions by multiple actors move the state

    State estimate: where the actor thinks the game currently is

    Policy / strategy: conditional plan over trajectories

    Objectives: local or intermediate targets induced by that policy

So yes, strategy is framed not just by values and a causal model, but by a model of strategic interaction. In ML/control terms, this is closer to:

    model-based planning in a multi-agent setting

    game-theoretic control

    POMDP-ish reasoning if state is uncertain

That also suggests “model” may need subtypes:

    state-transition model: how the world evolves

    other-agent model: how others choose actions

    value-realization model: how states map to value

I think that is the next real clarification: not another glossary yet, but splitting model into these components and seeing whether that makes the architecture click.

Okay, what next?

Next, I’d do a model decomposition pass.

Right now the most overloaded term is model, so the highest-value move is to split it into a few components and see which are actually needed. My best guess is:

    State-transition model: how actions change the state

    Other-agent model: how other actors choose/respond

    Value-realization model: how states/trajectories map onto values

Then test whether the architecture becomes cleaner:

Value space

    State space

    State estimate

    model bundle
    → strategy/objective package
    → action/policy

That is probably more useful than another glossary right now, because it will tell us whether “model” is one thing or three things. After that, we can do a much cleaner lean glossary.

Okay, proceed.

Good. Here’s the model decomposition pass.
Model decomposition

The term model is currently doing too much work. I think it should be split into at least three components.
1. State-transition model

Answers:

    If actions are taken, how does the state change?

This is the causal/dynamical part.

Examples:

    sanctions increase economic pressure

    proxy support increases deterrent depth

    military buildup raises escalation risk

    trade openness increases interdependence

This is closest to:

    control theory dynamics

    transition models in planning

    Pearl/SEM-type causal structure

2. Other-agent model

Answers:

    How do other actors choose and respond?

This is the game-theoretic part.

Examples:

    if I sanction them, they will escalate through proxies

    if I concede now, they will press harder later

    if I build power quietly, they may underreact

    if they think I am bluffing, deterrence fails

This is closest to:

    game theory

    opponent modeling

    multi-agent planning

3. Value-realization model

Answers:

    How do states or trajectories realize value?

This is the bridge from state space to value space.

Examples:

    prosperity increases well-being

    sovereignty increases dignity

    impurity reduces holiness

    regional depth increases security

    interdependence increases peace, or instead vulnerability

This is the layer you were pointing to when you said:
economic abundance is only good insofar as it leads to freedom, happiness, etc.

So this is crucial.
Possible fourth component
4. Classification / ontology model

This is maybe separate, maybe not.

Answers:

    What counts as what?

Examples:

    X counts as coercion

    Y counts as impurity

    Z counts as aggression

    this is a deterrent, not an attack

I still think this is better kept separate from “model” proper, because it is not dynamic. It is more like ontology / semantics.

So I would keep classification rules separate.
Revised architecture

Now the planning picture becomes:
Primitive-ish

    Actor

    Value space

    State space

    Classification rules

    State estimate

    Model bundle:

        state-transition model

        other-agent model

        value-realization model

    Instruments

Derived

    capability assessments

    constraint assessments

    strategy/objective package

    actions

    state transitions

    ↓

    ↓

Why this helps
A. It separates different kinds of “how the world works”

Previously, “model” mixed together:

    physics / social causality

    strategic interaction

    what counts as success

Now those are distinct.
B. It explains disagreement better

Two actors can share:

    the same values

but differ on:

    transition model

    other-agent model

    value-realization model

Example:
both want prosperity,
but one thinks trade yields mutual gains,
the other thinks trade creates dependence.
C. It clarifies objectives

Objectives are not primitive desires.
They are induced by:

    values

    value-realization model

    current state estimate

    strategic interaction

Stress test on your economic example

Shared value:

    welfare is good

Different value-realization model:

    prosperity leads to welfare
    or perhaps not directly

Different other-agent model:

    rivals will cooperate
    vs

    rivals will exploit dependence

Different state-transition model:

    trade increases total pie
    vs

    trade shifts leverage and fragility

Result:
same value,
different strategy/objective package.

This works very cleanly.
Stress test on the moral purity example

Value:

    purity is good

Classification rule:

    homosexuality counts as impurity

No dynamic model needed yet.

If you then add:

    impurity spreads through toleration

    impurity degrades order
    you are adding:

    state-transition model

    value-realization model

So this also fits cleanly.
What still looks uncertain
1. Value space vs values

These are probably just two levels of the same thing.
For v1, you may only need “values,” not a full “value space” formalism.
2. Constraints vs capability

Still likely derived from:

    instruments

    state estimate

    model bundle

    organizational competence

3. Objectives vs strategy

I still think these are best treated as a package, not fully separate sequential stages.

        transition model

That feels much cleaner than where we started.

Okay, let's do this, and also, while you're doing it, can you opine on how midfield would play in here or whether it does?

Yes. MIDFIELD still fits, but lower in the architecture than we originally had it.
One-page architecture summary
Purpose

A planning architecture for strategic behavior that does not assume:

    one privileged true model of the world

    one actor’s interpretation is objectively correct

    goals map directly to strategies

Instead, actors:

    value certain ends

    classify phenomena in particular ways

    use models of dynamics and interaction

    estimate the current state

    choose strategies accordingly

Core primitives
1. Actor

A decision-making unit.

Examples:

    state

    regime

    faction

    network

2. Values

What is good or bad in itself for the actor.

Examples:

    security

    sovereignty

    dignity

    purity

    justice

    prosperity, if treated as terminal

3. Classification rules

Non-causal rules for what counts as what.

Examples:

    sanctions count as coercion

    homosexuality counts as impurity

    proxy support counts as deterrence, not aggression

    nuclear latency counts as proliferation risk

These are basically ontology / is-a / counts-as relations.
4. State space

The set of possible configurations of the world.

Examples of dimensions:

    sanctions pressure

    deterrence balance

    alliance credibility

    internal legitimacy

    regional depth

5. State estimate

Where the actor thinks it is in state space.

Not privileged ground truth; just the actor’s working estimate.
6. Instruments

Available means, channels, or tools of influence.

Examples:

    sanctions authorities

    missile systems

    diplomatic channels

    proxy networks

    propaganda channels

7. Model bundle

The actor’s operative models, split into three types:
a. State-transition model

How actions change the state.

Examples:

    sanctions increase pressure

    proxy support raises deterrent depth

    military buildup increases escalation risk

b. Other-agent model

How other actors behave and respond.

Examples:

    if pressured, Iran will escalate asymmetrically

    if accommodated, a rival will push further

    if bluff is detected, deterrence fails

c. Value-realization model

How states or trajectories realize values.

Examples:

    prosperity increases welfare

    sovereignty increases dignity

    regional depth increases security

    impurity decreases holiness

This is the key bridge between state space and value space.
Derived constructs
8. Capability assessment

Expected effective ability to use instruments in the current modeled environment.

Depends on:

    instruments

    model bundle

    state estimate

    organizational competence

    anticipated opposition

9. Constraint assessment

Expected actionable limits in the current modeled environment.

Depends on:

    model bundle

    state estimate

    institutions

    material limits

    opponent response

10. Strategy–objective package

A broad policy/path plus its intermediate target states.

This is best treated as a package, not a strict sequence.

Why:

    strategy shapes which intermediate objectives make sense

    objectives shape which strategies are worth choosing

11. Action

A specific move taken under the strategy.
12. State transition

The resulting movement in state space.

Then:

    state transition updates state estimates

    the cycle repeats

Compact architecture

Actor
Values
Classification Rules
State Space
State Estimate
Instruments
Model Bundle:
  - State-Transition Model
  - Other-Agent Model
  - Value-Realization Model
        ↓
Capability / Constraint Assessments
        ↓
Strategy <-> Objectives
        ↓
Action
        ↓
State Transition
        ↓
Updated State Estimate

Tiny worked example: U.S.–Iran
Values
U.S.

    security

    stability

    alliance reliability

Iran

    sovereignty

    regime continuity

    dignity / anti-subordination

Classification rules
U.S.

    proxy activity counts as destabilizing aggression

    nuclear latency counts as proliferation risk

Iran

    sanctions count as coercion

    regional partner depth counts as deterrence

State estimate
U.S.

    Iran has substantial asymmetric reach

    escalation risk is elevated

Iran

    U.S. coercive pressure is high

    direct conventional vulnerability is high

Model bundle
U.S.

    sanctions can impose pressure

    proxy activity can raise escalation risk

    strong deterrence can contain Iran

Iran

    forward deterrence reduces vulnerability

    sanctions resistance improves endurance

    direct weakness requires asymmetric depth

Instruments
U.S.

    sanctions machinery

    force posture

    alliance channels

Iran

    proxy channels

    missile systems

    evasion channels

Derived
U.S. capability assessment

    strong sanctions capability

    strong alliance coordination capability

Iran capability assessment

    moderate-to-strong asymmetric deterrence capability

    limited conventional capability

Strategy–objective package
U.S.

    strategy: containment/coercive pressure

    objectives: reduce Iranian leverage, maintain deterrence

Iran

    strategy: forward deterrence/endurance

    objectives: reduce vulnerability, preserve leverage, survive pressure

Where MIDFIELD fits

MIDFIELD still fits, but not as the top-level architecture.

It fits best as a taxonomy for instruments, and secondarily for some capability assessments.
Best placement
Primary role:

MIDFIELD classifies instruments

    Military

    Intelligence

    Diplomacy

    Finance

    Information

    Economic

    Legal

    Development

So:

    sanctions authority = Finance / Legal

    proxy channels = Military / Intelligence / Information

    embassy network = Diplomacy

    missile force = Military

    trade access tools = Economic

    propaganda channels = Information

Secondary role:

MIDFIELD helps organize capability assessments

Examples:

    financial capability

    diplomatic capability

    intelligence capability

But capability is more than MIDFIELD domain membership. It also depends on:

    model bundle

    state estimate

    competence

    opponent interaction

So MIDFIELD is a means taxonomy, not a planning theory.
What MIDFIELD does well

It helps answer:

    what kinds of tools are available?

    where is an actor strong or weak?

    what domains are being substituted for others?

Example:

    U.S. substitutes Finance for Military via sanctions

    Iran substitutes Intelligence/Military hybrids for conventional weakness via proxies

    China often substitutes Economic/Financial tools for direct military confrontation

That is genuinely useful.
What MIDFIELD does not do

MIDFIELD does not explain:

    what the actor values

    how the actor thinks the world works

    how the actor models rivals

    why one strategy is chosen over another

So it belongs below values/models/state estimates, not above them.
Cleanest statement on MIDFIELD

    MIDFIELD is a taxonomy of usable means.
    It helps classify instruments and sometimes capabilities, but it does not replace the planning architecture.

My opinion

MIDFIELD still belongs in the system, but in a more modest way than we first thought.

Originally it looked like:

    the execution layer of grand strategy

Now I think it is better seen as:

    the instrument layer taxonomy

    useful for operational decomposition

    not the core decision logic

That is a better fit.

The next best move is probably to convert this into a lean glossary v4 using only the terms that survived this architecture.
