I think it's really probably a pretty small set of terminal values. And then on Proxy activity counts as destabilizing aggression. I feel like that probably needs a model as an intermediary because I can imagine cases where, like, you get to rally around the flag effect and actually terrorism or proxy activity is actually stabilizing. And then a state, I think, estimate needs to be described in terms of variables which would input models. So, like, Iran has substantial asymmetrical reach. I don't know if that's really a state. Maybe it is, but they could just probably should be more carefully defined.

Yes — those are good flags, and I think they point to three real corrections.

First, on values: I agree we are still letting semi-terminal things leak into the value layer. I think the discipline should be:

    Values are very small in number and very abstract

    things like security, sovereignty, stability, alliance reliability, regime survival often belong downstream, unless the actor really treats them as terminal in themselves

So a cleaner split is probably:
Likely terminal-ish values

    well-being / flourishing

    dignity / honor

    purity / holiness

    justice

    autonomy / non-subordination

    maybe continuity / persistence of self or group

Usually non-terminal, therefore derived

    security

    stability

    alliance reliability

    regime survival

    sovereignty, often

    regional influence

Those are often better treated as:

    objectives

    or maybe state-space properties valued because they protect terminal values

So yes, I think your instinct is right: the value layer should be much smaller and more abstract.

Second, on classification vs model: I agree with your proxy example. “Proxy activity counts as destabilizing aggression” is too quick as a bare classification rule. More carefully:

    classification rule might be:

        proxy activity counts as aggression

    then a model says:

        aggression raises instability risk

        or in some cases, aggression increases cohesion and stabilizes a regime via rally effects

So the framework should not let classification do causal work. Classification says what kind of thing something is. Model says what effects that kind of thing has. That’s a good correction.

Third, on state estimate: yes, it should be expressed in terms of more carefully defined variables, not loose prose summaries. “Iran has substantial asymmetric reach” is more like a summary claim. A cleaner state estimate would be over variables like:

    proxy network density

    missile reach

    attribution uncertainty

    sanctions pressure

    escalation risk

    conventional force vulnerability

Then “Iran has substantial asymmetric reach” becomes a higher-level description of several estimated variables, not a primitive state term.

So I think the architecture gets cleaner if we say:

    terminal values are sparse and abstract

    classification rules are non-causal only

    models map classified or observed states/actions to consequences

    state estimates should be over explicit variables, not prose summaries

That suggests the next useful move is probably a tight variableization pass:

    shrink the value layer to maybe 5–8 candidate terminal values,

    force state estimates into variable form,

    ensure classification rules never smuggle in causal assumptions.

That would probably improve the framework more than another glossary rewrite right now.

Okay, proceed. And I don't, I mean, we're gonna have examples. I don't, I don't think we need to necessarily have a full enumeration of all the variables and the values or, and especially not in the model layers, because at the end of the day, probably what I would do with this is use a large language model to read through different sorts of analyses of these sorts of conflicts and pick out the variables and the models and the rest of this stuff. So we just kind of need, you know, schemas and definitions which can serve as extraction prompts and kind of the ontologies, like their domains and ranges, dependencies, and I don't, I think we have kind of, we talked about the energy relationships with these things playing roles, which I think kind of plays into the dependency structure of like how we got to figure out, like necessary and sufficient for these energy relationships and that sort of thing, that I can kind of extract all of this stuff from text.

Yes — that makes sense, and it changes the goal in a good way.

If this is mainly going to be used as an extraction schema for an LLM, then we should stop trying to fully specify the content universe and instead specify:

    the slots

    their meanings

    their dependencies

    their allowed relations

    what counts as evidence for extracting each one

So the next move is not “list all values/models/variables.” It is to define a minimal ontology schema for extraction.
Extraction-oriented schema
1. Core entity types

These are the things the model should try to extract from text.
Actor

A decision-making unit.

Examples in text:

    state

    regime

    institution

    faction

    network

Fields:

    id

    label

    type

    scope

    parent_actor optional

Value

A terminal evaluative commitment.

Definition:
Something treated as good or bad in itself, not merely because it leads to something else.

Fields:

    id

    label

    polarity positive or negative

    holder actor

    evidence_span

Extraction test:
Would the text still treat this as desirable even if it did not obviously lead to anything else?
ClassificationRule

A non-causal categorization.

Definition:
A rule that says some phenomenon counts as some category.

Fields:

    id

    subject

    category

    holder

    evidence_span

Examples:

    sanctions count as coercion

    proxy warfare counts as aggression

    homosexuality counts as impurity

Extraction test:
Does the text classify or label something, rather than explain what it causes?
StateVariable

A dimension of state space.

Definition:
A variable whose value helps locate a system in state space.

Fields:

    id

    label

    domain

    type structural / positional / flow

    scope

    unit_type

    evidence_span

Examples:

    sanctions pressure

    deterrence balance

    alliance credibility

    internal legitimacy

Extraction test:
Could this reasonably vary over time or across cases and help define “where the system is”?
StateEstimate

An actor-attributed estimate of one or more state variables.

Fields:

    id

    actor

    variable

    estimated_value

    confidence

    evidence_span

Extraction test:
Is the text saying what an actor believes the situation currently is?
Model

A mapping used by an actor to relate states, actions, and values.

Subtypes:

    transition_model

    other_agent_model

    value_realization_model

Fields:

    id

    subtype

    holder

    description

    evidence_span

Extraction test:
Is the text saying how something leads to something else, how another actor behaves, or how a state realizes value?
Instrument

A tool, channel, or means available for influence.

Fields:

    id

    label

    holder

    midfield_tags

    evidence_span

Extraction test:
Is this a means/channel/tool rather than an assessed effective ability?
CapabilityAssessment

An assessment of expected effectiveness.

Fields:

    id

    holder

    instrument_refs

    target_effect

    assessment

    context_dependencies

    evidence_span

Extraction test:
Is the text assessing what the actor can effectively do, given the environment?
ConstraintAssessment

An assessment of actionable limits.

Fields:

    id

    holder

    restricted_action_or_strategy

    source_of_constraint

    assessment

    evidence_span

Extraction test:
Is the text saying what limits feasible action?
StrategyObjectivePackage

A linked policy-and-target construct.

Definition:
A broad path plus its intermediate targets.

Fields:

    id

    holder

    strategy_label

    objective_refs

    rationale

    evidence_span

Extraction test:
Is the text describing a broad approach together with what it is trying to achieve along the way?
Action

A specific move.

Fields:

    id

    holder

    label

    target

    time

    instrument_refs

    evidence_span

StateTransition

A claimed or observed change in state variables.

Fields:

    id

    trigger_action_refs

    affected_variable

    direction

    magnitude optional

    evidence_span

Extraction test:
Is the text saying that some action or process changed a variable?
2. Relation schema

These are the domain/range rules.
HoldsValue

    domain: Actor

    range: Value

UsesClassificationRule

    domain: Actor

    range: ClassificationRule

UsesModel

    domain: Actor

    range: Model

EstimatesState

    domain: Actor

    range: StateEstimate

HasInstrument

    domain: Actor

    range: Instrument

SupportsCapabilityAssessment

    domain: Instrument or Model or StateEstimate

    range: CapabilityAssessment

SupportsConstraintAssessment

    domain: Model or StateEstimate or StateVariable

    range: ConstraintAssessment

InformsStrategyObjectivePackage

    domain: Value or ClassificationRule or Model or StateEstimate or CapabilityAssessment or ConstraintAssessment

    range: StrategyObjectivePackage

IncludesObjectiveTarget

    domain: StrategyObjectivePackage

    range: StateVariable

EnablesAction

    domain: StrategyObjectivePackage or Instrument or CapabilityAssessment

    range: Action

CausesTransition

    domain: Action

    range: StateTransition

UpdatesEstimate

    domain: StateTransition

    range: StateEstimate

3. Dependency structure

This is the key extraction logic.
Primitive-ish for extraction

These are the first things to look for in text:

    Actor

    Value

    ClassificationRule

    Model

    StateVariable

    Instrument

Second-pass extracted

These depend on the first pass:

    StateEstimate

    CapabilityAssessment

    ConstraintAssessment

Third-pass extracted

These depend on the earlier passes:

    StrategyObjectivePackage

    Action

    StateTransition

So the extraction order should roughly be:

    identify actors

    identify values, classifications, models, state variables, instruments

    identify state estimates, capabilities, constraints

    identify strategy-objective packages

    identify actions

    identify transitions

That ordering is useful for prompt design.
4. Necessary vs sufficient guidance for extraction

This is where your ER / dependency concern matters.
Value

Necessary:

    text indicates intrinsic desirability or undesirability

Not sufficient:

    merely being important or useful

ClassificationRule

Necessary:

    text says X counts as Y, is a Y, or is treated as Y

Not sufficient:

    text says X causes Y

Model

Necessary:

    text implies a mapping or expectation of how something leads to something else

Not sufficient:

    mere description of current state

StateVariable

Necessary:

    text refers to a dimension that can vary and locate the system

Not sufficient:

    one-off event without broader state relevance

CapabilityAssessment

Necessary:

    text evaluates expected effectiveness, not just possession

Not sufficient:

    mere existence of an instrument

ConstraintAssessment

Necessary:

    text indicates a limit on feasible action

Not sufficient:

    generic difficulty or cost without action-limiting implication

StrategyObjectivePackage

Necessary:

    text links a broad approach to one or more target states

Not sufficient:

    isolated action without broader path logic

5. MIDFIELD in the schema

MIDFIELD fits as an attribute system for instruments, not as a primary ontology layer.

So:
Instrument

has attribute:

    midfield_tags: [M, I, D, F, I, E, L, D]

This is useful for extraction because it gives the model a controlled vocabulary.

Examples:

    sanctions channel -> Finance, Legal

    proxy network -> Military, Intelligence, Information

    embassy access -> Diplomacy

    missile systems -> Military

You can also optionally let CapabilityAssessment inherit or summarize MIDFIELD tags from referenced instruments.

So MIDFIELD helps extraction by:

    constraining the kind of instrument being extracted

    making cross-case comparison easier

But it should not structure the whole ontology.
6. Prompting implication

This schema is good for LLM extraction because each term can be turned into a prompt like:

    “Extract any terminal values attributed to actor X.”

    “Extract any classification rules used by actor X.”

    “Extract any causal, strategic, or value-realization models attributed to actor X.”

    “Extract state variables relevant to this conflict.”

    “Extract estimates of those variables attributed to each actor.”

    “Extract instruments, tagged with MIDFIELD.”

    “Extract capability or constraint assessments, with evidence.”

    “Extract strategy-objective packages.”

    “Extract actions and claimed state transitions.”

That’s a much better use case than trying to pre-enumerate all content.
7. My recommendation

The next best step is to turn this into a compact extraction codebook with, for each entity:

    definition

    positive extraction cues

    negative cues / confusion cases

    required fields

    relations

    one example

That would be the thing most directly usable as an LLM extraction prompt scaffold.

I am curious for a state variable, in my mind, basically, we just define two different types, values, state, and I'm hesitant to say world state because we already got where those, we talked about how ethics might be a little bit too materialistic, but basically, in the way I'm thinking about it is the non-value state for like this, you know, state configuration space. Really, all of these should only exist if they're a variable which is an input for a model. So it's almost like you define your value space, and then all models which have inputs which terminate or, you know, have some effect on the value dimensions, and those inputs to the models are the only thing you really need to carry about as variables for the non-value state. And then I'm guessing you can, although, again, I haven't really thought this through, but you can go downstream from there where like instruments only are relevant in as much as they can affect the variables which are inputs for models which can affect the values. And then there's probably other downstream dependencies like this. Although I'm not sure, but it probably won't be that simple where there's just a linear dependency network, and it's probably going to involve bouncing back and forth iteratively through the different layers.

Yes — I think that is a very strong simplification.

What you’re proposing is basically:

    Only keep non-value variables that matter as inputs to models that ultimately affect values.

I think that’s probably the right discipline.

So instead of thinking in terms of:

    “world state” or “state variables” as everything descriptively true about the environment,

you think in terms of:

    Value variables: dimensions of what matters intrinsically

    Non-value variables: only those state/configuration variables that enter into one or more models relevant to value realization

That is much cleaner.
Why this is good

It prevents the ontology from bloating into:

    every descriptive feature of the world

    every observable fact

    every policy detail

Instead, a non-value variable only deserves inclusion if it plays a role like:

    it is an input to a transition model

    it is an input to an other-agent model

    it is an input to a value-realization model

So yes, in your framing:

    values define the evaluative space

    models map from non-value variables, actions, and trajectories into effects on value variables

    only variables that appear in those mappings need to be represented

That feels like the cleanest architecture so far.
Even cleaner formulation

You may not need “state variable” as a generic primitive at all. You may instead want:

    Value variable

    Model input variable

Where:

    value variables are the axes of value space

    model input variables are the non-value variables that models actually use

That avoids the “world state” problem entirely.
Instruments in this framing

Yes — instruments then become relevant only insofar as they can affect model input variables.

So:

    instrument → changes model input variables

    models → translate those changes into expected value effects

That means instruments are not primary ontological stars. They are intervention points in the system.
Capabilities and constraints in this framing

This also helps with the earlier ambiguity.

    Capability = expected ability to change relevant model-input variables in desired ways

    Constraint = expected limit on changing relevant model-input variables in desired ways

That’s actually a very elegant reframing.
Important caution

The one thing I would not assume is a purely one-way chain. As you said, it is probably not linear.

Because:

    some model input variables affect other model input variables

    actors model each other recursively

    value-realization may depend on trajectories, not just immediate states

    classification rules may determine which variables are even treated as relevant

So the system is probably:

    sparse

    model-mediated

    recursive / iterative

rather than a simple stack.

But that’s okay. The key simplification still holds:

    A non-value variable should only exist in the ontology if it is used by at least one model relevant to value realization or strategic interaction.

I think that is one of the best design rules we’ve arrived at.
Best next move

I’d now rewrite the schema around just three high-level things:

    Value variables

    Model input variables

    Models connecting them

Then place:

    instruments

    capabilities

    constraints

    strategy

    action

as things defined by how they intervene on or reason over model input variables.

That would be a major simplification.

Okay, let's proceed.

Yes. I think this is the cleanest turn so far.
Minimal schema pivot
Three core primitives
1. Value variables

Dimensions of what is intrinsically good or bad for an actor.

These are the terminal evaluative axes.

Examples:

    dignity

    holiness

    flourishing

    autonomy

    justice

A value variable belongs in the schema only if the actor treats movement on that dimension as good or bad in itself.
2. Model-input variables

Non-value variables that matter only because they are inputs to one or more models.

These are the descriptive/configurational variables you carry in the system.

Examples:

    sanctions pressure

    proxy reach

    alliance credibility

    deterrence balance

    internal legitimacy

    economic output

A model-input variable belongs in the schema only if at least one model uses it.

That is the key discipline.
3. Models

Mappings that connect model-input variables, actions, and trajectories to other variables, including value variables.

These are the real engine.

Subtypes still make sense:

    Transition model
    how actions or current conditions change model-input variables

    Other-agent model
    how other actors choose/respond

    Value-realization model
    how model-input variables or trajectories affect value variables

What this does to the rest of the ontology
Instruments

No longer a top-level conceptual center.

Definition:
A means/channel/tool that can intervene on model-input variables.

So:

    sanctions machinery matters because it can change sanctions pressure, trade access, elite pain, etc.

    proxy networks matter because they can change deterrence balance, escalation risk, regional depth, etc.

Capabilities

Derived.

Definition:
Expected ability to use available instruments to move relevant model-input variables in desired directions.

So capability is no longer mysterious. It is:

    intervention effectiveness

    relative to a model

    relative to current state estimates

    relative to opponent response

Constraints

Also derived, or at least partly derived.

Definition:
Expected limits on moving relevant model-input variables in desired directions.

So capability and constraint become near-duals:

    capability = expected room to move variables

    constraint = expected limits on moving variables

Strategy

A policy/path for trying to move model-input variables over time, given models, toward better value realization.
Objective

Probably not primitive.

Definition:
A selected intermediate target over model-input variables induced by a strategy.

So objectives are waypoints, not deep ontology objects.
Action

A specific intervention through instruments intended to change model-input variables.
Clean dependency picture

Value Variables
Model-Input Variables
Models
    ↓
State Estimate over Model-Input Variables
    ↓
Capability / Constraint Assessments
    ↓
Strategy
    ↓
Objectives (intermediate target variable configurations)
    ↓
Actions via Instruments
    ↓
Changes in Model-Input Variables
    ↓
Model-evaluated effects on Value Variables

That is much cleaner.
Why this is better

It solves a lot at once.
1. No bloated “world state”

You no longer need to represent everything about the world.

Only carry:

    value variables

    model-input variables

2. No fake objectivity

You do not need a privileged “true world state.”

You just need:

    actor state estimates over model-input variables

    analyst state estimates if needed

3. Instruments become properly subordinate

They matter only insofar as they can move model-input variables.
4. Capabilities and constraints become intelligible

They are not weird primitive things; they are assessed ability/limits relative to modeled variable movement.
5. Objectives become easier

They are selected target configurations of model-input variables, not vague nouns.
Extraction-oriented schema version

If you want this for LLM extraction, the core entities now become:
Primitive extraction targets

    Actor

    ValueVariable

    ModelInputVariable

    Model

    Instrument

    StateEstimate

Derived extraction targets

    CapabilityAssessment

    ConstraintAssessment

    Strategy

    ObjectiveTarget

    Action

    ClaimedVariableTransition

Domain/range sketch
HoldsValueVariable

    Actor -> ValueVariable

UsesModel

    Actor -> Model

EstimatesVariable

    Actor -> StateEstimate

    where StateEstimate references a ModelInputVariable

HasInstrument

    Actor -> Instrument

InstrumentAffectsVariable

    Instrument -> ModelInputVariable

ModelUsesVariable

    Model -> ModelInputVariable

ModelAffectsValue

    Model -> ValueVariable

SupportsCapabilityAssessment

    Instrument / Model / StateEstimate -> CapabilityAssessment

SupportsConstraintAssessment

    Model / StateEstimate / Instrument -> ConstraintAssessment

InformsStrategy

    ValueVariable / Model / StateEstimate / CapabilityAssessment / ConstraintAssessment -> Strategy

SelectsObjectiveTarget

    Strategy -> ObjectiveTarget

    where ObjectiveTarget references ModelInputVariable plus desired direction/value

EnablesAction

    Strategy / Instrument / CapabilityAssessment -> Action

ActionChangesVariable

    Action -> ClaimedVariableTransition

    transition references ModelInputVariable

Tiny U.S.–Iran sketch in this new framing
Value variables
U.S.

    security

    alliance credibility only if terminal, otherwise drop

    stability only if terminal, otherwise drop

Iran

    dignity / non-subordination

    ideological continuity

    continuity of governing order if terminalized

Already this forces discipline.
Model-input variables

    sanctions pressure

    deterrence balance

    proxy reach

    internal legitimacy

    nuclear latency

    escalation risk

Models
U.S.

    sanctions increase pressure

    proxy reach increases escalation risk

    nuclear latency threatens long-run security

Iran

    proxy reach improves deterrence

    sanctions endurance preserves order

    direct weakness requires asymmetric leverage

Instruments
U.S.

    sanctions tools

    force posture

    alliance channels

Iran

    proxy channels

    missile channels

    evasion channels

Then everything else is derived from that.

That already feels much cleaner.
My main recommendation

I think the next step should be a lean codebook v1 built around only these concepts:

    ValueVariable

    ModelInputVariable

    Model

    Instrument

    StateEstimate

    CapabilityAssessment

    ConstraintAssessment

    Strategy

    ObjectiveTarget

    Action

    ClaimedVariableTransition

That is now specific enough to guide extraction and abstract enough to stay general.

Well, I assume the models should be determined before the model input variables, just to flag that. And again, on the capability strategy thing, I think this really needs to be interpreted into game theoretic sense. And it's been a while since I've looked at game theory. I mean, even, I think that there's like an interaction matrix. So, I mean, it's more about defining the strategic game, even for an infinite turn game. You don't have to go through an infinite iteration to do this. But I don't know enough about game theory to know how to define that or how that works in here.

Yes — I think that flag is right.

The order should not be:

    first enumerate variables

    then define models over them

It should be closer to:

    models determine which variables are worth carrying

So the workflow is:

    identify the actor’s values

    identify the actor’s model family for how outcomes happen

    extract only the variables that those models actually use

In other words, model-input variables are not primitive in a deep sense. They are model-relative state variables.

That is a big improvement.

On the game-theory point: I think you’re right that capability and strategy only really make sense inside a specified game. A cleaner way to put it is:

    Game form / interaction model defines:

        players

        what each player can do

        when they move

        what they observe

        how the state changes

    Capability is then basically:

        the actor’s feasible or effective action set in that game, given current conditions

    Strategy is:

        a policy over histories or beliefs in that game

So capability is not just “power” in the abstract. It is more like:

    what moves are effectively available to me in this game, from this state, against these opponents

And strategy is:

    how I condition my choices over time on state/history/beliefs

That means the architecture probably wants a new central object:
Interaction model / game model

This would specify:

    actors

    state variables

    action sets

    observation structure

    transition structure

    maybe payoff/value mapping

Then:

    Capabilities are derived from the actor’s feasible action set in that interaction model

    Constraints are the negative side of that same feasibility structure

    Strategy is a policy in that interaction model

    Objectives are local target states induced by that policy

So yes, I think the more correct picture is:

Values

    Interaction model

    State estimate
    → define relevant variables, feasible actions, and policy space

And from there you get:

    capability

    constraint

    strategy

    objective targets

If I were simplifying this for v1, I’d probably rename “model bundle” to something like:

    Interaction model

        includes transition assumptions

        other-agent assumptions

        value-realization assumptions

Then say:

    variables are extracted as the state dimensions referenced by the interaction model

    capabilities are feasible/effective moves in that model

    strategies are policies in that model

That feels much closer to the game-theoretic picture you’re pointing at. The next best step is probably to sketch the minimum contents of an interaction model.

Okay, proceed.

Good. Here’s the minimum contents of an interaction model.
Interaction model: minimum schema

This is the object that defines the strategic game or planning environment.
1. Players

Who is making consequential choices.

    states

    regimes

    factions

    networks

    coalitions

Field:

    players

2. State dimensions

The variables the game tracks.

These are the model-relevant variables, not all facts about the world.

Examples:

    sanctions pressure

    deterrence balance

    proxy reach

    internal legitimacy

    escalation risk

Field:

    state_variables

This is why you were right that models come before variables.
3. Action sets

What each player can do.

These are not abstract values or goals. They are feasible move types in the game.

Examples:

    sanction

    arm proxies

    escalate rhetorically

    negotiate

    strike

    evade

    mobilize

Field:

    action_sets[player]

4. Observation structure

What each player can observe or infer.

This matters because strategy is usually conditional on beliefs, not full truth.

Examples:

    perfect observability

    noisy signals

    attribution uncertainty

    hidden capabilities

    hidden resolve

Field:

    observation_structure

5. Transition structure

How actions move the state.

This is the dynamic part.

Examples:

    sanctions increase pressure

    proxy support increases depth and risk

    diplomacy may reduce tension

    strikes may reduce capability but raise escalation risk

Field:

    transition_rules

6. Opponent-response assumptions

How players think others will react.

This is the game-theoretic part proper.

Examples:

    if pressured, Iran escalates asymmetrically

    if U.S. appears weak, Iran presses harder

    if deterrence is credible, opponent backs off

Field:

    response_model[player -> player]

7. Value mapping

How states or trajectories affect terminal values.

This is the bridge from descriptive state to evaluative significance.

Examples:

    more deterrent depth increases security

    more sanctions pressure lowers flourishing or continuity

    more disorder lowers dignity or order

Field:

    value_realization_rules

8. Time structure

How the game unfolds.

Examples:

    one-shot

    repeated

    sequential

    open-ended

    finite horizon

    indefinite horizon

Field:

    time_structure

9. Feasibility structure

What moves are effectively available from a given state.

This is where capabilities and constraints come from.

Examples:

    can sanction, but only if allies cooperate

    can strike, but at high escalation cost

    can arm proxies, but with attribution risk

Field:

    feasible_actions(player, state_estimate)

This is the key place where:

    capability = expected effective feasible move set

    constraint = expected limit on feasible move set

Clean implication

From this, we can now define:
Capability

A derived assessment of what actions or policies are effectively feasible for an actor in the interaction model from its current state estimate.
Constraint

A derived assessment of what actions or policies are blocked, dominated, or too costly in the interaction model from its current state estimate.
Strategy

A policy over states, histories, or beliefs in the interaction model.
Objective target

An intermediate target over state dimensions induced by the strategy.

That is much cleaner than treating these as standalone primitives.
Compact architecture now

Actor
Values
Classification Rules
Interaction Model
State Estimate
    ↓
Relevant State Variables
Feasible Actions
Capability / Constraint Assessments
    ↓
Strategy / Objective Package
    ↓
Action
    ↓
State Transition
    ↓
Updated State Estimate

Why this helps

It fixes three earlier issues:
1. Variables are model-relative

You only track state variables referenced by the interaction model.
2. Capabilities are game-relative

They are not just “power.” They are effective move options in the game.
3. Strategy is properly game-theoretic

It is a policy in a multi-actor system, not a vague plan.
MIDFIELD here

MIDFIELD now fits even more cleanly.

It belongs mostly in the action set / instrument layer of the interaction model.

So:

    MIDFIELD classifies the channels of action

    the interaction model tells you how those channels affect state variables

    capability tells you how effectively you can use those channels in this game

So MIDFIELD is not the game.
It is part of the move vocabulary inside the game.

That is probably the cleanest placement so far.
Best next move

I think the next best step is to write a lean glossary v4 using this updated architecture, because the core structure now feels much more stable.

Okay, let's do that.

Lean glossary v4

This version uses the more stable planning / interaction architecture we converged on.
Core idea

Actors do not choose strategy directly from goals. They act through an interaction model.

The basic picture is:

Actor + Values + Classification Rules + Interaction Model + State Estimate
→ Capability / Constraint Assessments
→ Strategy / Objective Package
→ Action
→ State Transition
→ Updated State Estimate
1. Actor

A decision-making unit.

Examples:

    state

    regime

    faction

    institution

    network

Use this term when the text attributes choices, evaluations, or strategies to some unit.
2. Value

A terminal evaluative commitment: something treated as good or bad in itself.

Examples:

    dignity

    holiness

    justice

    flourishing

    non-subordination

A value is not included just because it is important. It should be included only if the text treats it as intrinsically desirable or undesirable, not merely useful for something else.
3. Classification Rule

A non-causal rule that says what counts as what.

Examples:

    sanctions count as coercion

    proxy warfare counts as aggression

    homosexuality counts as impurity

    nuclear latency counts as proliferation risk

A classification rule does not explain effects. It assigns categories.

Good test:
If the statement is basically “X is a kind of Y” or “X counts as Y,” it is a classification rule.
4. Interaction Model

The actor’s operative model of the strategic environment.

This is the central object.

An interaction model minimally includes:

    players

    state dimensions

    action sets

    observation structure

    transition structure

    opponent-response assumptions

    value mapping

    time structure

    feasibility structure

It answers:

    how the game works

    what moves exist

    how others respond

    how actions change relevant variables

    how states or trajectories affect values

This term replaces the earlier overloaded use of “world model.”
5. State Space

The set of possible configurations tracked by the interaction model.

This is not “everything true about the world.” It is only the space induced by the variables that matter in the model.

Examples of state dimensions:

    sanctions pressure

    deterrence balance

    proxy reach

    internal legitimacy

    escalation risk

6. State Variable

A dimension in state space.

Only include a state variable if it is used by at least one part of the interaction model.

Examples:

    sanctions pressure

    alliance credibility

    economic output

    proxy activity

    regional depth

A state variable is not a one-off event. It is a dimension that can vary and help locate the system.
7. State Estimate

An actor-attributed estimate of current values of relevant state variables.

This replaces any privileged “true world state” in the framework.

Examples:

    Iran estimates U.S. coercive pressure as high

    the U.S. estimates proxy reach as substantial

    an analyst estimates escalation risk as moderate

State estimates are always from some perspective.
8. Instrument

A tool, channel, or means of influence available within the interaction model.

Examples:

    sanctions authorities

    missile systems

    diplomatic channels

    proxy networks

    propaganda channels

Instrument is about available means, not effective power.

MIDFIELD fits here as a taxonomy for instruments.
9. MIDFIELD

A taxonomy for classifying instruments by channel of action:

    Military

    Intelligence

    Diplomacy

    Finance

    Information

    Economic

    Legal

    Development

MIDFIELD is not the architecture itself. It is a controlled vocabulary for the instrument layer.
10. Capability Assessment

A derived assessment of what an actor can effectively do in the interaction model from its current state estimate.

Capability is not just possession of tools. It depends on:

    instruments

    interaction model

    state estimate

    organizational competence

    likely responses by others

Examples:

    strong sanctions capability

    weak conventional strike capability

    moderate proxy activation capability

A capability assessment is game-relative, not absolute.
11. Constraint Assessment

A derived assessment of what actions or policies are effectively blocked, too costly, or too risky in the interaction model from the current state estimate.

Examples:

    direct war is too risky

    sanctions are binding

    conventional escalation is infeasible

    alliance obligations restrict flexibility

Constraint and capability are near-duals:

    capability = expected room to act effectively

    constraint = expected limit on acting effectively

12. Strategy

A policy or broad approach for acting in the interaction model.

A strategy is not a single act. It is a conditional or extended plan.

Examples:

    containment

    coercive pressure

    forward deterrence

    sanctions resistance

    balancing

    integration

Strategy is chosen relative to:

    values

    classification rules

    interaction model

    state estimate

    capability and constraint assessments

13. Objective Target

An intermediate target over state variables induced by a strategy.

Examples:

    lower sanctions pressure

    increase deterrent depth

    reduce proxy activity

    increase alliance credibility

Objective targets are not terminal values. They are waypoints or target regions in state space.

They are best treated as part of a strategy–objective package, not as fully independent primitives.
14. Action

A specific move taken by an actor.

Examples:

    sanction bank X

    deploy force Y

    arm proxy Z

    make threat A

    negotiate offer B

Action is narrower than strategy and usually uses one or more instruments.
15. State Transition

A change in state variables resulting from action and interaction.

Examples:

    sanctions pressure increases

    deterrence balance shifts

    escalation risk rises

    internal legitimacy falls

This is what earlier “outcome” was mostly pointing toward.
For v4, state transition is the cleaner term.
16. Derived planning structure

Not a primitive term, but an important summary:
Strategy–Objective Package

A strategy together with its induced intermediate targets over state variables.

This package is usually more accurate than forcing a strict order where objectives always come before strategy.
Distinction rules
Value vs Objective Target

    Value = intrinsically good or bad

    Objective target = a desired movement in state space because it is expected to improve value realization

Classification Rule vs Interaction Model

    Classification rule = what something counts as

    Interaction model = how states, actions, and other actors generate effects

Instrument vs Capability

    Instrument = available means/tool/channel

    Capability = expected effective use of instruments in the game

State Space vs State Estimate

    State space = possible configurations

    State estimate = where an actor thinks the system is in that space

Strategy vs Action

    Strategy = broad policy / conditional plan

    Action = specific move

Outcome vs State Transition

For this version, prefer state transition over “outcome.”
Extraction-oriented summary

If using this as an extraction schema, the main things to look for are:
Primitive-ish extraction targets

    Actor

    Value

    Classification Rule

    Interaction Model components

    State Variable

    State Estimate

    Instrument

Derived extraction targets

    Capability Assessment

    Constraint Assessment

    Strategy

    Objective Target

    Action

    State Transition

Minimal architecture summary

Actor
Values
Classification Rules
Interaction Model
State Estimate
    ↓
Capability / Constraint Assessments
    ↓
Strategy <-> Objective Targets
    ↓
Action via Instruments
    ↓
State Transition
    ↓
Updated State Estimate

I think this is the cleanest version so far.

So the game master, which kind of the point is about right for the game master kind of can keep usable systems both sides. I think this is a very promising use case, and your flag about there not being one interaction model is exactly right. In a game, you really want at least three layers:

    Player-side interaction model(s): what each side believes about the game

    Analyst-side estimates: advisory, uncertain, partial, possibly wrong

    Game-master resolution model: the hidden adjudication logic that produces realized transitions

That is much better than pretending there is one shared model. It also maps well to how real conflict works: actors act on partial models, not truth.

So the architecture we built can be repurposed pretty naturally for this. I’d frame it like this:
For each side

You maintain:

    values

    classification rules

    interaction-model estimate

    state estimate

    instruments / available actions

    capability and constraint assessments

    strategy / objective package

For the game master

You maintain:

    hidden state estimate

    hidden interaction-model estimate

    hidden modifiers and latent variables

    adjudication rules for state transitions

    uncertainty distributions rather than fixed outcomes

Then the loop is:

    player chooses action in natural language

    side analyst LM estimates likely effects and confidence

    game master LM resolves using its hidden state/model

    state transitions are revealed partially and asymmetrically

    each side updates its own state estimate and model estimate

That is a really nice fit for LLMs, because the hard part is not exact optimization, it is flexible interpretation, incomplete information, and natural-language policy reasoning.

A few things I’d strongly recommend if you build this:

First, separate advice from adjudication. The side analyst LM should never be the same role as the game-master LM. Otherwise you collapse uncertainty and hidden information.

Second, make the game master output not just outcomes, but also:

    visible effects

    hidden effects

    confidence

    what each side is allowed to observe

Third, treat capabilities and constraints as estimated by players, but as resolved by the game master. That matches your missile example exactly.

Fourth, keep the ontology lightweight. For gameplay, you probably do not need the whole schema exposed every turn. You want it in the backend as structure for prompts, extraction, memory, and adjudication, while the front-end interaction stays natural language.

So yes: I think what we built is less useful as a static descriptive ontology by itself than as backend scaffolding for an LLM-mediated strategic simulation system. That actually may be the strongest application so far.

The next useful step would be to sketch the role architecture:

    player

    side analyst

    game master

    state store / memory

    adjudication loop

Okay, and just to clarify, I do still want, like, a programmatic core for some of this. It's just kind of what makes the LLM so nice and, for the most part in this, is that players can act in natural language and they can ask their analyst about the observable information, which the game master determines what is observable potentially with, you know, the assistance of programmatic mechanisms. But, like, there's still a lot of, like, parameterization of the inputs into these programmatic aspects by the LLM, and I gave you the kind of trivial example of when I built this before where the LLM might determine the probability of some action being successful and then use a random number generator to determine, like, to be the role of the dice for that.

Yes — that hybrid approach makes sense.

The clean split is:

    LLM for interpretation and parameterization

        parse natural-language actions

        infer which instruments/capabilities/models are implicated

        estimate probabilities, ranges, hidden dependencies, observability

        explain reasoning in analyst-style language

    Programmatic core for state, rules, and resolution

        maintain canonical game state

        store hidden vs observable information by side

        enforce action schemas and constraints

        sample outcomes from probability distributions

        apply deterministic state updates where appropriate

        log turns and provenance

So the LLM is not replacing the engine; it is acting like:

    a semantic interface

    a structured estimator

    and sometimes a soft adjudication assistant

while the programmatic layer remains the source of persistence, turn order, hidden state, and randomness.

Your missile example is exactly the right pattern:

    player submits natural-language action

    analyst/game-master LLM maps it to structured fields

    LLM estimates success probabilities or outcome distributions given the current hidden/public state

    programmatic layer samples from that distribution

    programmatic layer updates canonical state

    each side gets only the observations they are allowed to see

    analyst LLM helps each side interpret the new situation

That also suggests a nice division of artifacts:

    Canonical state store

        variables

        hidden variables

        inventories / assets

        turn history

    Action schema

        actor

        target

        instrument(s)

        intended effect

        confidence / ambiguity flags

    Adjudication packet

        LLM-estimated probabilities

        assumptions used

        hidden modifiers

        RNG result

        resulting state transition

    Observation packet

        what each side sees

        confidence / ambiguity

        possible deception/noise

I think that is stronger than a pure LLM system and stronger than a pure rules engine. The LLM gives flexibility and richness; the programmatic core gives consistency and replayability.

The next useful step is probably to sketch the turn loop and data objects for this hybrid system.

Okay, proceed.

Here’s a clean hybrid turn loop + data object sketch.
1. Core roles
Player

Acts in natural language.

Example:

    “Move two carriers into the South China Sea and announce freedom of navigation operations.”

Side analyst LLM

Advises one side using only that side’s visible information.
Tasks:

    summarize observable state

    estimate likely effects

    suggest actions

    flag uncertainty

Game master LLM

Uses hidden/public state plus adjudication prompts to:

    interpret actions

    estimate outcome distributions

    propose state transitions

    determine observability

Programmatic core

Canonical source of truth for:

    state storage

    hidden/public partitions

    turn order

    RNG

    rule enforcement

    logs

2. Turn loop
Step 0: Current state exists

Programmatic core stores:

    canonical hidden state

    public state

    side-specific observable state

    turn history

Step 1: Player query/action

Player sends natural language.

Example:

    “Launch a limited missile strike on the carrier.”

    “Ask my analyst what China is likely to do if we sanction their banks.”

Step 2: Side analyst pass

If the player asks for advice, side analyst LLM receives:

    side-visible state

    side’s own model/values/doctrine if used

    recent history

Outputs:

    natural-language assessment

    optional structured estimate:

        likely outcomes

        uncertainty

        possible opponent responses

        candidate actions

Step 3: Action parsing

If the player commits to an action, either:

    side analyst LLM

    or a dedicated parser LLM

turns it into a structured action object.

Example fields:

    actor

    action type

    target

    instruments used

    intended effect

    scope

    timing

    ambiguity flags

Programmatic core validates:

    legal action type

    actor has relevant assets/instruments

    timing/turn legality

Step 4: Game master adjudication prep

Programmatic core sends game master LLM:

    canonical state

    hidden variables

    structured action

    recent history

    any system rules

    desired output schema

Game master LLM returns:

    interpretation of action

    relevant variables affected

    probability distributions / effect ranges

    hidden modifiers

    observability consequences

    recommended state transition packet

Step 5: Programmatic resolution

Programmatic core:

    applies deterministic updates where rules are fixed

    samples RNG for uncertain outcomes

    resolves contested effects

    updates canonical state

This is where your “flip a coin” or random number draw lives.
Step 6: Observation generation

Game master LLM or a smaller observation module determines:

    what each side can observe

    what remains hidden

    what appears noisy/ambiguous

    what public reporting says

Programmatic core stores:

    public observation packet

    side A observation packet

    side B observation packet

Step 7: State update and loop

Each side receives only its allowed observations.
Side analyst LLM can then interpret the new situation for that side.

Then next turn begins.
3. Minimal data objects
A. CanonicalState

Programmatic, hidden source of truth.

{
  "turn": 12,
  "state_variables": {
    "carrier_survivability_us": 0.72,
    "missile_salvo_capacity_china": 0.61,
    "regional_escalation_risk": 0.48
  },
  "assets": {
    "us_carrier_1": {"location": "SCS", "visible_to": ["US", "China_partial"]},
    "china_missile_brigade_3": {"location": "coastal", "visible_to": ["China"]}
  },
  "hidden_modifiers": {
    "us_countermeasure_bonus": 0.25
  }
}

B. SideStateView

What one side can see.

{
  "actor": "China",
  "observed_variables": {
    "carrier_presence_us": "high",
    "regional_escalation_risk": "moderate"
  },
  "known_assets": {
    "us_carrier_1": {"location": "SCS", "confidence": 0.8}
  },
  "uncertainties": [
    "unknown carrier defensive upgrades"
  ]
}

C. ActionIntent

Parsed player action.

{
  "actor": "China",
  "action_type": "limited_missile_strike",
  "target": "us_carrier_1",
  "instruments": ["anti_ship_missiles"],
  "intended_effect": "damage_or_deter",
  "scope": "limited",
  "timing": "current_turn"
}

D. AdjudicationPacket

Returned by game master LLM before RNG.

{
  "action_id": "act_203",
  "relevant_variables": [
    "carrier_survivability_us",
    "missile_salvo_capacity_china",
    "regional_escalation_risk"
  ],
  "estimated_effects": {
    "hit_probability": 0.30,
    "damage_if_hit": 0.65,
    "escalation_increase": 0.22
  },
  "hidden_modifiers_used": [
    "us_countermeasure_bonus"
  ],
  "observation_rules": {
    "China_observes": ["launch_result_partial"],
    "US_observes": ["incoming_attack_detected"],
    "Public_observes": ["regional_explosion_reports"]
  }
}

E. ResolutionRecord

Created programmatically after RNG.

{
  "action_id": "act_203",
  "rng_draws": {
    "hit_roll": 0.44
  },
  "resolved_effects": {
    "hit": false,
    "damage": 0.0,
    "regional_escalation_risk_delta": 0.22
  },
  "state_updates": {
    "regional_escalation_risk": 0.70
  }
}

F. ObservationPacket

Delivered to each side.

{
  "actor": "China",
  "turn": 12,
  "observations": [
    {
      "type": "strike_result",
      "content": "Impact uncertain; no confirmed carrier loss.",
      "confidence": 0.45
    }
  ]
}

4. LLM vs programmatic split
Best handled by LLM

    parse natural language

    infer intended action schema

    identify relevant variables

    estimate probabilities/ranges

    infer hidden dependencies

    generate side-specific briefings

    decide observability/noise in nuanced cases

Best handled programmatically

    state persistence

    hidden/public access control

    turn sequencing

    action validation

    RNG

    deterministic updates

    audit log / replay

    consistency checks

5. Where your ontology fits
In backend state / prompts
Values

Can shape:

    side analyst advice

    game-master interpretation of likely strategic intent

Classification rules

Can shape:

    how actions are interpreted

    what counts as coercion, escalation, deterrence, etc.

Interaction model

Can shape:

    adjudication prompts

    which variables matter

    what transition logic is assumed

Instruments

Map directly into action schemas.
Capability / constraint assessments

Can be generated by LLMs each turn or cached as structured estimates.
State estimates

Perfect for side analyst memory.
6. Minimal adjudication prompt pattern

For game master LLM:

    Current hidden state

    Public/side-visible state

    Structured action

    Relevant interaction model assumptions

    Required output schema:

        relevant variables

        probability estimates

        hidden modifiers

        observation consequences

This keeps the LLM bounded and auditable.
7. Good design rule

Use the LLM to produce:

    distributions

    ranked possibilities

    structured justifications

Use the programmatic core to produce:

    the realized branch

That’s the strongest hybrid.
8. Likely next step

The most useful next artifact is probably a JSON schema set for:

    CanonicalState

    SideStateView

    ActionIntent

    AdjudicationPacket

    ResolutionRecord

    ObservationPacket

That would make this much more implementable.

I kind of skimmed here, but I think we're missing some of the elements that we discussed previously that would really make this fun. Like, you can, like, the LLMs to assign each side as value propositions ahead of time, and it could take certain models to be the ground truth models for the game, stuff like that.

Yes — that’s a good catch. What I sketched was mostly the runtime loop, but not the scenario-generation / worldview-assignment layer, which is a big part of what would make this interesting.

I think you want two different uses of models in the system:
1. Scenario truth layer

This is the game-master layer.

Before the game starts, the system can assign:

    ground-truth value structures for each actor

    ground-truth classification rules

    ground-truth interaction-model components

        transition assumptions

        response tendencies

        hidden capabilities / constraints

    initial hidden state

These do not have to be fully “true” in any metaphysical sense; they are just the latent scenario parameters the game will use for adjudication.

So for example:

    China actually values dignity and regional primacy more than prosperity in this scenario

    U.S. actually has stronger countermeasures than China knows

    escalation actually triggers domestic rally effects above some threshold

    sanctions actually have weaker-than-expected bite because of hidden evasion channels

That gives the game a hidden backbone.
2. Actor belief / analyst layer

Then separately, each side gets:

    its own estimated values of the other side

    its own estimated interaction model

    its own state estimate

    its own analyst interpretation

This is where the fun comes from:

    players can be wrong about what the other side values

    players can be wrong about capabilities

    players can be wrong about the transition dynamics

So the real architecture is more like:
Pre-game generation

    generate latent scenario truth

    generate side-specific imperfect views of that truth

Runtime

    players act in natural language

    analysts advise from side-specific views

    game master resolves using latent truth

    observations are filtered back imperfectly

That’s much richer.
What should be assigned ahead of time

I’d add a scenario initialization layer with at least:

    actor value profiles

    actor classification-rule profiles

    actor interaction-model profiles

    hidden state variables

    observability rules

    stochasticity rules

    optional doctrine/personality style for each side analyst

And crucially:

    ground-truth adjudication model

    side-estimated model A

    side-estimated model B

That separation is probably one of the most important design features.
Why this makes it more fun

Because then the game is not just:

    choose action, get consequence

It becomes:

    infer what the other side actually values

    infer whether your analyst is over- or underestimating risk

    probe hidden state

    manipulate the other side’s model

    exploit misperception

That is much more like real strategy.
Clean role split with this added
Scenario generator

Sets latent truth:

    values

    models

    hidden state

    uncertainty structure

Side analyst

Operates on side-specific estimated world
Game master

Resolves using latent world
Programmatic core

Stores both:

    latent truth state

    side-visible belief states

That’s the missing layer.
My recommendation

The next artifact should probably be a scenario initialization schema, not more turn-loop detail. That schema should include:

    latent actor profiles

    latent interaction-model parameters

    side belief-model parameters

    hidden/public state partitions

That would connect the fun pregame design with the runtime system.

Yeah, I just wanna clarify that you probably wouldn't, in my mind, give the state estimate to each side. What you would do is give them observables from which they could estimate the state, which, I mean, the player could dig through it all if they wanted to, but they would probably use the analyst to help them think through it. And then probably the what makes things observable would be controlled by the LLM. I think that would be pretty difficult to, even if probabilistically, with the roll of the dice, maybe pretty hard to do, like a programmatic rules about what becomes observable in the same way that you could develop models, although potentially, because even the models, I think, are gonna be well underspecified and the LLMs are gonna have to make a lot of judgment calls. And one thing that's... Possible is to like, I don't know if it's good to have like a whole set of models and just randomly pick one, or you could have like a probability distribution across models that the player could have access to.
