So it's like, that's the way I'm imagining it's like a structured equation model that contains all plausible models. And then, you know, some of these times there's edges, sometimes there's not. Sometimes, like the edges can have different weights. And maybe what's the players are given is this meta model, and then the probability distribution across various implementations of it. So they might know that some model is more likely than another model, but they don't know what the underlying reality is.

Yes — that is a much better design.

You’re right: don’t give players a state estimate. Give them:

    observations

    uncertainty

    and maybe analyst help

Then state estimate is something each side constructs, not something the engine hands them. That is much more realistic and more fun.

So the cleaner stack is:

    latent scenario state

    observation-generation process

    side observations

    side analyst inference

    player decision

And yes, I agree that observability is probably too context-sensitive to hard-code cleanly. The LLM is better suited to deciding:

    what each side notices

    how noisy it is

    what is misattributed

    what remains hidden

with the programmatic layer still handling:

    persistence

    hidden/public partitions

    RNG

    logs

Your metamodel idea is also strong. I think that is the right term, or close enough. Instead of saying there is one true model or a bag of unrelated models, you define:

    A metamodel = a parameterized family of plausible models

where:

    some edges may exist or not

    some weights vary

    some hidden variables vary

    some response functions vary

Then the latent scenario samples one realization from that metamodel, while players may be given:

    the metamodel structure

    priors over parameters / edge existence

    maybe exemplar plausible instantiations

So they know the space of possibilities, but not which world they are in. That is much more elegant than “pick one model at random.”

The nicest architecture now looks like this:

    Metamodel

        variables

        possible edges

        possible weights

        possible hidden variables

        possible observability rules

    Scenario realization

        one sampled or selected parameterization of the metamodel

    Observation model

        generates side-specific observations from latent state

    Side analyst inference

        turns observations into provisional state/model estimates

    Player action

        natural language

    Game-master adjudication

        uses latent realization to update state

That is a very strong hybrid design. The next best step is probably to
sketch the metamodel schema: what objects and parameters it contains.

Okay, let's proceed. And I assume the way we would go about this is think through, just in the same way we're doing before, is what are the plausible terminal values? What are the models that can affect these values and what are kind of like the inputs into these models? And then, like, we're gonna work our way down to, like, what are the instruments that can affect the variables that are the inputs to the models that affect the terminal values, type thing. And the other thing I just want to flag is you can also, I mean, we can have other agents also. So because you said, like, the game master could determine misattribution, which I'm not a huge fan of the game master just, like, giving wrong information. I'm okay with him giving ambiguous information, especially if one side is trying to engage in deception against the other side. But it would be cool. you could have, like, an intelligence agency agent separate from the analyst agent, or you could have third countries or, like, all sorts of, you could have, like, agents representing the general populace of your country that might try and impose political pressure for all of these things, you know? And you could, we could have the same kind of meta-model for these, except really all you need is, like, prompting. So the public might have a certain prompt, and you don't know what that prompt is, you might even know the kind of the distribution of probabilities across that prompt. But, yeah.

Yes — I think that’s the right construction path.

And I agree with both of your flags:

    Build top-down from terminal values through models to variables to instruments

    Generalize “actor” beyond the two principal states

Those both make the system much stronger.
1. Top-down construction path

I think the metamodel should be built in this order:
A. Terminal values

Start by specifying a small set of terminal value dimensions.

Not an exhaustive philosophical list — just the dimensions the scenario family will allow actors to weight differently.

Examples:

    dignity / status

    flourishing / welfare

    autonomy / non-subordination

    holiness / purity

    justice / fairness

    continuity / survival of the collective

    order / stability, if treated terminally in that scenario

Then each actor gets:

    a weight vector over these values

    maybe some hard thresholds or taboo regions

B. Model family

Then define the family of plausible models that map:

    state/input variables

    actions

    trajectories
    into:

    changes in other variables

    and ultimately effects on value dimensions

As we said, this likely decomposes into:

    transition components

    opponent-response components

    value-realization components

And yes, the metamodel should allow:

    edge existence uncertainty

    weight uncertainty

    threshold uncertainty

    hidden-variable uncertainty

C. Model-input variables

Only then define the non-value variables.

A variable belongs only if:

    it is an input to at least one model component

    or it is a state dimension needed for action feasibility or observation

Examples:

    sanctions pressure

    force survivability

    internal legitimacy

    alliance cohesion

    proxy reach

    attribution confidence

    escalation risk

D. Instruments / action channels

Then define the instruments that can move those variables.

This is where MIDFIELD fits best:

    as a taxonomy of action channels

Examples:

    sanctions tools

    missile forces

    covert networks

    diplomatic channels

    information ops

    legal mechanisms

E. Feasibility / capability layer

Then derive:

    what each actor can probably do with those instruments under different modeled conditions

So yes, your intuition is right: the design should cascade from:
values -> models -> variables -> instruments -> feasible actions

Not the reverse.
2. Generalizing beyond two principal states

I strongly agree. The architecture should not assume just:

    player A

    player B

    analyst A

    analyst B

    game master

Instead, it should allow a set of agent roles, each with:

    a perspective

    a value profile or prompt prior

    an observation channel

    an action set, possibly tiny

    a model or partial model

    influence on state transitions or observables

So you can have:
Principal actors

    U.S.

    China

Advisory actors

    U.S. analyst

    China analyst

    intelligence agencies

    military planners

Environmental / third-party actors

    Taiwan

    Japan

    Russia

    Philippines

    private shipping firms

    global media

Domestic pressure actors

    public opinion

    political opposition

    military hawks

    markets

    elite coalitions

That is a huge improvement, because then:

    misperception does not have to be “game master lies”

    it can arise from partial observability, deception, noisy signals, third-party reports, domestic filtering, etc.

That is much better.
3. On misattribution and ambiguity

I agree with your discomfort.

The game master should generally not invent falsehoods arbitrarily.

Better sources of incorrect belief are:

    ambiguous observations

    noisy signals

    hidden state

    deception actions by another actor

    biased analyst interpretation

    domestic or institutional filtering

    incomplete observability

So the game master should mostly control:

    what is observable

    what is hidden

    what is noisy

    what is delayed

    what is spoofed by another actor

That is very different from “the game master just tells a side something false.”

I think that is the right design norm.
4. Prompt-defined agents

Yes — this is a very natural extension.

Some agents do not need a full heavy ontology instance. They can be represented by:

    a role

    a prompt / value prior

    a partial model

    an influence channel

Examples:
Public opinion agent

    values: security, prosperity, casualty aversion, nationalism

    action channel: political pressure, approval shifts, protest intensity

    observability: mostly public signals

    model: reactive, noisy, limited horizon

Intelligence agency agent

    values: maybe not terminal in same way; more role-driven

    action channel: collection, interpretation, warning, covert distortion

    observability: privileged access

    model: uncertainty-heavy, attribution-focused

Third-country actor

    own value weights

    own state estimate

    own action channels

    may be neutral, opportunistic, allied, or deceptive

That means the architecture should probably distinguish:

    full actors: can pursue broad strategies

    partial / role actors: can influence narrower subsets of variables or observations

5. Metamodel schema sketch

Here’s the clean version.
Metamodel contains:
A. Value dimensions

    list of terminal value axes

    optional taboo/threshold rules

B. Variable pool

    candidate model-input variables

    domains and admissible ranges

C. Model family

    possible transition edges

    possible response functions

    possible value-realization mappings

    uncertainty over edge existence and weights

D. Observation family

    who can observe what

    noise structure

    delay structure

    deception susceptibility

E. Instrument/action family

    action channels by MIDFIELD tag

    preconditions

    costs

    affected variables

F. Agent family

For each agent type:

    role

    value-weight prior

    model prior

    observation profile

    action profile

    influence channels

G. Scenario realization

A sampled or selected instantiation of:

    actor weights

    active model edges

    hidden variables

    observability parameters

    initial state

6. Clean conceptual loop

So the full game becomes:
Before play

    define metamodel

    sample scenario realization

    generate side-specific partial information and role prompts

During play

    players receive observations, not truth

    side analysts infer likely state/model

    players act in natural language

    game master adjudicates using latent realization

    observation model reveals noisy/partial consequences

    domestic/third-party agents may also act or update

    repeat

That is really strong.
7. What I’d do next

The next best step is probably not more abstract ontology refinement.

It is to create a metamodel template with fields like:

    value dimensions

    variable pool

    model-edge family

    observation rules

    agent roster

    instrument roster

    scenario priors

That would make this much more actionable.

I guess now I'm kind of getting skeptical of whether the metamodel can ever actually do what I want or whether it just will just fundamentally limit the creativity of players in the game, as opposed to having more of a prompt for kind of the model that the world operates under. Because, like, if we did go a programmatic route, I would want it to be in a way where if a player comes up with some novel, plausible input variable or some novel, plausible instrument to affect some input variable that the game could, say, like, could incorporate that on the fly rather than just saying, hey, we didn't plan for you to do this, so we can't make that move.

Yes — that skepticism is warranted.

A rigid metamodel would absolutely risk turning the game into “choose from the menu,” which is the opposite of what makes LLM-mediated play interesting. I think the right answer is:

    Use the metamodel as a soft scaffold, not a closed action grammar.

So instead of saying:

    only these variables exist

    only these instruments exist

    only these moves are legal

you say:

    these are the default variables, instruments, and model components

    but the LLM/game master can propose schema extensions on the fly

That suggests a better architecture:
Closed core, open edge

Have a small canonical core that is programmatically stable:

    agent identities

    hidden/public state store

    turn order

    RNG

    observation channels

    a baseline variable set

    a baseline action/intervention schema

Then allow an open extension layer where novel things can be introduced if they satisfy a few tests:

    is this a plausible new variable?

    is this a plausible new instrument/channel?

    does it connect to existing models or require a local model patch?

    is it redundant with an existing variable/instrument?

So if a player invents a clever move, the system should not say “not allowed.” It should say something like:

    here is the closest existing schema match, or

    here is a proposed extension:

        new instrument

        affected variable(s)

        observability implications

        rough effect distribution

Then the game master LLM and programmatic core can decide whether to:

    map it to existing structures

    extend the schema locally for this scenario

    or reject it as incoherent

Best design principle

Think of the metamodel as:

    an ontology of defaults
    not

    a whitelist of everything possible

Practical rule

Every player action goes through one of three outcomes:

    Direct match to existing schema

    Analogical match to nearest existing schema

    Schema extension proposal

That is probably the sweet spot.
What the programmatic layer does in that world

The engine still handles:

    persistence

    hidden state

    RNG

    action logs

    side-specific observations

But the LLM layer can dynamically:

    add a temporary variable

    add a temporary instrument

    add a local causal edge

    propose a confidence-weighted effect model

So the system stays creative without becoming shapeless.
My opinion

You probably do not want a fully enumerated metamodel.
You want:

    a minimal core metamodel

    plus a controlled improvisation mechanism

That feels much more aligned with your actual goal.

The next useful step is probably to define the on-the-fly extension protocol:

    when a novel move appears

    how the system decides whether to map, extend, or reject it.


I guess, and I don't have a strong opinion on this, but I'm wondering, like, why even have the default or the ontology of defaults if we're going to allow extras? It just seems like if we have the ability to handle things we didn't think of, then maybe, yeah, we don't even need the whitelist of defaults, but I'm not really sure how this would work in practice. Like, if it was just a prompt on kind of what the, like, fundamental world model was. Yeah, I just, it's hard for me to think through the trade-offs here.

That trade-off is real. I think there are basically three designs, and your discomfort is pointing at the middle one.
1. Fully pre-specified schema

Pros:

    consistency

    easier state tracking

    easier coding

    easier replay/audit

Cons:

    brittle

    menu-like

    kills creativity

    forces premature ontology choices

2. Pure prompt-world

Pros:

    maximally flexible

    very creative

    easiest to prototype

Cons:

    drift

    inconsistent adjudication

    weak memory/state discipline

    hard to debug

    hard to compare games

3. Thin canonical spine

This is probably the sweet spot.

You do not predefine every variable, instrument, or model edge.
You only predefine the minimum needed for continuity:

    actors / roles

    hidden vs public information partitions

    turn structure

    canonical event log

    RNG / adjudication hook

    a few anchoring state dimensions if the scenario clearly needs them

    the scenario prompt describing the world’s governing logic

Then everything else can be created on the fly, but when it is created, it gets written into the canonical state/log in a structured way.

So the ontology of defaults is not there to limit imagination. It is there to give the system:

    memory

    persistence

    comparability

    bookkeeping

The key distinction is:

    Default ontology should anchor continuity, not constrain possibility.

In practice, that means a player can invent a novel move, and the LLM can say:

    this introduces a new variable

    this introduces a new instrument

    this adds a new hidden modifier
    and the engine simply stores it.

So the real alternative is not “whitelist vs no whitelist.”
It is:

    closed ontology
    vs

    open ontology with canonicalization

I think you want the second.

A good way to think about it is:

    the scenario prompt defines the broad world logic

    the runtime ontology is partially emergent

    the programmatic core records whatever becomes real in play

So yes, you may not need much of a default ontology beyond:

    type categories

    storage schema

    adjudication protocol

Not:

    full variable lists

    full instrument lists

    full model lists

That suggests the next useful artifact is not a metamodel full of defaults, but a canonicalization protocol:
when a new thing appears, how do we name it, type it, relate it, and store it so the game remains coherent?

Okay, even your thin canonical spine doesn't really, those are more, so I was thinking those aren't really the way I was thinking about models, as in inputs, variables to models that had some sort of causal outcome, like an SEM on either terminal values or intermediate variables that could, that could, had a pathway to terminal values. But then I see you did say a few anchoring state dimensions, but the whole thing is, either we're gonna do this programmatically with like an SEM and maybe like a non-deterministic stochastic SEM, and if we're adding new things, then we need to be able to like update the SEM, I guess, which maybe isn't super difficult, and actually, yeah, I do think your thin canonical spine is, yeah, I just don't know.

Yeah — I think you’ve isolated the actual fork.

There are really two different kinds of spine we’ve been blurring:

    Game-engine spine

        actors

        turns

        observability

        logs

        RNG

        hidden/public state

    Causal-model spine

        variables

        edges

        weights

        update rules

        stochasticity

Your concern is about the second, and I think it’s the right concern.

If the real heart of the system is:

    “actions change variables, variables propagate through a causal structure, and eventually affect terminal values,”

then yes, the right formal picture is much closer to:

    a stochastic SEM

    or a dynamic causal graph

    possibly with game-theoretic policies on top

And then the real question is:

    Can that causal graph be open to extension during play?

I think the answer is yes, but only if you separate:
Closed semantics, open ontology

Meaning:
Closed semantics

You fix the types of things that can exist and the ways they can relate:

    variable

    action

    actor

    causal edge

    observation edge

    hidden variable

    value variable

And:

    action can affect variable

    variable can affect variable

    variable can affect value

    observation can reveal variable noisily

    etc.

Open ontology

You do not pre-fix which variables or edges exist.
Those can be added during play.

So if a player invents a new plausible variable, you do not break the engine. You:

    create a new node

    type it

    connect it with proposed causal edges

    assign provisional weights/distributions

    store it in the causal graph

That actually seems quite workable.

So the SEM is not fixed in content; it is fixed in schema.

That may be the cleanest answer.
So what the “thin canonical spine” should be

Not:

    a predefined list of strategic variables

But:

    a predefined causal grammar

Something like:
Node types

    value variable

    intermediate variable

    hidden variable

    action

    observation

    actor

Edge types

    causal influence

    intervention

    observation/noise

    value-realization

Edge attributes

    sign

    weight/range

    uncertainty

    conditionality

Update rules

    deterministic

    stochastic

    thresholded

    sampled

That way, when a player introduces a novel move, you are not asking:

    “was this variable on our whitelist?”

You are asking:

    “can this be represented in the causal grammar?”

That feels much better.
Practical implication

So instead of a metamodel that says:

    here are all the variables

you want a metamodel that says:

    here is the kind of graph the world can be represented with

Then the LLM can dynamically:

    add nodes

    add edges

    modify edge weights

    create hidden modifiers

And the programmatic layer can still:

    store the graph

    sample transitions

    keep history coherent

My current best formulation

You probably want:

    A dynamic stochastic causal graph with game-theoretic actors acting on it.

Where:

    the graph schema is fixed

    the graph contents are extensible

That gives you:

    creativity

    persistence

    causal structure

    programmability

without requiring a giant fixed variable list.
What I think next

The next most useful thing is probably to define that causal grammar explicitly:

    node types

    edge types

    allowed edge directions

    what can be added on the fly

    how an LLM proposes a graph patch

    how the engine accepts and stores it

That would resolve this fork much better than continuing to debate fixed vs open models abstractly.

Let's do a stress test of like an example. So I guess lay out, you know, a simple causal grammar and world model, and then, you know, a move or two within that map clearly into the schema and then a more creative move which is possible but which the schema didn't pre-predict.

Good idea. Let’s do a small stress test with:

    a minimal causal grammar

    a tiny world model

    two ordinary moves that fit cleanly

    one creative move the schema did not pre-enumerate, but can still absorb

1. Minimal causal grammar

We want the grammar to be closed in semantics, open in content.
Node types

    Actor

    ValueVariable

    StateVariable

    Action

    Observation

Edge types

    ACTS_ON: Action -> StateVariable

    CAUSES: StateVariable -> StateVariable

    REALIZES: StateVariable -> ValueVariable

    OBSERVES: StateVariable -> Observation

    INFORMS: Observation -> StateEstimate
    We can keep this implicit for now.

Edge attributes

    sign: positive / negative

    weight: weak / medium / strong or numeric

    uncertainty: low / medium / high

    conditionality: optional text/rule

Update logic

When an action occurs:

    it intervenes on one or more state variables

    those propagate through causal edges

    some observations are generated

    actors update their estimates

That’s enough for a stress test.
2. Tiny world model

Let’s use a stripped-down U.S.–Iran-like setting.
Actors

    US

    Iran

Value variables

    Security_US

    Dignity_Iran

State variables

    SanctionsPressure

    ProxyReach

    EscalationRisk

    DeterrenceBalance

Causal edges

    SanctionsPressure CAUSES EscalationRisk
    sign: positive, weight: medium, uncertainty: medium

    ProxyReach CAUSES DeterrenceBalance
    sign: positive for Iran, weight: strong

    ProxyReach CAUSES EscalationRisk
    sign: positive, weight: medium

    DeterrenceBalance REALIZES Dignity_Iran
    sign: positive, weight: medium

    EscalationRisk REALIZES Security_US
    sign: negative, weight: strong

That gives us a very small causal world.
3. Pre-enumerated ordinary moves
Move A: U.S. imposes stronger sanctions

Action:

    Action_US_SanctionBanking

Edges:

    Action_US_SanctionBanking ACTS_ON SanctionsPressure

        sign: positive

        weight: strong

Resolution

    SanctionsPressure goes up

    EscalationRisk tends to go up through the CAUSES edge

    Higher EscalationRisk lowers Security_US through the REALIZES edge

This already shows an interesting effect:

    the action may be intended to help U.S. goals

    but under this model it also carries a negative risk channel

Move B: Iran expands proxy support

Action:

    Action_Iran_ExpandProxySupport

Edges:

    Action_Iran_ExpandProxySupport ACTS_ON ProxyReach

        sign: positive

        weight: strong

Resolution

    ProxyReach goes up

    DeterrenceBalance rises

    EscalationRisk also rises

    Higher DeterrenceBalance raises Dignity_Iran

    Higher EscalationRisk lowers Security_US

This maps cleanly.
4. Creative move not pre-predicted

Now suppose the U.S. player says:

    “We quietly leak evidence that Iran’s proxies are siphoning funds from local populations, hoping to damage Iran’s influence without direct escalation.”

This was not in the pre-enumerated schema.

It introduces something like:

    information operation

    legitimacy effect

    maybe proxy cohesion effect

Step 1: LLM proposes graph extension

New action:

    Action_US_LeakProxyCorruptionEvidence

New state variable:

    ProxyLegitimacy

New causal edges:

    Action_US_LeakProxyCorruptionEvidence ACTS_ON ProxyLegitimacy

        sign: negative

        weight: medium

        uncertainty: high

    ProxyLegitimacy CAUSES ProxyReach

        sign: positive

        weight: medium

        uncertainty: medium

Optional further edge:

    ProxyLegitimacy CAUSES Dignity_Iran or perhaps an intermediate variable like regional influence

        maybe positive, weak

Step 2: Engine accepts patch

The engine does not say “illegal move.”
It says:

    this is representable in the grammar

    add one new variable

    add two new edges

    assign provisional weights/uncertainty

Step 3: Resolution

    ProxyLegitimacy decreases

    Lower ProxyLegitimacy reduces ProxyReach

    Lower ProxyReach weakens DeterrenceBalance

    Lower ProxyReach may also reduce EscalationRisk

So this creative move can now be adjudicated inside the same grammar.
5. Why this is a useful stress test
What worked

The causal grammar handled:

    ordinary kinetic/financial moves

    a novel information move

    new variables added on the fly

without changing the semantics of the engine.
What got exposed

A few things quickly appear:
A. Value variables are still sparse

This is good. We did not need many.
B. State variables are model-relative

Also good. ProxyLegitimacy only got added because the move required it.
C. Actions do not need to be pre-whitelisted

They only need to be translatable into:

    interventions on variables

    plus possible graph patches

D. The hard part is patch quality

The real question becomes:

    when is a proposed new variable legitimate?

    when is it redundant with an old one?

    how are weights assigned?

That is exactly where the LLM/game-master judgment comes in.
6. Programmatic interpretation

The programmatic core can still do this cleanly.
Stored objects

    node list

    edge list

    current variable values

    uncertainty distributions

    turn history

On creative move

The LLM proposes:

    new node(s)

    new edge(s)

    intervention weight(s)

The engine:

    validates type correctness

    stores the patch

    samples outcomes

    updates state

So the schema remains stable while the graph contents evolve.
7. One more even more creative move

Suppose Iran says:

    “We stage a controlled cyber incident that appears to come from a rogue militia to test whether the U.S. can attribute attacks correctly.”

Now we may need:

    AttributionConfidence

    PublicAmbiguity

    maybe an observation-layer patch, not just a causal patch

So the engine would add:

New state variable:

    AttributionConfidence

Edges:

    Action_Iran_FalseFlagCyberTest ACTS_ON AttributionConfidence

        sign: negative

    AttributionConfidence CAUSES EscalationRisk

        maybe negative or positive depending on model

    AttributionConfidence OBSERVES Observation_US_IntelAssessment

        noisy channel

This shows the grammar may also need observation-side variables, not just causal state variables.

That’s a valuable stress-test finding.
8. What this suggests

This example supports the idea that you want:

    a fixed causal grammar

    an extensible graph

    LLM-proposed graph patches

    programmatic storage and resolution

And it also suggests the grammar probably needs at least one more edge family:

    REVEALS / NOISILY_OBSERVES for observability and attribution dynamics

9. Best next move

The next high-value artifact is probably a graph patch protocol:

When a move introduces something novel, the LLM must output:

    new nodes

    node types

    new edges

    edge types

    weights/uncertainties

    justification

    whether patch is temporary or persistent

That would make this much more implementable.

I don't think escalation risk is a state. Escalation is an action, and whether the risk of that action depends on who you're playing against. So if you're playing against an AI, that might make sense, but I think like escalation risk, even against an AI, is better encoded in the prompt.

I think that’s a strong correction. Escalation risk is probably not a state variable in the core graph. It’s better treated as a derived assessment inside the interaction model: a judgment about how likely some actor is to choose an escalation action given the current state, its values, and its model. So the state might include things like force posture, proxy reach, attribution confidence, domestic pressure, deterrence balance, and so on. Then “escalation risk” is computed from those through the actor/opponent model, not stored as a basic state variable.

That points to a cleaner split:

    State variables = things the game tracks directly.

    Action policies / response propensities = encoded in the interaction-model prompt or policy function.

    Risk estimates = analyst or game-master outputs derived from the model, not canonical state.

So in the earlier toy example, I’d replace EscalationRisk with more concrete tracked variables like:

    force vulnerability

    attribution confidence

    domestic pressure

    deterrence balance

    public ambiguity
    Then the prompt/model says something like: “Given these variables, Iran is more/less likely to escalate asymmetrically.” That fits your instinct much better, especially in a human or LLM-mediated game, where the interesting part is not a stored scalar called escalation risk, but the conditional behavior rule of the other side.

So yes: I’d demote “escalation risk” from the ontology and move it into the interaction-model prompt / derived analyst assessment layer. That’s a useful refinement. The next step should probably be to restate the causal grammar with a stricter rule: only track concrete state variables; keep policy propensities and action-likelihood judgments in the model layer unless there is a strong reason to materialize them.

I still don't think these are, let's leave out non-player characters outside of like the game master and the analyst, so all actions within the world are kind of taken by characters. And then I think non-agent characters, you just want them to be a prompt. But I'm thinking more something like if you tariff some good, then there will be, you know, a negative hit to both economies of some magnitude. Yeah, I'm not sure how to really define what I'm saying, but just things like attribution, confidence, attribution is, it's like an assessment of one of the players of the game. It's not a variable within the world state. Domestic pressure could be once we get into non-player characters, but let's leave out the non-player characters for the initial stress test. Deterrence balance, again, deterrence is, to be deterred is like a strategic choice by one of the players, not a state within the game.

Yes — I think that’s a real correction, and I’d adopt it.

You’re drawing a cleaner boundary:

    State variables should be things that are in the game world independent of a player’s judgment.

    Things like attribution confidence, deterrence, and escalation risk are not world-state variables; they are assessments, expectations, or policy responses of actors.

I think that’s right.

So for an initial stress test with only:

    player A

    player B

    analyst

    game master

the ontology should probably track only fairly concrete variables like:

    tariff level

    trade volume

    carrier location

    missile inventory

    sanctions intensity

    shipping disruption

    GDP loss or industrial output change

    force readiness

    observed damage

Then the interaction-model prompt handles things like:

    whether player B feels deterred

    whether player A attributes an attack to player B

    whether either side chooses to escalate

    whether a move changes resolve, fear, caution, etc.

So a cleaner rule is:

    Track concrete world conditions in state. Keep beliefs, assessments, propensities, and strategic interpretations in the model/prompt layer.

Your tariff example is a good one:

    state variable: tariff rate on category X

    state transition: trade volume drops, domestic prices rise, export revenue falls

    model-layer interpretation: this may increase willingness to retaliate, or increase caution, depending on the actor model

Likewise:

    carrier moved into region = state

    missile salvo launched = action

    damage to carrier = state transition

    deterrence effect = model-layer judgment about future action choice

So I’d now tighten the grammar to something like:
Node types

    Actor

    ValueVariable

    ConcreteStateVariable

    Action

    Observation

Edge types

    ACTS_ON: Action -> ConcreteStateVariable

    CAUSES: ConcreteStateVariable -> ConcreteStateVariable

    REALIZES: ConcreteStateVariable -> ValueVariable

    OBSERVES: ConcreteStateVariable -> Observation

And then separately:
Prompt/model layer

    actor response rules

    attribution judgments

    deterrence judgments

    escalation choices

    confidence estimates

Those are not canonical state nodes unless you later decide to materialize them for engineering reasons.

I think this is a better design. It keeps the stored state cleaner and pushes the fuzzier strategic-psychological content into the analyst / game-master inference layer, where it probably belongs.

The next useful move is probably to rerun the toy stress test one more time with this stricter rule: only concrete state variables are allowed in the graph.

OK, but again, there is no, I don't know if there is an interaction model. If we're leaving out non-player characters and it's just me and my friend playing against each other, the interaction model is within the kind of, it's purely between me and my friend. It's not within the game unless I'm missing something. So it's like, if I'm, like, to say that if I'm playing my friend, who's Player B, that the game is going to have something in the code that determines whether my friend feels deterred is just seems fundamentally to be missing the point.

Yes — I think that’s exactly right, and it’s an important correction.

If the opposing sides are human players, then there is no need for the engine to contain an “interaction model” of whether your friend feels deterred, coerced, humiliated, reassured, etc. That is the player’s job. Putting that in the code would collapse the game into single-player adjudication and remove the strategic psychology that makes it interesting.

So the clean split is:
What belongs in the game engine

Only things that are not chosen by the players themselves, like:

    concrete state variables

    hidden state

    observability

    physical/economic causal consequences

    stochastic resolution

    bookkeeping

Examples:

    tariff rate

    ship location

    missile inventory

    shipping disruption

    industrial output hit

    damage

    public facts vs hidden facts

What belongs to the players

    whether they feel deterred

    whether they escalate

    whether they reinterpret the other side’s move

    whether they bluff

    whether they back down

That is not state in the game; that is human agency.

So for a two-player human game, the engine should mostly model:

    the environment and consequences of actions, not the internal response function of the players.

That means “interaction model” was too broad for this use case. More precise terms would be:

    world dynamics model or environment model

        how tariffs affect trade

        how missile strikes affect assets

        how blockades affect shipping

        how sanctions affect output

and then separately:

    player strategy/psychology

        left to the human players

        optionally discussed with their analyst LLM

So yes: if it’s you and your friend, the engine should not decide whether your friend is deterred. It should decide things like:

    whether the strike hit

    how much damage occurred

    what became observable

    what changed materially in the environment

Then your friend decides what to do next.

That’s a much cleaner design.
What the analyst LLM still does

The side analyst can still say things like:

    “This move is likely to make Player B more cautious”

    “This may be seen as escalatory”

    “This could weaken their willingness to intervene”

But that remains advice, not canonical state.
Revised architecture

For the human-vs-human version, I’d simplify to:

    Values

    Concrete state variables

    Environment/world-dynamics model

    Instruments/actions

    Observability rules

    RNG/programmatic resolution

And leave:

    deterrence

    attribution confidence

    escalation propensity

    resolve

to:

    the players

    or their analyst assistants

So yes — your objection is right. The engine should model what the world does, not what the players feel.

Yes, I think that’s right.

For non-player characters, the cleanest design is: they are not special ontology objects with different logic. They are just agents played by prompts. So a human player and an NPC differ only in who supplies the decision:

    human player = human chooses

    NPC = prompted LLM chooses

They should see:

    the same observation packet

    the same constraints on hidden information

    the same available actions

The only extra thing the NPC gets is a role prompt or value/personality prompt.

On hidden state, I’d avoid thinking of it as “the game knows the true hidden world” in a strong metaphysical sense. It is more like:

    there is a canonical scenario record

    some fields in that record are visible to one side, both sides, or neither side

    observations are generated from that record plus rules/judgment

Your submarine example is a very good one because it shows what the engine should do. The engine should not decide psychology; it should decide geometry, visibility, and contact opportunities.

So the engine/core should handle things like:

    asset location, region, route, readiness

    whether paths intersect

    whether a sensor/platform is in a position to observe

    whether observation succeeds automatically, probabilistically, or not at all

That suggests a cleaner split for the two-player game:
Engine handles

    canonical record of positions, inventories, and concrete conditions

    movement and location logic

    visibility / observability logic

    stochastic resolution where needed

    what each side is told

Analyst/players handle

    interpretation

    deterrence judgments

    escalation choices

    deception planning

    strategic meaning

So in your submarine case:

    if deployment is vague, the engine uses coarse region-level logic and uncertainty

    if deployment is specific, the engine uses more precise contact logic

    then it generates an observation packet like:

        “possible surface contact detected”

        “high-confidence sighting”

        or nothing

That feels like the right design.

The key idea is:

    the engine adjudicates opportunity for observation, not the meaning of the observation.

And similarly:

    the engine adjudicates material consequences, not the player’s reaction to them.

That is probably the cleanest form of the hybrid system.

The next best move is probably to sketch the minimum canonical record for this two-player game:

    assets

    locations

    routes/orders

    observability rules

    public/side-specific observation packets


Yeah, I think the other cool thing is, I mean, you're gonna have a lot of non-player characters, and you could use, like, espionage and stuff. So, I mean, you could have, like, so, you know, you could give the people in the Iranian military different profiles, and then the U.S. could spend energy spying on these people, and maybe one of them has some blackmailable opportunity, or they could be, the U.S. thinks they can be turned based on what their spy agents are doing, and they could go try and flip that guy, but he might actually be a double agent feeding disinformation from the Iranian side and stuff like that.

Yes — that’s exactly the kind of thing this architecture could make unusually fun.

What you’re describing is basically an agent-rich hidden-information layer on top of the physical/geopolitical game:

    named or semi-named individuals

    factions

    loyalty/ambition/corruption/ideology traits

    espionage actions that reveal, distort, or exploit those traits

    counterintelligence and double-agent dynamics

And I agree with your earlier point: these should still mostly behave like prompted agents with hidden profiles, not like rigid scripted objects.

So the clean way to think about it is:
Two coupled layers
1. External strategic layer

    ships

    missiles

    sanctions

    trade

    deployments

    observability

2. Internal human-network layer

    officers

    intelligence officials

    politicians

    oligarchs

    clerics

    business elites

    journalists

    factions

Then espionage actions operate mostly on layer 2:

    collect dossier

    assess recruitability

    plant rumor

    offer bribe

    threaten exposure

    test loyalty

    run deception channel

And the effects from layer 2 can propagate into layer 1:

    leak plans

    sabotage readiness

    distort observations

    shift orders

    slow response

    trigger purges

That’s really strong.
Why this works well with LLMs

Because these NPCs do not need fully hard-coded psychologies. They can have:

    hidden profile fields

    prompt-level motives

    some persistent memory

    and role-constrained observations

So an Iranian officer could have hidden traits like:

    ambition: high

    ideology: medium

    corruption tolerance: high

    family vulnerability: moderate

    loyalty to regime faction A: high

Then the U.S. player’s intel action doesn’t get a clean answer like “recruitable: yes/no.” Instead it gets:

    ambiguous evidence

    confidence estimates

    possible leverage points

And then if they try to flip him, the game master adjudicates whether:

    he turns

    he stalls

    he reports the approach

    he becomes a double agent

That is much richer than a standard war game.
Clean design rule

Keep three things separate:
1. Hidden profile

Stored canonically by the engine/game master.
2. Observed indicators

What the spying side sees.
3. Interpretation

What the analyst says those indicators probably mean.

That prevents the game master from just handing out truth, while still allowing espionage to matter.
Minimal profile schema for espionage NPCs

You do not need much at first. Something like:

    role

    faction

    access level

    ambition

    ideology

    corruption susceptibility

    fear / risk tolerance

    current stress

    hidden loyalties

    possible vulnerabilities

    possible deception status

That is enough to generate a ton of interesting play.
Best part

This also solves your earlier concern about arbitrary falsehoods.
The game master is not “making things up” so much as:

    tracking hidden profile state

    generating observations from it

    letting players infer incorrectly if they misread the signals

That feels much fairer and more game-like.
Main caution

This can explode in complexity very fast.

So I’d probably make this:

    optional

    sparse

    focused on only a few strategically important NPCs per side at first

Otherwise the game becomes an intelligence soap opera instead of a strategy game.
My recommendation

This is a very strong future extension, but probably as:

    a named-agent espionage module
    on top of the simpler two-player strategic core.

The next useful artifact, if you want to keep going in this direction, is probably a minimal NPC espionage schema with:

    hidden traits

    observation types

    recruitment/deception actions

    possible outcomes.


Well, I mean, it could be an intelligence soap opera, but I mean, my idea is that each player gets, like, you know, a limited time turn. And so all of the time you spend collecting information and playing the intelligence game, you're not playing what might be more important games of economics or, you know, domestic political messaging or military strategy or thinking through research and development tracks or whatever.

Exactly — and that’s what makes it strategically interesting rather than bloated.

The intelligence layer does not have to be “extra content.” It can be a competing use of scarce turn resources. Then espionage becomes one domain among several:

    military moves

    diplomacy

    economic policy

    domestic messaging

    R&D

    espionage / counterintelligence

So the real game resource is something like:

    turn time

    action slots

    attention budget

    political capital

    maybe analyst bandwidth

Then the tradeoff is the point:

    spend a turn flipping an officer, or

    spend it deploying forces, or

    spend it on tariff policy, or

    spend it on public signaling

That means the “intelligence soap opera” only takes over if the player chooses to make it central, which is actually good design.

This also suggests a very clean principle for the system:

    Every action, including information actions, should compete for the same scarce decision budget.

That keeps espionage from being a free bonus layer.

It also means you probably want a shared action economy:

    each turn, each side gets a limited number of major actions

    maybe some minor/free actions

    all domains draw from the same pool

Then espionage is not special. It is just another class of action with:

    uncertain payoff

    delayed payoff

    asymmetric information value

That’s actually elegant.

So yes, I think your instinct is right: the intelligence game becomes valuable because it trades off against other games, not because it is separately capped. The next useful design artifact is probably a turn economy schema:

    action budget

    action classes

    time-to-effect

    observability

    reversibility

    domain tags (military, economic, intel, etc.)


Well, in my mind, I don't want turns to be limited by actions. I want them to be limited by resources, essentially. Like you could, you know, yeah, I mean, in my mind, in the full game, although I think it's important to work carefully towards this, like you could have a spymaster agent just like the president does, and you don't spend time micromanaging his, what he is doing, but you can give him broad kind of intelligence policy instructions and things to focus on. And, you know, you never get to see the prompt of your intelligence chief or any other character in the game. Yes — that’s a much richer design, and it points away from a turn economy based on “action slots” and toward a resource-and-delegation economy.

That means the core scarce things are more like:

    money / material resources

    time

    attention

    political capital

    organizational capacity

    trust in subordinates

    maybe legitimacy / public tolerance

Then actions are not capped directly. They are capped indirectly by whether you have:

    the resources to fund them

    the bandwidth to direct them

    the institutions to execute them

    the right agents in place

That’s a much better fit for the kind of game you’re describing.
What this implies structurally

You probably want three layers of play:
1. Principal layer

The player acts like president / supreme leader / top leadership.
They do not micromanage everything. They:

    set priorities

    allocate resources

    appoint / fire / promote

    issue broad directives

    personally intervene on critical decisions

2. Agent layer

Subordinate agents carry out policy under hidden prompts and varying competence:

    intelligence chief

    military chief

    trade minister

    propaganda head

    science/R&D lead

    regional commander

The player can influence them, but not fully control them.
3. World layer

The environment resolves:

    material consequences

    observation opportunities

    hidden developments

    stochastic events

That’s a very strong architecture.
Why this is better than action slots

Because it creates the exact kind of strategic tension you want:

    Do I spend attention personally managing the Taiwan crisis, or let my naval chief handle it?

    Do I pour money into espionage or missile defense?

    Do I appoint a loyal but mediocre intelligence chief, or a brilliant but politically dangerous one?

    Do I spend political capital firing someone after a failure?

That is much more interesting than “2 actions per turn.”
What delegation really means here

Delegation should be treated as:

    a way to convert high-level intent into many lower-level actions

    at the cost of:

        imperfect control

        agency slack

        hidden motives

        possible incompetence or betrayal

So your spymaster example is excellent:

    you give broad intelligence priorities

    the spymaster agent interprets and executes them

    you get reports, not full transparency

    you can evaluate, reward, punish, replace

That’s very close to how real leadership works, and it also gives LLM agents a natural role.
Clean design principle

    The player controls priorities, resources, appointments, and exceptional interventions.
    Subordinate agents control execution under uncertainty.

That feels like the right backbone.
What the programmatic core would need

Not action slots, but:

    resource pools

    organizational chart

    agent roster

    delegation links

    trust/competence/loyalty estimates

    ongoing tasks / projects

    reporting flows

Then each agent can have:

    domain

    hidden prompt/profile

    current objectives

    resource allocation

    competence

    loyalty

    initiative level

Best next move

The next useful artifact is probably a role-and-delegation schema:

    player role

    subordinate agent roles

    what players directly control

    what agents control

    what resources are allocated

    how reports flow back up

That seems like the real missing piece now.

One other thing I want to flag is, in the previous instantiation of this game, what I had was basically, like, if you played the U.S. versus Iran, there's like no way you're gonna win as Iran. But basically, at the end of the game, there is an LLM that looks, that acts as kind of the strategic expert, and he looks at how both players played the game given their, you know, their starting world state and constraints and capabilities and all of that thing, and then assigns them a score based on how they have managed their strategy in the context of their terminal values. But yeah, I don't think, the other thing is I don't think we really need a role and delegation schema necessarily. Well, maybe we do, but a lot of this stuff is just large language models. So yeah, but tell me what you were thinking about the role and delegation schema.

That scoring layer makes a lot of sense, and it solves a real problem: asymmetric starting positions.

Instead of “win/lose,” the game becomes:

    how well did each side convert its resources and constraints into value realization

    how coherent was its strategy

    how well did it adapt

    how much unnecessary risk or waste did it incur

So Iran does not need to “beat” the U.S. in absolute terms. It can score well by:

    preserving core values under pressure

    imposing costs efficiently

    avoiding catastrophic mistakes

    exploiting asymmetries intelligently

That seems like the right design.
On the role/delegation schema

I agree you probably do not need a heavy formal schema for it.

What I had in mind was just a lightweight control structure, not a big ontology. Something like:

    player can allocate resources and set directives

    player can optionally create or appoint specialist agents

    specialist agents have:

        domain

        broad prompt/personality/competence

        resource budget

        reporting channel

That’s enough.

Because you’re right: a lot of this can just be LLM-driven. You do not need a giant formal role schema if what you really want is:

    “my intelligence chief handles this domain unless I intervene”

    “my naval commander handles deployments under my strategic guidance”

    “I can inspect, override, reward, or fire them”

So I’d now think of delegation as:

    a gameplay option

    not a foundational ontology object

Scoring layer

I think the strategic-expert LLM at the end should evaluate players against at least four things:
1. Value realization

How well did the player advance its terminal values, given its side?
2. Strategic coherence

Did its actions fit a consistent model and strategy, or were they contradictory and wasteful?
3. Constraint management

Did it respect or intelligently bend its real constraints?
4. Resource efficiency / adaptation

Did it use scarce resources, attention, and opportunities well?

Optionally:
5. Opponent-relative performance

Not “did you win,” but:

    did you outperform what would normally be expected from your position?

That last one is especially important for weak actors.
My updated take

The core system probably needs:

    environment/world dynamics

    hidden/public observations

    stochastic adjudication

    resource pools

    optional delegated agents

    end-of-game strategic scoring

And delegation can stay pretty lightweight unless you later find it needs more structure.

The next best artifact is probably a scoring rubric schema for that end-of-game strategic expert.

Yeah, I think really all of this stuff we can deal with later, and really the real question is whether it's worth having a formal, like, structured equation model or meta model for this, or whether we just kind of descriptively describe the world and then allow the game master to make reasonable estimates on, like, the probability of different effects and their effect sizes. I think are the kind of two things. So it's like, if you do action X, there's kind of like three possibilities with this probability distribution. So it might be A, B, and C. And then if A happens, the effect of that might be, you know, one, two, or three with this distribution. So I'm trying to think of, like, a good example, but it's like, if you fire a missile, it might hit or it might miss. If it hits, there's a possibility that it sinks the ship. There's a possibility that just damages the ship. So I guess maybe that's just three possibilities, sink, damage, miss. But yeah.

I think that is the real fork, yes.

There are basically two adjudication paradigms:
1. Formal causal/metamodel approach

You define:

    variables

    edges

    update rules

    maybe stochastic weights

Then actions propagate through that structure.
Upside

    consistency

    auditability

    reusable logic

    easier debugging

    easier to compare scenarios

Downside

    front-loaded design burden

    ontological brittleness

    risk of constraining creativity

    hard to capture edge cases elegantly

2. Descriptive world + game-master estimation

You describe:

    the world

    the actors

    the hidden/public facts

    broad scenario logic

Then the game master LLM estimates:

    plausible outcomes

    probabilities

    effect sizes

    observability

Upside

    flexible

    creative

    easy to prototype

    handles novel actions naturally

Downside

    less consistency

    harder to debug

    harder to replay or compare

    more model drift across turns

My view

For the kind of game you want, I would start with the second.

Not because the first is bad, but because the first is much more likely to prematurely lock in assumptions you do not yet trust. Your whole conversation has been uncovering how hard it is to define the “right” formal structure. That is a sign to avoid overformalizing too early.

So I would begin with:

    a descriptive scenario/world prompt

    a canonical hidden/public state record

    a game-master adjudication prompt

    structured outputs like:

        possible outcomes

        probabilities

        effect-size ranges

        observability consequences

Then let the programmatic layer handle:

    RNG

    state persistence

    logs

The best practical compromise

You do not need a full SEM at first. Use a lightweight structured adjudication schema.

For each action, the game master outputs something like:

    possible_outcomes: [A, B, C]

    probabilities: [0.3, 0.5, 0.2]

    state_effects_if_A: ...

    state_effects_if_B: ...

    state_effects_if_C: ...

    observability: what each side learns

That gives you most of the benefits of formalization without forcing you to prebuild the whole causal graph.

So your missile example becomes:

    miss: 0.5

    damage: 0.35

    sink: 0.15

Then for each branch:

    update ship status

    maybe update regional posture

    maybe generate partial observations

That is enough to make the system work.
My recommendation
Phase 1

Use:

    descriptive world prompt

    structured probabilistic adjudication

    programmatic resolution/state store

Phase 2

As repeated patterns emerge, formalize only the parts that are clearly recurring:

    visibility/contact logic

    movement/range logic

    economic sanctions logic

    inventory depletion

    maybe some espionage mechanics

Phase 3

Only then consider a stronger metamodel/SEM for the parts that benefit from it.
Short version

I would not start with a full structured equation metamodel.

I would start with:

    LLM-generated outcome distributions + programmatic state updates

and only formalize the recurring pieces later.

That is probably the highest-leverage path.
